THE RED PAGE
IN A GARDEN.
What more tender
Than the cosmos’ shadow on Helen’s face?
What more slender
More heavenly than Helen’s English grace?
Cosmos, Helen:
What sadder thought that in so small a
space
Death shall dwell in
So fair a flower, so beautiful a face?
Q.
HAMILTON WOOD.
IMPRESSION DE NUIT.
We watched Night loose her dusky hair
About the wearied limbs of Day.
The green hills glimmered into gray ;
Long dotted lights marked street and square.
One fallen yellow rose, the moon
On the dark water, faintly stirred,
Lay prone. A steamer passed ; we heard,
Thin and far-off, a foolish song,
And saw her black and ghostly spars ;
Like some old galleon, out of mind
Long since. . .What whispered in the wind
Beneath the still, unsleeping stars?
N.s.w.
FRITZ S. BURNELL.
Opal- Hearted Verses
Less than half a century ago Australia
was commonly written of as a grey, gaunt,
haggard, funereal, melancholy or otherwise
forbidding land. Australian writers of the
present generation are describing her differ-
ently. The desert still remains “a misty
mid-region,” though not a “dead heart” as
alleged. It is neither “dead” nor a “heart” ;
but rather a feature like the human appen-
dix, whose purpose has not yet been discov-
ered. The settled territory now appears
more correctly as a warm brown land, whose
droughty spasms cannot dim the radiant
green and gold of her borders. Instead of
being monotonous and sombre, so rich in
color is Australia that she justifies the phrase
of “an opal-hearted country” given in Doro-
thea Mackellar’s verses printed nere a fort-
night ago.
DOROTHEA MACKELLAR.
Miss Mackellar’s book has since been pub-
lished under the title of The Closed Door and
other Verses (Australian Authors’ Agency,
Melb., Is.). The title is not a happy one, for
the contents are decidedly of the open door
and the open air. The author has used her
own eyes, and has seen much to admire and
love in her country. Her pages glow with
the eolor of life, because she had steeped
her soul in it, as explained in these
stanzas:—
The lovely things that I have watched un-
thinking,
Unknowing, day by day,
lhat their soft dyes had steeped my soul in
color
That will not pass away—
Great saffron sunset clouds and larkspur
mountains,
And fenceless miles of plain,
golden-green in that unearthly
Hear shining after rain;
And nights of blue and pearl; and long
smooth beaches
tru i w . as sunburnt wheat,
Edged with a line of foam that creams and
■ _ hisses,
Enticing weary feet.
* # * *
There is no night so black but you shine
through it,
nn , ls no morn so drear,
u Golor of the World, hut I can find you
Most tender, pure and clear.
From the muddy greys of Lake Eyre to the
hectic green of a Sydney park at night, all
the colors of Australia seem to have been
captured in these verses. They abound in
I‘ttle pictures such as this from “The Dream
feoul”:—
In forests I have waited, still, expectant and
unseen,
While sunbeams pierced the darkness with
their swords of living green.
And this, after the fight with the galleon
“Santa Maria”:—
When she sunk there in our sight
With a little lapping sound
Slight as if a skiff had drowned,
Staggering we turned to go
For our ship had felt the fight—
Out to sunset showed our wake
Writhing like a wounded snake
Till we came to Samballo.
Flying foxes are seen “hanging from the
kurrajongs like monstrous magic grapes” in
“The Settlers”—a fine vision of Pan and his
crew in the Bush. She has the tempera-
ment which finds beauty in common things
and romance in a bit of sea visible from her
window.
For that strip of sapphire sea,
Set against the sky —>
Far horizons mean to me —
And the ships go by,
Framed between the empty sky
And the yellow sands,
While my freed thoughts follow them
Out to other lands.
And Miss Mackellar has the faculty of im-
parting her emotions to the reader. She
does not seem to owe much to other writers,
though in “Magic” there is a suggestion of
Kipling. The quality of this book, its fresh-
ness and sincerity, put the author in the
front rank of those who are now writing
verse in Australia.
Conan Doyle’s Verses
Conan Doyle’s Songs of the Road (Angus
and Robertson, 55.) is a book of journeyman
work of the same character and quality as
his Songs of Action. The well-known reci-
tation, “The Groom’s Story,” is provided
with a sequel, and an old wheeze turns up
again in the rain-soaked canvas hailed as a
masterpiece of Post-impressionism. The
story of Bendigo, the prize-fighter who got
religion, has some good points :
Bendy lie turned Methodist—he said he felt
a call.
He stumped the country preachin’, and you
. bet he filled the hall,
If you seed him in the pulpit a-bleatin’like
a lamb,
You’d never know bold Bendigo, the pride
of Nottingham.
But the Devil he was waitin’, and in the
final bout,
He hit him hard below his guard and knock-
ed poor Bendy out.
It happened - through some rival pugs who
jeered at Bendy in chapel, insinuating that
he had left the ring because the ring was
leaving him
Bendy fairly sweated as he stood above and
prayed :
“Look down, O Lord, and grip me with a
strangle hold!” he said.
“Fix me with a strangle hold ! Put a stop on
me! . .
I’m slippin’, Lord, I’m slippin’, and I’m
dingin’ hard to Thee.”
Bendy “slipped” and went for those pugs in
a way that did more for the mission than
any preaching could have done.
In “Religio-Medici” Dqvle expresses the
belief that all is good if understood: —
He lets the youthful dreamer store
Great projects in his brain, " -
Until he drops the fungus spore
That smears them out' again.
He stores the milk that feeds the babe,
He dulls the tortured nerve ;
He gives a hundred joys of sense
Where few or none, might serve.
And still He trains the branch of good
Where the high blossoms be,
And wieldeth still the shears of ill
To prune and prune His tree.
John Oliver Hobbes
The Life of John Oliver Hohies, told by
her father and her own letters (Dymock’s),
confirms the impression made by her books.
She was a bundle of nerves, strung tightly
by domestic unhappiness, and responding
sharply to the emotions. It was fitting that
she should adopt a man’s name for her liter-
ary work, for it shows a virile grasp of life
and an absence of sentimentality.
“I chose the name of John because it is
my father’s and my son’s, Oliver because of
the warring Cromwell, and Hobbes because
it is homely.”
Pearl Mary-Teresa Richards was born near
Boston, Mass., in 1867, and went to England
in the following year. When 19 she married
a Mi;. Craigie; four years later she had di-
vorced him, and entered the Catholic Church.
She gave as a reason for her conversion the
reading of Rabelais and Newman.
Some matters of literary interest appear
in her letters.... On a lecture tour in Amer-
ica she coined the word “blimming,” to repre-
sent the pleasant talk which says nothing.
She wrote to George Moore, praising
highly his Esther Waters, and to Gosse, in
admiration of his adroit memoir of Coventry
Patmore.
“Patmore (to me) belonged to the type
known as ‘Trials.’ I feel for the three
wives... .Rabelais does not shock me ; Field-
ing does not shock me; but the ‘mystics’ and
plays like Quex and Suzanne and ‘Angels in
Houses’ are not for me. To be frank, Pat-
more makes me i 11.”.. .The March Hare per-
suading himselLfchat he is a butterfly is an
unbeautiful study.”
Gosse was one of the few critics who did not
enrage her. She wrote to Moberley Bell
complaining of The Times notice of her play,
“The Flute of Pan,” although it credited her
with humor and unmistakable nobility.
Bell replied:—
There is one thing and only one thing
that the Omnipotent has absolutely failed to
create. He has made good women, bad wo-
men, clever women, silly women, selfish wo-
men, and heroines of self-sacrifice, but He
has never yet made a woman who could
stand —I will not say adverse criticism but —
one note of deviation from absolute and un-
qualified praise of any of her literary efforts.
... .If we had said that “The Flute of Pan”
was equal in parts to Othello, you would still
have resented the “in parts.”
“The Flute of Pan” is a satire on women
who are doing men’s work. She was “dead
against women’s suffrage,” and in several of
her letters she attacked the position which
Olive Schreiner has taken in her new book —
Woman and Labor.
Perhaps John Oliver Hobbes will be re-
membered longest for her novels — The School
for Saints and Robert Orange. Orange, who
appears in both books, is an idealised por-
trait of Disraeli. .
♦
“R. 8.” : Extract from “Lettres Anglaises”
in Her cure de France, 16/3/T1: —
“Whence comes the success of some Eng-
lish novels which are reputedly ‘wicked,’ and
what kind of public assures this success?
If French readers were offered such novels
they would not consider them in the least;
not that they would find them insufficiently
spiced and touchy—this is an aspect which
does not frighten us any more in France—
but they would probably judge them to be a
compound of puerile perversity and un-
healthy erotism, a character which the
bolder onslaughts of realism never had. It
is puzzling, for example, to find how one
can see the least merit and take the most
transitory interest in such books as His
Hour , by Elinor Glyn. The novels of Vic-
toria Cross are perhaps not so absurd; one
recognises at least an effort to be sincere,
which is too often suffocated under a heap
of rather badly evolved philosophical Con-
siderations. In Self and the Other, the
latest work of this author, the mostly insig-
nificant incidents and the—in spite of all —
banal situation are wrapped up in a senti-
mental and high-flown rhetoric. These two
novels make curious reading, and leave
doubts about the mentality of those who
admire them.
• * * * *
Edison suggests the possibility of
printing on leaves of nickel which
will outlast the bricks of Babylon.
How many of the annual million or
so new books deserve preservation? Many
of them ax‘e made for the day, and are thrown
away with the orange peel and the empty
jam-tins ; yet a multitude remain to cumber
the earth. If a Caliph Omar were to arise
in each century, and burn off the accumula-
tions of the bookshops (leaving a library
here and there), there might be a series of
new Shakespeares, instead of one nearly
buried under 99 thousand volumes of com-
ment. Anyhow, “Snooks” of Outback makes
a proposal worth considering:—
Wanted, a Minister for Books. All un-
dated, or non-descriptive titled books to be
heavily taxed. All “out-of-dates” to be
stamped “um-eliable.” Every superseded
.work to be withdrawn from sale and pulped
by the State Pulper, and all mediocre and
played-out writers to be “waratahed.” All
books (excepting instructive works) to be
issued with paper covers only, so that they
will eventually wear out, or demand reprint-
ing. _ Government catalogues to be issued
periodically, such catalogues to give sum-
mary of contents. Bookbinders can work
for private bookowners who want favorites
embalmed in. fancy covers. This will boom
the artistic craftsman and kill the other sort.
The success of Frank Harris’s The Man
Shakespeare seems to have given him a
second wind. In addition to his continuation
of the Shakespeare argument in connection
with the women of the dramas, he has writ-
ten a play— Shakespeare and his Love (F.
Palmer) —and has carried on a fierce news-
paper controversy with Bernard Shaw, whom
lie charged with stealing his thunder for an
amusing skit —± ne Dark Lady of the Son-
nets (printed in The English Review for
January). The Shavian dialogue has no-
thing to do with the Shakespeare wrangle;
but, like most of Shaw’s “dramas,” was writ-
ten with a practical purpose, which is the
establishment of a National Theatre in Lon-
don. Shaw’s lette.r in The Nation effectu-
ally punctimed Harris’s temporary inflation.
Harris has also been writing down the
talks he has had with such men as Cai-lyle,
Froude, Dilke and Russell Wallace. They
are good reading, and will no doubt get into
book form.
* ' # * *
H. G. Wells, interviewed by The Bodleian
about The New Machiavelli, denied that his
chai-acters were portraits -of any politicians
or other notabilities; each is a blend of fea-
tures drawn from a number of persons. Nor
is the author to be identified with the Rem-
ington of his story, who went over to Con-
servatism.
I am still as much of a Socialist—as I
understand the term —as ever I was. I still
hold the views which I expressed two or
three "years ago in New Worlds for Old.
lhey aren’t, perhaps, orthodox Socialist
views. I was trying in that book to impose
certain broader interpretations upon the
queer pedantries of the Fabian Society, but,
barring a few verbal and statistical modifica-
tions, I stand by that book now. Just as I
would still endorse my ten-year-old Antici-
pations.”
The March number of T.P.’s Monthly has
an article on the Fabian Society, interesting
on account of the portraits of members.
* * * *
“Grad” : To have sat at the feet of Profes-
sor MacCallum is to have become an en-
thusiast. Searching critic and laborious
student as he is, his judgment of literary
values is well-nigh impeccable. He at-
tacks his subject with the steady patience
and certainty of a mouse at an enormous
cheese, and lie penetrates to the heart of it.
But his students soon become aware that he
is not merely delivering addresses on Shakes-
peare or the Scottish Chaucerians, but is
letting them into some of the secrets of a
heart fired by noble ideals, and inspiring
them to love the highest when they see it.
Of all the words that I have heard him utter
from the platform, which do I recall most
readily? Well, I forget altogether the sub-
ject of the lecture, but he broke off to give
an apt illustration, left his notes, and re-
minded us of something which had just been
reported in the daily press—the death in a
sewer of a man who , perished in saving his
mate. “When I read that, gentlemen,” said
the Professor, with emotion. “I envied the
man his opportunity.” The applause was
spontaneous, lie meant it, and we loved
him.' That was 20 years ago.
* # - # *
Age is not mellowing Thomas Hardy, nor
is the use of verse abating the grimness of
his irony. The April Fortnightly Review
prints his “Satires of Circumstance” in 12
scenes, of which this is one:—
IN TIIE CEMETERY.
“You see those mothers squabbling there?”
Remarks the man of the cemetery.
“One says in tears, * 'Tis mine lies here!'
Another, ‘ Nay mine, you Pharisee!’
But all thqir children were laid therein,
At different hours like sprats in a tin.
And then the main drain had to cross,
And we moved the lot some nights ago
And packed them away in the general foss
With hundreds more. But their, folks don’t
kno.w.
And as well cry over a new-laid drain
As anything else, to ease your pain!”
After 20 years F. C. S. Schiller has re-
vised Riddles of the Sphinx. He finds now
what his readers long felt, that, after all,
philosophy is. an agreeable spiritual pur-
suit, and not a manner of life, as he once
thought, “in the simple-minded old Greek
way.”
“Practically ... a system Of metaphysics,
with whatever pretensions to pure thought
and absolute rationality it may start, is
always in the end one man’s personal view
of the universe, and the metaphysical crav-
ing, often so strong in the. young, is nothing
but the desire to tell the universe what one
thinks of it. . . . but the idea that it is to
hold true literally for all and for all time,
.is ludicrous.” -
In spite of his many changes, the clear,
buoyant quality of the original work is pre-
served, together with its essential doctrines.
In Prof. Schiller’s later wanderings through
controversial by-ways, his writing lost
some of its clarity and persuasiveness; but
in traversing again the broad ways of
audacious theory (his first passage < over
which he still secretly admires), he has re-
captured his earlier manner. The ordinary
reader,, whose interest in philosophy he was
the first to consider,"is the better for it.
The revision brings the book in line with
his present position, as far as could be
managed without an entire re-casting; in
places he merely tones down the language,
but he can add a note on atoms as ball-
rooms for electrons, and we still read of the
orderly and considerate habits of Spencer’s
“Unknowable.”
Here and there one misses a phrase or
an illustration, that was a memory aid to
an argument or a line of reasoning, but
this “Philosophical Pilgrim’s Progress,”
made in his adventurous supple youth (be-
fore “the hypertrophy of the critical ten-
dency had produced a degeneration of the
constructive powers,” he words it some-
where), is still the ihost delightful, and with
the possible exception of Shodworth Hodg-
son’s “Time and Space,” the best introduc-
tion to the lofty sport of metaphysics.
(Sonnenschen, 1910; 10s. net). \

The smart young business man who wants
to get a hustle on, or the slow old business
man who finds himself in a backwash, and
wants to get out, will receive the needful
stimulus from The Poicer of Advertising
(Geo. Robertson and Co.), by A. O. Richard-
son, who has gained most of his experience
in Australia. The book is better suited to
local traders’ requirements than most of the
imported works on advertising.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.
My Lady.
For the maker pursueth after Art as though she
were his ladye fayre.
A chill, plain circlet of eternal gold
Is ever on her clear, white forehead set.
Behind it ripples all her lustrous hair,
And on her snowy garment’s shining fold
Gleams one red jewel. Her white bosom bare
Is dewy warm with her sweet womanhood.
Her eyes are wide as one who long hath stood
Full of large dreams her soul may not forget.
A thousand lovers hath she, but to none
Was given save to touch her finger tips
For some brief moment in a maze of years
Spent with long toiling; or, perchance, to one
To hear her voice a-murniur in his ears,
To feel her white robes flutteringly pass,
To kiss her sandal-prints in thymey grass,
And perish of desire for her warm lips.
Yet surely know that she is to be won ;
Somewhere between the gates of birth and death
Is given to one to kiss her on the mouth.
Lo, once in the wide setting of the sun,
When vast cloud shadows filled the wintry south,
And all the west was one carnelian flame
Set round with agate wavings—ah, she came
So near, so near, I felt her fragrant breath.
And once in a lone cottage of the plain—•
A humble place, rough-hewn with logs and bark,
And far from mansions of the world’s .desire—
I saw her through the shadowy window-pane,
One of the circle by the red-tong'ued fire,
Nursing a bushman’s babe upon her knee.
But unseen hands withheld the door from me,
I burned without in all that bitter dark.
And once within the crowded city street
I caught the flash of her red jewel-star,
And strove to follow. But the heedless throng,
Crowding and pressing round my eager feet,
Wrought me despair. And, 10, a strange, sweet
song
Fell like cool droppings from a hidden spring,
Silver on silver note. I heard her sing,
But knew not if the voice were near or far.
And once, ah, once, I walked a land of dream
Amid tall heroes that large nectar quaffed
From shining flagons, broidered with the past
In hammered silver. By a hoar, grey stream,
Shadowed with world-old cedars, Time had cast
His fatal hour-glass sideways as he slept.
And, 10, beside me through the gloom there step!
My lady, and she looked at me and laughed.
But somewhere in the shining evening west,
Or ’mid the green leaf curtains of the south,
Panting and breathless I shall claim her mine,
See the red jewel-star upon her vest,
And feel her lingering arms about me twine—
Ay, though a death be in the cup I prize,
Yet will I drink my draught from those deep eyes,
Press her white breast, and kiss her on the mouth.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Luck!
The ribbon on Aurora’s camisole
Gleams through an open-work of snowy lace
And frilling of white cloud, and morning’s bowl
Reflects the dimples of her winsome face.
She parts the velvet curtains of brown night,
Puts out the whiteness of the last pale star,
And stoops to draw from out the wells of light
Good luck, to splash across day’s eastern bar.
This is my lucky day, I know and feel
The promise that, like music, round me drips,
Sweet-eyed Fortuna leaves her spinning-wheel
To kiss me like a lover on the lips.
Yes, it is good to live, the world is gay,
I breathe the spirit fragrance of the rose;
Through golden mists he comes —he comes to pay
The shining twelve-and-sixpence that he owes.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.
The Surf-Call.
A gloriops morn for a bathe!
The two-mile swath of a gleaming scythe
Lies white for the wind to swathe.
The tide is a true spring tide;
The cool, wet sand is as crisp and blithe
And fresh as a week-old bride.
And warm is the sun, and sane
As flesh allied unto souls that rest
And tread not the paths of pain.
’Mid sparkle of gold and blue,
A white gull follows a curdling crest
And screams in its glee to you.
White surf sings a boisterous song
And chuckling, eddying water creeps
The lips of the bay along.
Oh, who could a niggard be,
When warm blood, drunk with the surf-call,
leaps
To greet and embrace the sea?
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
Faith.
Sweetheart, why trouble to disturb
The bones of controversies dead,
Or present joyous laughter curb
With fearful doubts of woes ahead?
For though Time’s shadows on us steal
Present delights are all we feel!
You look upon the road I came
To feed the jealous doubts of love,
And burn your heart with futile flame.
Ah! little can past follies prove:
My past untruth can little show,
When you, yourself, I did not know.
I am an infidel of things
That lure your faith—do not believe
That Fate our lives together brings,
Or we a destined lot achieve.
But if our lives together run,
What matters how ’twas all begun?
I doubt those sentiments that you
In all your romance ne’er forget,
That I was fashioned you to woo,
As truthless superstitions; yet
If you love me as I love you,
There’s truth enough to make them true.
N.S.W.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
I’er Annum . . . . ..£l6O
Half-yearly .. .. .. 0 13 6
Quarterly . . .. .. 0 7 0
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communica-
tions to be addressed to William Macleod, Manag-
ing Director.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper {and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. icill be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,

THURSDAY, MAY 11, 1911.
WHY LABOR MUST GO FORWARD.
Though the Referenda result will check
the Labor movement, it cannot stop it. Year
by year, despite press hostility, reverses, lack
of cash, internal differences, and a thousand
other factors,- it forges ahead. One power-
ful reason is that it is hated by the right
people. There is a type of selfish Austra-
lian reactionary who, under one alias or an-
other, is always to be found ranged up
against the Democratic cause, and the hos-
tility of this class is a political asset. The
very rich Australian is amongst the worst of
the unworthy rich people in the world. A
Tory daily gave this fact away the other
morning in a manner that was none the
less damning because it was unconscious.
It said :
In Australia, when a township feels in need of
some local improvement, its leading citizens rise
up in a deputation and ask the Government for
money....ln England apparently it is more usual
to decide that all the men of wealth in the country
should be asked for contributions.
The “readiness of rich Australians to neglect
all but their legal obligations” may be dis-
covered by a trip through any section of
the Commonwealth. Coopers, Wilsons,
D’Arcys and scores of others draw their tens
of thousands yearly from this country, but
they rarely give a farthing back to it of
their own free will. England and America
are dotted with Leland Stafford Universi-
ties, Tate Galleries, Wallace Collections—
splendid recognitions by the affluent of their
duty to the State. Not one Australian mil-
lionaire out of 50 has donated so much as a
village pump to the land where he made his
pile. Even in New York, the Pierpont
Morgans have started admitting the citizens
to their homes on fixed dates, to view the art
treasures there; and in Britain this public-
spirited practice has existed for generations.
The Australian plutocrat who happens to
have a home in this country would see the
public shot before he allowed them to scrut-
inise the 30-guinea couches and the 30-shil-
ling oleographs which Lord Rosebery dis-
covered there.
* * * *
No class can consistently flout its obliga-
tions without paying the penalty. The
penalty which Australia’s rich class pays is
the dislike and distrust of a huge section
which, under different circumstances, would
be its warm upholders. A cable message
mentioned a while back that the British
Lords were willing to submit their fate to a
Referendum of the people. Melbourne Age,
writing on the subject, said amazedly:—
It is a very striking and significant fact that the
whole Peerage of Great Britain is willing to put
its political destinies into the keeping of the popu-
lar vote of England.
If the British landlord resembled the Austra-
lian landlord the affair would be astonishing.
In the circumstances an appeal to the masses
is the Lords’ best hope of salvation. The
arguments of cold reason may be all against
the Peers. They may be anachronisms;
their House may be a block to needed legis-
lation ; numbers of them may be wasters or
worse; but, as a body, they are liked and
respected, and they know it. At last gen-
eral election in the United Kingdom the
South and West of England, and the agri-
cultural parts of the Midlands, went solidly
for the Peers’ cause. The Liberal majority
was gained in Wales and Scotland —where
special issues were raised —and in the big
towns where alone the “landlord cry” cut
any ice. There is, on the other hand, not a
Legislative Council in Australia which would
not be summarily wiped out if the electors
were given the chance to do it at the ballot
box; and the voting would be as much on
personal grounds as on the grounds that
these institutions are useless and out of date.
The typical “upper class” Briton only leaves
his native land when he is too ill or too
poor to live there: his Australian prototype
only settles in Australia when he is too ill
or too poor to exist anywhere else. Aus-
tralian “Society” is the only body of its kind
in the world in which abuse of local products
and local conditions is accepted as a sort of
hall-mark of gentility. It is not strange
that the bulk of self-respecting natives
should grow impatient with their unpatriotic
rich, and incline to the view that there
must be merit in any cause to which these
folks are opposed.
* * * *
Labor scores, and must go on scoring, by
reason of the fact that, in the nature of
things, its tendency is to act while the other
party talks. The alias “Liberalism,” adopt-
ed by Anto-Laborites in Australia, deceives
no one. This country’s “Liberal” party is
plain Tory. The vast sums of money which
were squandered during the recent campaign
on full-page newspaper advertisements and
millions of billheads, on the salaries of an
army of “organisers,” and in other less worthy
directions, was not supplied by citizens an-
xious for legislative change of any sort whatever.
The “Liberals” who so lavishly financed the
Anti-Referenda campaign have reason to be
satisfied with things as they are. Their
ideal leader is one who is able to talk round
the subject of reform in such a fashion as
to suggest that reform is pushing at the front
door, and yet keep the menace permanently
anchored on the far side of the garden gate.
A highly-gifted politician in this line was
N.S.W.’s Joseph Carruthers. With stu-
pendous nerve he christened his party—the
party which was where it was with the ex-
press object of preventing anything being
done, or (to borrow a “Liberal” euphuism)
to “act as a check on experimental legisla-
tion” —the “Reform Party.” During upwards
of three years he neither reformed nor at-
tempted to reform anything. His successor
was less adroit, or more honest, or some-
thing. For a while Wade showed a dis-
tinct tendency to do something in the way of
reform. As a result, he alienated the re-
gard of his warmest supporters. His land
legislation—providentially, from his point
of view, flung out by the Legislative Coun-
cil—drove “Liberals” like J. Macarthur
Onslow, of Camden Park, to open mutiny.
Warned thereby, Wade became as others of
the Anti-Socialist, Reform, Liberal persua-
sion. Since then he has enjoyed the ut-
most popularity in Anti-Labor circles—and
has in consequence been enthusiastically put
out of office by the electors.
* * * *
Even the narrowest and most provincial
of Labor administrations can feel secure,
more or less, because there is work that must
be done, and no other party to do it. What
thinking citizen, for example, could expect a
Wade administration to do more than orate
about a North Shore bridge or tunnel?
Sydney Ferry shares are not held by the
helpless widows and orphans whom Wade
humorlessly claimed were going to be ruined
if the McGowen Government’s ferry scheme
materialised, but by the very bulwarks of
“Liberalism.” To displease these people—
and there is no surer way of doing it than
by touching their vested interests—is to
undermine the entire “Liberal” edifice by
removing a large portion of the cash-founda-
tion on which it rests. It is the same with
a thousand other reforms. Democrats are
pained at the Yankee asininities of King
O’Malley; Australians grieve over the
States Frights sentiments of W. A. Hol-
man ; and when Verran wowses they ex-
perience the tired feeling like other men.
But such calamities are not confined to the
Labor party. Take it for all in all, that
party stands for Australianism, as opposed
to that brand of flag-wagging Jingoism
which can see merit in every country but
its own; and it stands for deeds as opposed
to oratory, which is full of sound and promise
signifying nothing. So wherever a practi-
cal Australian patriot is found to-day his
tendency is to vote Labor, as being, with all
its faults and flaws, the one visible hope on
the horizon.
SOME ADVICE THAT IS WHOLLY
IMPRACTICABLE.
The N. S. Wales branch of the Peace So-
ciety has again arisen from its placid dreams
about the brotherhood of man, to urge upon
Australia the extremely simple method of
avoiding war by declining to have anything
to do with it. The society in its manifesto,
issued over the signatures of the gentle Miss
Rose Scott, president, and one Wm.
Cooper, vice-president, extends the glad in-
formation that there is no occasion to fear
the hostility or envy of Asia, nor is there
the least necessity for Australia “to throw
itself into the vortex of barbaric militarism
while the world is moving forward” —pre-
sumably to universal arbitration, or the mil-
lennium, or some other unreliable and in-
definite period. The building of the Com-
monwealth navy, the adoption of compulsory
military training, are declared to be merely
the result of fear of a bogey created by Aus-
tralians themselves, or, if there is anything
more substantial than bogey, its substance
is none the less of our own creation. Here-
abouts, the manifesto becomes somewhat
mixed, and sets out to discredit its earlier
view of the position by pointing out that,
“while Australian arrogance in seizing a con-
tinent lying within reach of Asia, and
warning off the inferior races might, at the
time, have seemed neither difficult nor dan-
gerous, owing to the weakness of the Asiatic
races, the lesson Japan taught Russia made
a great difference.” It did. So also with
the lesson taught to the murdered Korea;
and it is a peculiar inconsistency even in a
Peace Society to quote those two examples
of the incontestably war-like and treacherous
nature of the Japanese in the forefront of an
appeal to this country to trust blindly to
that people’s chivalrous and peaceable spirit.
* * * *
But the manifesto goes further. What, it
asks, is the good of fighting anyhow, since
Australia hasn’t a hope of winning; and it
proceeds to suggest that, in the event of in-
vasion, White Australia should give up the
ghost without a kick, leaving as its last
will and testament to history the sum total
of its philosophy—“ What’s the use?” Ob-
serve :
We have a vast continent, not half peopled, and
on its northern border, the nearest to Asia, practi-
cally uninhabited. Who has proclaimed that fact
more widely than ourselves, and, by coupling the
name of Japan therewith, pointedly drawn the at-
tention of that country? At the same time dubbing
her people “undesirable aliens,” and an “inferior
race,” we have ostentatiously warned them off;
action we could hot substantiate for a single day if
Japan chose to contest it, except with the help of
our Mother Country, which help she could give only
by turning her back upon cherished traditions and
ideals, and at the probable cost of her Asiatic Em-
pire. For be it remembered we are a people of but
5,000,000, while the Japanese number nine times as
many, with armies and navies which we cannot
hope to approach for many long years, and, as re-
gards that portion of our country where we profess
to see greatest danger, as near to her base as we,
with as good, if not better means of communication.
What hope could we have of beating Japan in such
a struggle?
In other words, what is the profit of hu-
manity’s applause to the bleeding patriot,
when, with discretion, he might be an un-
scratched serf? \Vhy should his cities,
which he has taken so much time and trou-
ble to build, be sacked and destroyed, when
the early hoisting of the white flag would
preserve them for a neighbor who has no de-
sire to do more than put Ins name above the
door? Why should he jeopardise his women
to an infuriated soldiery, when an amicable
arrangement or a dishonorable understand-
ing could be arrived at? we cannot help
ourselves Rose Scott and William
Cooper say so!
* * * *
Miss Scott and her Peace Society also
assure us that the Japs are not a people who
love war for its own sake, nor have they at
any l time gone out of their way to attack
their neighbors ; indeed, they are looking for-
ward to a renewal of their alliance with
Britain, and to the proposed arbitration
treaty between that country and the United
States of America. Just here Miss Scott
may be offered a chair, while somebody else
gets up and says a few words. Less than
a month ago Count Ernest Reventlow,
perhaps the most noted strategist and mili-
tary writer in Germany, offered his opinion
about the pacific Jap. In a carefully-pre-
pared essay he declared that even amongst
the most sceptical it was not denied that
Nippon was preparing for war with the
United States for the supremacy of the
Pacific. Says the expert:—
The seizure of the American islands in the Pacific
would be essentially a defensive act on Japan’s part,
notwithstanding its aggressive character. It is a
question of life with .Japan, and there is no doubt
whatever that her rulers are, fully aware of this
truth.
Reventlow gives it as his firm conviction —
he says there is no doubt —that Japan could
at the present time seize the Philippines,
Hawaii, United States possessions in Sa-
moa, and the island of Guam. Then “the
most powerful American fleet conceivable
would be helpless in such an immense area
of operation as the Pacific Ocean, without
advance bases, so that capture of these bases
by Japan would render that country undis-
puted mistress of the Pacific.” Either the
expert Reventlow is wrong, or the inex-
pert Sydney Peace Society has misjudged
the mind of its squat friends across the
pond.
* * * *
Anyhow, the Peace Society can be as-
sured, on the authority of the Japs them-
selves, that its advice to Australians to
trust their country and liberties to the
friendly feelings of an alien race has no sym-
pathy from them. No later than last year
the Kokumin Shimbun made it clear that
the Japanese had learned “that the most
indubitable rights are worth nothing unless
they are supported by a suitable force.” And
the Kokumin Shimbun knows quite a heap
more about the matter than the innocent-
souled Miss Rose Scott or even W. Cooper,
vice-president.
THE HONOR OF TOIL.
Despite the proud boast that Britain still
leads the world in making onion pickles and
iron bedsteads, it is an incontestable fact
that the British person has had from time
immemorial a rooted objection to doing
things. To the insular mind work has al-
ways been degrading, and idleness has been
the hall-mark of nobility. The internal evi-
dence of the language itself reveals this. A
very awful character who prowls about with
a blood-stained knife is known in bellow-
drama as a “villain” ; but “villain” at first
simply implied a town-dweller, a person who
supported himself by labor at some craft,
instead of riding abroad to cut throats and
collect plunder. Similarly, a low person
is a “churl,” and we throw all the contempt
we can into the term; yet “churl” in earlier
days was merely a laborer in the fields, a
useful toiler who made several blades of
wheat grow where formerly there had only
been a battlefield, or a rubbish tip for dump-
ing dismantled barons and other refuse.
Again, it is most objectionable to be called
a “cad” ; yet the original “cads” were merely
the followers of an enthusiastic Labor leader
named Jack Cade, who had a wild idea that
the time was ripe for setting up wages
boards while Plantagenet kings still held
their crowns by grace of a meat-axe, and
their personal dexterity in keeping their
seats on a bucking and slippery throne. The
uses of these words, and of a dozen others
which might be dragged into the argument,
go to show that the persons who shaped and
compiled that appalling conglomeration of
mental traps and etymological confusion
known as the English language had a su-
preme contempt for any man who demeaned
himself by doing any ordinary and useful
work. Nor must it be supposed that the
energy of the race found outlet in war and
blood-letting. The historical record reveals
the fact that there were only 4000 English-
men at Poitiers, 10,000 at Agincourt, and
26,000 at Waterloo. The remaining mil-
lions of the people simply stayed at home,
and, dodging work with what skill they
might, secured to themselves the largest pos-
sible measure of comfort for the smallest
amount of effort. In short, the English na-
tion for a thousand years proved its sterling
ability for leaning on a post, avoiding war.
and despising labor.
* * * *
The natural outcome of the national pen-
chant for doing as little as possible, was the
degradation of those who, weakly yielding to
the demands of hunger, actually consented
to do something. They sank to a condition
which would be mildly described as abject,
were kicked feelingly by their idle com-
patriots, starved to the last point of endur-
ance, and taught by their .spiritual over-
lords that it was their birthright to be trod-
den upon by those whom they fed with their
toil. About the middle of the eighteenth
century a Scotch craftsman in verse, one
James Thomson, who poured forth a
dreary mass of jingle to the lasting injury
of the “Seasons,” taught the nation to sing
that “Britons never, never, never shall be
slaves.” The ditty admirably suited the
mood of the people, and they have gone on
singing it with increased enthusiasm ever
since. Everyone who could do so dodged
anything that bore the least resemblance to
slavery, and in doing so developed marvel-
lous skill in coercing or inveigling others in-
to bearing the loads which he himself had a
distaste for carrying. People who forgot
how to work, in the joy of compelling others
to do it, were said to have “risen from the
ranks.” By the beginning, of the nineteenth
century the output of articles made under
British supervision had become enormous.
English parents had by that time learned
to sell their children to the factories; and
England held more negro slaves than any
other nation. The slaves worked under
the whip; so did the children. The only
difference was that the slaves got more to
eat. All the while the people continued to
sing “Rule Britannia !” Then came im-
provement in the factory conditions, and the
abolition of negro slavery, and it appeared
as if the dwellers in the fog-girt isle would
at last be compelled to thin out and do some
serious toil. The genius of the nation,, how-
ever, saved it from such a fate. The intro-
duction of the sacred principle of Freetrade,
and the further extension of a vast black-
and-tan Empire, preserved the ancient repute
of the land. This is the inner meaning of
that splendid Imperialism which spreads it-
self in magnificence of grovel on such occa-
sions as the coronation of a new king. It
indicates that the old tradition of not doing
things is being upheld in heroic fashion,
and that the nation’s bosom swells with
pride at the knowledge that a goodly part of
it is still dodging real work.
* * * *
The ancient British tradition has spread
to Australia. The thing shows itself at
every turn. The desire for black and yellow
serfs to do our bidding is perpetually assert-
ing itself. The refusal of white men to be
serfs makes it a miracle in the eyes
of many that the Commonwealth can rub
along at all. The wish of the majority
to do as little as possible is perpetually in
evidence. You may discover signs of it by-
observing those who lean against posts, or
by studying the figures of the late Referenda
polls. Only by constant vigilance cau the
principle of a White Australia be main-
tained : it is a principle which runs directly
counter to the ancient tradition. White
Australia implies the elevation of labor to
honor, the full recognition of the worker’s
service, and the determination to overcome
the hereditary tendency to fatigue. It im-
plies that the “villain” is more worthy than
the baron, the “churl” more noble than the
loafer, and the “cad” a person playing a
very important part in the new order of
things. The watchwords of a people who
side-stepped personal effort are of no pos-
sible use to a nation which disdains to prop
itself with black or yellow auxiliaries. Such
a nation must feel the honor of doing things,
and step forth confidently and aggressively
on the new national path it has chosen.
Ever since the original Adam started dig-
ging Asia with a clumsy wooden spade, it
has been recognised that someone has got
to work; but the aim and object of most
of Adam’s children has been to put the bur-
den on the shoulders of someone else, and sit
down and superintend. Britain has no 1 ’
been exceptional in its line of thought and
conduct. It has merely been one of the
best at the game, as it has hitherto been
played. Greek civilisation was built on
slavery; Roman, on slavery plus the plun-
der of many nations ; American, at first on
slavery, and afterwards on the exploitation
of cheap immigrant labor from overseas.
It remains for Australia to show that a true
civilisation can be built upon a foundation
of national industry.
* * * *
Whisker to whisker, and foot to foot, the
forces of the new and old ideas are now do-
ing battle on Australian soil. It is not a
fight that is going to end in one round or
in twenty. The new notion is handicapped
by a thousand considerations of sentiment;
and even those whose grip of the situation
is clearest have the ancient microbe in their
blood. Labor statesmen can still on occa-


sion grovel with the best, and a bout of fi a S:
flapping can still temporarily deprive good
Australians of their reason. But the bigger
view slowly forces itself upon the peop^ly.
Nations of superior loafers have had their
day ; the nation of superior workers is com-
ing along.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
New Protection or No Protection —Which?
The highly-protected manufacturer who
isn’t prepared to pass on any of the fruits
of the Protection to his work-people may
yet find that his position isn’t impregnable,
even though the Commonwealth has been hit
with a “No.” Canada has an Act which
says that six citizens may go to any Court
and charge that any Trust is squeezing too
much out of the consumer. If they put up
a good case the Department of Labor is
ordered to investigate, and the complainants,
the Trust and the Government each appoint
a member of an investigation committee.
The committee can call for papers and per-
sons, administer oaths, and lock up for per-
jury. If it finds the Trust guilty of en-
hancing prices it can SUSPEND ANY
TARIFF DUTIES WHICH PROTECT
THE TRUST’S PRODUCT. Further, the
committee can issue an order suspending or
abrogating a patent right, if it finds that a
Trust is using the thing to bleed the public;
and Canada is relying on this provision to
knock out the Machinery Trust. Without
the power which it vainly sought the other
day, the Australian Commonwealth certainly
cannot compel a Protected manufacturer to
share the fruits of the Protection with the
work-people. But, as the Canadian exper-
ience shows, it can still say—“ Very well.
We didn’t introduce Protection merely to
put money into a few manufacturers’ poc-
kets. If you won’t do the fair thing we
asked —if you block the thing for which we
really designed Protection—then the Pro-
tection must go. It’s either New Protection
or no Protection at all —now which is it to
be?” And when the Commonwealth Govern-
ment makes that quite plain, the vigor with
which various “No-Noers” will urge Holman
and the others to “voluntarily concede some-
thing to the Commonwealth” will be aston-
ishing.
*
Once More the Decayed Fish Merchant.
An A. J. Simpson, of N.S.W., is alleged
by New York Sun—one of the most reliable
dailies in the U.S. —to have spoken as fol-
lows to an interviewer at the Waldorf Hotel
last month :
I own 150,000 head of sheep, am worth about
£350,000, and employ 80 men.
A Labor Government has just come into power by
an overwhelming vote, with Mr. Fisher as Premier.
All progress has stopped, and, for myself, lam
looking about for another country to live in.
Both the general and the State elections took
place in January, and the, Labor party carried all
the States except Tasmania and Queensland.
McGowen, the new Premier of New South Wales, is
in favor of applying the progressive land-tax policy
to the smaller farmers as well as the big ones.
Outside of Australia the effect has been bad because
of the impression produced by our new legislation.
We cannot get any more money from the capitalists
of England.
Our Government in N.S.W. is worse than that of
M.L. Some months ago we had a big coal strike at
Newcastle, and gas and electric light works were
tied up, and the trams stopped running.
The allegations of Simpson, who lives at the
Waldorf when he is in New York, who is. on
his way to the Coronation, and who claims
to be a disgusted person in the act of aban- ,
doning the country where he has amassed
over a quarter of a million sterling, are in-
correct to the following extent: —
(1) Progress has not stopped in Australia, as a
result of the Labor party’s rise to power. Ten years
ago, with Labor nowhere in politics, the production
of Australia was worth £112,273,000; to-day, with
Labor on top, the figure is £174,509,000. Ten
years ago the bank deposits amounted to 90 millions
sterling; to-day they are 174 millions.
(2) Labor won in N.S.W. and S. Aus. alone at
the State elections. McGowen has never breathed a
word about applying the progressive land-tax to
small farmers.
(3) Australia’s foreign credit has not been dam-
aged by Labor legislation. On the contrary, it has
distinctly improved. Labor’s Defence policy evoked
a chorus of cheers from oversea investors.
Its immigration restriction legislation and
much of its social legislation have been copied
by Britain. The lamentable inaccuracy of the
charge that Australia “cannot get any more money
from the capitalists of England” is evidenced by
the fact that every State Premier has successfully
raided the foreign pawnshop in the last six months.
(4) The Newcastle coal strike occurred under an
anti-Labor regime, and was largely due to anti-
Labor’s maladroitness.- The strike didn’t stop the
trams or tie up the electric light, and the only gas
co. it affected was a little North Sydney concern,
which, having neglected to provide a reserve stock
of coal, had to switch off its supply for a few hours.
Now, what is Australia going to do about
its Simpsons? This latest slanderer is re-
ported in one of America’s most widely-read
papers—and he does not question the ac-
curacy of the report—to have denounced his
native land as a ruined, discredited spot,
where there is no progress, and nothing is
heard save the clattering boots of departing
Capital. The yarn happens to be a crude,
ill-fashioned lie, easily disnrovable by any
official reference book. But that makes
Simpson’s conduct none the less noisome ;
and th.ere is the consideration that the aver-
age newspaper reader doesn’t turn up Year
Books to verify statements made about a
foreign country by a native of that country.
One Australian of the Simpson breed does
the Commonwealth more harm —by discourag-
ing the immigration which it so vitally needs
—than a hundred of his fellow-countrymen
who live mostly in gaols. Yet there is no legal
means of getting at the Simpsons. They.carry
on their travels abroad a sort of free license
to libel the land which has provided them
with the means to travel. Obviously, it is
up to Australia to cancel that license by
holding the possibility of a heavy fine or a
snell in gaol over those who make a hobby
of traitorism. And until that fine or that
imprisonment is prescribed, it is up to every
true Australian to treat the national slan-
derer, no matter who he may be, as an out-
cast, unworthy of respect—an ungrateful
hound that bites the hand that feeds it.
Another Result of “Making the Railways
Pay.”
Last week Willie Watt, Acting-Premier
of Victoria, published the shameful informa-
tion that the Cabinet had decided to order
40 locomotives from “abroad,” because the
Newport Railway Shops, though working
night and day, are unable to meet the de-
mand for rolling stock. That is one ot the
many disastrous results of importing a tormgn
expert to “make the railways pay. Ex-
perts of the Tait brand never intend to make
this country their home. They come un-
der engagement for a few years, and look
forward to saving a few thousands and then
clearing. Any fool could make the railways
or any other big works “pay under those
circumstances. All he has to do is to starve
the system, postpone repairs, neglect re-
newals, and ignore all claims tor additional
plant, and then, given even a normal re-
venue, he can show a wonderful surplus.
When he sees that the time has come for
the whole business to break down, he comes
in out of the wet, and departs with his thou-
sands and a great reputation as a successful
manager. No sooner is he gone in triumph
than the trouble begins. All this or some-
thing very like it happened and is happening
in connection with Tait. He retrenched
wherever possible, he maintained no re-
serve supplies of material, rolling-stock or
men, he paid little heed to the convenience
and not very much to the safety of the public,
and he starved the equipment of the State
workshops to such an extent that they have
been working for months at breaking strain.
He “made the railways pay”—and now the
public has to balance the account. Those
40 locos will cost something like £130,000 —
which means that over £IOO,OOO will be spent
abroad in wages, instead of being spent in
the Commonwealth. Doubtless the Com-
missioners could not foresee the record har-
vests of this and last year, but the recur-
rence of “good” periods is as much a mat-
ter of certainty as that of droughts, and due
provision should have been made for expan-
sion. Not only has that not been done,
but the normal supply has not been main-
tained. The State coal mine at Wonthaggi
—now going on for two years old —has taken
much rolling-stock, but this has practically
been made up by starving the firewood, timber
and other trades. Even had the Commis-
sioners, in true farsightedness, increased
their capacities for building engines, trucks
and carriages beyond immediate require-
ments, they would still have been on firm
ground, for the Westralian Government is
simultaneously importing 20 locos, at a cost
of £66,800, and these could then have been
built at Newport. So £200,000 is stupidly
and thriftlessly sent abroad! That means,
roughly, the loss to Australia of 1000 arti-
sans and their families, for at least a year,
and probably for always, and the loss of
much valuable experience and training. It
is a heavy price for “making the railways
pay” in a country that is crying out for
population.
Exit “ Haw, Damme! ”
The first number of the new Commonwealth
Military Journal —which is to be issued
monthly, without charge, to all officers —
reports some straight talk given by Colonel
Legge to the United Services in Melbourne.
The Defence Act says that all officers and
non-coms, shall be promoted from the ranks,
and this is so horribly contrary to Euro-
pean practice as to have caused misgivings.
Colonel Legge, in his lecture, cited three
authorities as sufficient: —
(1) The military correspondent of London Times:
“Japan could have organised her corps of officers,
had she desired to do so, as a caste apart, ala
Prusse, at once the strength, the glory, and the
weakness of the German army. There were many
who advised her to do so, but she wisely refrained,
and left open the door to merit wherever found.”
(2) General De Negrier (French): “One super-
iority of the Japanese consisted in the fact that their
army was not linked to the past by those Old World
conventionalities which we dignify by the name of
traditions.”
(3) Legge himself: “The weakest point about
old-established armies is, in my mind, the traditional
social distinction of the officer. In all walks of life
it is brains that count, and why not in an army?
I have seen Australians, from all States, under the
trying circumstances of war, and it is an undoubted
fact that, even with those having little previous
training, good results were in direct proportion to
the ability of the officers in charge, irrespective of
their birth, their breeding, or their money value.”
The new Military College, to be opened in
the Federal Territory next month, has been
recruited entirely by examination. . Not
even riding, shooting, or other physical at-
tainment has been allowed to count, except
that, of course- an ordinary medical inspec-
tion had to be passed. Whether physical
merit should not count towards the making
of an officer, as much as towards that of a
Rhodes scholar, is, no doubt, an arguable
question. But physical merit is a matter
of opinion, and it was feared, apparently,
that if it were acknowledged, backstairs in-
fluence might get a look in that way. Un-
der the present conditions it gets no look in
at all. Given some unworthy administra-
tion in the future, the present excellent in-
tentions may be perverted; but, if that can
be avoided, the “officah” who traditionally
said “Haw! Damme!” is going to be as ex-
tinct in Australia as the curate who said
“Oh fancy!” lived on bath-buns, and left
his go-losh about in public places.
Wanted, Official Defenders.
At the conference of Trades and Labor
Councils, held at Christchurch (M.L.) last
week, a waterside workers’ remit: “That
the conference endorse Dr. Findlay’s pro-
posal to appoint public defenders in each
centre of population throughout the Do-
minion, and urge him to bring down legis-
lation in that direction as speedily as pos-
sible,” was agreed to. When are Australia’s
State Governments going to get busy in the
same direction? The average layman has
but a faint conception of the gigantic-organi-
sation an accused person has to face. The
even-handed fairness of his criminal admin-
istration is a tradition with the Briton. At
the revolutionary who suggests that British
justice is hardly ever even-handed, and is
frequently unfair, the Briton is apt to fling
the deadly reproaches “Socialist” and “Pro-
Boer.” Yet the Revolutionary is right, and
incidents are continually cropping up to
prove it. For example, last month, a man
named Stinie Morrison was tried in Lon-
don for murder. The English papers dis-
cussed the case in all its bearings, as is their
Yankee custom these days. Quite casually
London Evening Times remarked: —
Two men were engaged in the actual murder of
Leon Beron. Both were present when Beron was
killed. One man, says the prosecution, was carried
by a fast taxi-cab and deposited near Kennington
Church, and was there met by the second murderer.
How did the second murderer get there ?He did
not fly — he could not have taken a taxi-cab, because
the police have now complete knowledge of the move-
ments of every taxi-cab in that district on that par-
ticular night.
It might have been that Morrison’s only
chance of establishing his innocence lay in
getting that “complete knowledge of the
movements of every taxi-cab” which the
police had, as a matter of course. Without
considerable wealth he could not, however,
hope to do so. It is one instance of the
countless mighty “pulls” which the Crown
has over an indigent prisoner. But the
Crown is a sportsman in its way. It insists
on giving the penniless accused a run for his
life or liberty—even if it is only the sort of
run a pigeon gets from a gun club. To that
end the Crown puts up an organisation, free
gratis, whereby the prisoner may fight the
Crown. That is to say, it gives him
a copy of the depositions, and assigns
him the most callow of junior barristers. A
typical case may be cited. |srown, of
N.S.W., was indicted for manslaughter. A
barrisfer of nine months’ standing was as-
signed to defend him. On reaching the back-
blocks town in which the sessions were to
be held, counsel called on his client in gaol.
Brown proved to be a citizen of indifferent
brain-power: he had no witnesses, couldn’t
put his hand on any at short notice, and was
due to be tried the next morning. The
prosecuting barrister was Jones, K.C., as
knowledgable and experienced a hand as a
criminal might wish to bump. At the end
of an impressive legal ceremony, Brown
was solemnly “shot sitting,” to the tune of
seven years’ penal servitude. It is not pos-
sible to put in the hands of a hard-up ac-
cused person, for the purpose of establishing
his innocence, the tremendous outside organ-
isation that the Crown has at its command
to establish his guilt. But at least he should
be given a square deal when he comes to
court. To give him that square deal his
defender must be on a par, in experience,
legal knowledge, capacity, and so forth, with
the average Crown Prosecutor. In fact,
Crown Defenders should be well-paid offi-
cials, claimable by any accused person. The
assigned-counsel arrangement is so patently
absurd that the wonder is it has lasted as
long as it has.
The Necessity for a Campaign of Insult.
Dattilo Rubbo, of Sydney, on being asked
to express a candid opinion of the pictures
at Bathurst (N.S.W.) Agricultural and Pas-
toral Show, said things which caused anguish
to the bucolics who had exhibited their
drawings and paintings. And he did well.
Australia’s Millets and Corots must ar-
rive; but the type of painter who is brought
into public notice by the agency of a horse
and cattle fair, would in most cases be bet-
ter employed hoeing mangels. The tragedy
is that hardly anyone says so. Every year
in every big country centre the citizens are
encouraged to spend weeks producing bad
drawings and worse paintings. The metro-
politan artist who judges the outrages gen-
erally murmurs that “the district shows a
distinct advance in the field of Art,” bestows
prizes on what strike him as the least dread-
ful, and departs in an atmosphere of friend-
liness. As for the press, adulation follows
the advertisement. Nothing is rarer than
the just word on the subject. In the coun-
try the absurdity of the indigenous art ex-
hibits is so obvious that they constitute a
standing jest among those who are not re-
sponsible for them, Not so in the cities.
In this regard Sydney, for example, is a by-
word among art-cultivated folk; yet it is
only dimly beginning to suspect the fact.
Last week a cable mentioned that the critics
had pronounced G. W. Lambert’s latest
Academy picture to be “the finest nude study
in the exhibition.” Lambert is an Austra-
lian, and last year he gave a one-man show
in Melbourne. The trustees of the Sydney
Gallery viewed the exhibition, and bought
nothing. Yet they wasted a large sum dur-
ing last year on pictures which —re-
garded as contributions to a national col-
lection —were as unworthy as anything at
the Bathurst Show; and hardly any news-
paper or public man raised a word of pro-
test against either that scandal, or a hun-
dred similar ones. The chief trouble with
this country’s artistic health is the amazing
consideration that is shown to ineptitude.
No one in authority tells the trustees of the
Sydney Art Gallery that they are a politi-
cally-appointed band of sheep-breeders, auc-
tioneers and tradesmen, possessing no techni-
cal or other acquaintance with Art, and un-
fitted, therefore, to buy pictures for the na-
tion. No one raises a word of protest
against the horrible statues that deface the
public spaces. No one informs the folks
who contribute monstrosities to the picture
galleries of agricultural shows, or the offi-
cials who use public funds to encourage
them, that the former deed represents wasted
effort, and the other wasted cash. Lately
the N.S.W. Government announced that it
intended to provide proper facilities for the
encouragement of Art. It is to be sincerely
hoped that the public will back up the Go-
vernment; and it can’t do that worthy thing
better than by telling the bald truth, always
and everywhere, concerning bad art.
Postmaster-General Thomas, the man
who, according to the embittered daily pa-
pers, was introducing chaos to his depart-
ment, straightened out still one more postal
tangle last week, when the penny post set-
led down all through the Commonwealth.
For over 10 years of Commonwealth rule
every State kept its own postal practices:
in Victoria you could send a letter any-
where within the State for a penny; in N. S.
Wales you could only send 20 miles (as a
rule) for the copper; in S. Australia you
couldn’t send a private letter across the
street for less than twopence, though you
could send various specified kinds of litera-
ture for one penny. Already Thomas had
arranged that the stamp of one State should
be good in every other State; and, to com-
plete the work, the man who" “produced
chaos” will present the Commonwealth
with a set of all-Australian stamps very
shortly. As a matter of fact, most of the
commotion Thomas caused in the depart-
ment was due to the disturbance of anoma-
lies ; and it is wonderful how loud anomalies
will squeal when you prod them. Further,
the worse the anomaly, the bigger the undue
advantage that somebody is getting, as a
rule; so the worse the anomaly the worse
the squeal. But there were anomalies
that were just senseless, and didn’t
seem to be benefiting anybody. Years
ago, for instance, coaches used to
strike various Tasmanian townships
on the Sabbath; so the post offices were
opened on that day. In some such cases
there were only two or three mails a week.
The coaches ceased running years ago; or,
with the spread of railways, mails came in
every week-day; yet these post and telegraph
offices continued to remain open on Sun-
days, though they did practically no busi-
ness at all. Every other circumstance
changed, but the Postal Department re-
mained in the old coach rut. When Thomas
first heard of such things he was a Didy-
mus. When he found that the wasteful
absurdities really existed, and proceeded to
end them, he was a Revolutionary. Fortu-
nately, however, even the mildest-mannered
Laborite in Australia gets so used to being
called a Revolutionary that a little thing
like that no longer makes him pause for so
much as a moment.

In every second issue for six or eight weeks
past Melbourne Argus has gushed in catar-
acts of type over a marriage epidemic.
When the last Federal campaign was rag-
ing, the Argus and many of the candidates
it loaded with its favor declared that one
of the most deplorable effects of a Labor
Government would be a cessation of wed-
dings. To this extent Australia under the
Caucus would be like Heaven —there would
be neither marrying nor giving in marriage.
As a result of Capital receiving a shock to
its nervous system, and industry suddenly
withering up, the most lion-hehrted young
man would shrink from the responsibilities
of matrimony. Labor has been in office for
quite a time, and the Marriage March has
developed into a gallop, the bridegroom com-
eth in regiments, and churches and parson-
ages are working overtime. But so far the
Argus has not confessed its error, and ad-
mitted that its judgment was not worth a
stale egg. In fact, while it was whirling
its ecstatic bonnet in the air over the mar-
riage crop, it was putting into circulation
the same old horrid prophecies, and having
the usual visions of fright about the 1200
calamities that would convert Australia into
a sore on the face of nature if the people
voted “Yes” to the Referenda proposals.
From a “Liberal” canvasser: —
As one of the great Liberal party which defeated
the iniquitous Referenda proposals, I wish to say
that, through ignorance or apathy, we only had a
majority of 250,000 votes, a mere 40 per cent, vot-
ing on the questions. Had the other 60 per cent,
gone to the polls, our majority would have been
2,500,000. The apathy of bank managers, squatters,
mining and legal managers is well known, and the
ignorance of shareholders in Gas, Tramway and
other companies is appalling. Hardly a ship or
coal mine owner has ever been known to vote. The
Liberal press is too apathetic to point out to the
electors how they should vote. Misrepresentation
and abuse have been poured upon the great Liberal
party by the powerful and unscrupulous daily Labor
organs. Tens of thousands of pounds of Labor
money has been lavished in defence of the
party’s nefarious policy. Railway and other hoard-
ings have been defaced with these people’s placards
inviting and instructing electors to vote Yes. The
streets have been illuminated at night with electric
signs, inviting them to save the Commonwealth and
destroy the States. Their leaders tea’d and ban-
quetted the wives of struggling millionaires and
magnates, to bribe them into voting against their
convictions. Bricklayers, carpenters, sewerage la-
borers, even bottle accumulators, placed their motor-
cars at the disposal of their leaders. Trading on
the ignorance of our esteemed Chinese fellow-citizens,
Mr. Hughes captured the entire vote of the banana
importers by telling them that if they voted “No”
the marriage tie would be broken and their wives
and children handed over to Messrs. Deakin and
Cook. In spite of their machinations we have
scored a glorious victory. As Mr. Deakin says, we
owe a great deal to the solid and intelligent women’s
vote. The ladies responded nobly, and voted almost
to a woman in the cause of the downtrodden monopo-
list and sweater, and of the Svrian white-work fac-
tory employers, and other true friends of humanity.
Let us organise, and when next the bloated Demo-
crats attempt to rob us of our rights, they will see
that, instead of polling only 40 per cent., the
Commonwealth will poll 140 per cent, at least.
llow David Fell: —
When his leader, Wade, V.C., was making frantic
efforts to have the proposed Referenda tried, hanged,
drawn and quartered in the N.S.W. Assembly early
in December last, David Fell arose as the champion
of Australia and rebuked the Kembla hero. He told
the House: “It is not the first time I have de-
clared myself on the question of the Federation as
against the State. If young Australians have any
national sentiment they recognise the greater poten-
tialities of the Federation against those of the
State. I find to my political embarrassment that,
whenever a Federal question comes up here, I am
entirely out of touch with the members of my party.
The Liberal party has, unfortunately, to my mind,
always put up the State against the Federation,
and the party will always go down. If our party
were in power we should have no objection to the
course proposed. Speaking now for .the Federation, I
say that it should have the power Vbhich it is sought
to give it.” The words are the words of David
Fell, a free agent at the time. And what a
damning indictment of Party Government those words
are! After announcing that this is not the 1 first
time he has declared for Federation against the
State Frighters, and that Australians .must uphold
the former against the latter if they have any na-
tional sentiment, he twitted his own political pals
with always looking on the small side of things.
Then he told them that they were opposing the very
necessary amendments for party reasons, and de-
clared that the Federation should be granted the
powers asked. So far, good. But now consider
what happened afterwards. For over four months
the voice of David Fell was hushed; then, just at the
death, in obedience apparently to the pull of some
unseen party wire, he dawdled into print, lamely
declaring in favor of State Frights, Anti-National-
ism, the Party View, and a waterlogged Federal
Constitution ! After all the talk about Caucus me-
thods, the sflent subterranean “Liberal” method of
bringing party rebels to heel appears far more effi-
cacious.

monwealth’s telephones:—
The telephone system, as far as the mechanical
part of it is concerned, was never at a higher state
of efficiency than at present. The use of the metallic
currents, the conduits, and the installation of so
many common battery-boards of the most modern
type, have brought the State telephone system up-
to-date. It is the human factor that is not so satis-
factory. In past years the Department has been
starved for want of sufficient telephonists, and the
operators have had too many lines to attend to. Re-
cently the demand for telephones has increased so
rapidly that it has been found impossible to train
sufficient telephonists to cope with the new installa-
tions.
So the Labor Government isn’t blamable,
after all. Mechanically the telephone sys-
tem is “up-to-date,” and if the staff isn’t so
satisfactory it is only because “recently”-
that is, while the Telegraph was talking
wildly about the commercial community
throwing up the instruments in disgust—
the commercial community has been rush-
ing for new instruments so enthusiastically
