If anybody has noticed that Strawberry
is giving an extra pint, and that the
speckled hen lays an egg a day regularly
now and twins on Sunday, there’s no need
to get excited about it —it is just the result
of the Referenda vote. In six days the fol-
lowing things (among a thousand others)
were observed by the Melbourne papers:—-
(1) A man who had £IOO,OOO (evidently loose
cash in his pocket) waited until the vote was taken.
Now he has got his coat off and is bogging in at an
“industry” (kind and place not specified, as usual).
(2) Great masses of capital that were on the
wharves, upended, and ready to be hastily tipped
into steamers, have been dragged joyously back in
lorries to various banks and offices.
(3) Several farmers who were going to let weeds
grow on their land have harnessed up Dobbin and
Queen again, and are rapturously tearing the earth
up anew.
(4) Dairymen who had been “down-hearted over
the prospect” have corralled up Daisy and Cherry
once more,. and have kept milking them ever since.
(5) The vote means that the people want the old
Deakook crowd (whom they threw out so violently
a year ago) back again.
(6) It is a direct demand for Fisher to resign.
(7) No one is ever going to vote Labor again.
(8) Everybody is wondering why he ever did so.
(9) At the coming Vic. State elections Labor is
wiped out in advance.
Which in a roundabout way reminds The
Bulletin of two things it has observed
about small boys of a certain type
(1) When they have driven a mouse into a hole
they announce that they have met and killed a lion ;
(2) When they are in the dark they keep telling
themselves “I am not frightened,” in the hope that
somebody will listen to their voice instead of looking
at their shaking legs.
“Fiddling Nero” : I don’t want to unduly
swell the outcry against the Referenda back-
sliders, but I give notice that at the next
meeting of the Australian Labor party the
following questions will be asked: —-
(1) Why did a party of New South Wales Labor
members employ the week before polling day in a
picnic to Kosciusko ?
(2) How much of the cost of the jaunt was paid
by the jaunters?
(3) What extraordinary event was it that led
members away from the base of one of the most
momentous struggles a constituency could be engaged
in?
(4) If, as stated, Holman led the party to in-
spect Government improvements to the tourist re-
sort, will he, as Acting-Premier, explain why highly-
paid inspectors have to be assisted in their work
by members of the Assembly?
There are two or three other things I’d like
to know, but I’ve no doubt somebody else has
given notice of them. I’ll see they are not
overlooked, anyhow.
George Blakemore, lately general manager
of Great Cobar, talks oil from California: —
Just as the rush to use electrical power marked
the last years of the 19th century, so the rush to
use oil power promises to mark the second decade of
the 20th century.
The oil-production figures for California for 1910
were 76,000,000 barrels of 35 English gallons, and
apparently in a year or two even this large output will
be much increased. There is an agitation to have
the Pacific Coast fleet fitted with oil burners; the
railways have changed their locomotives long ago to
burn oil; the city municipal authorities are being
educated to use oil everywhere for roads. The one
city of Los Angeles consumed 5,000,000 barrels of
crude oil in road-making last year. If they knew
how to make roads in California they probably
wouldn’t use so much. When newly made the oil
read is a dream of delight to the motorist. Imagine
travelling on a perfectly smooth, black, india-rubber
belt—that is an oil road, freshly made. The con-
struction method is to plough up the centre of the
earth track, remove four inches of earth, replace it
with about three inches of rock crushed to egg size,
roll this, cover it with one inch of fine spalls and
roll again, then drench it with oil costing (delivered)
about ss. 3d. for 35 imperial gallons. After a day
or so, the oiled surface is well rolled, then dusted
over with about one-third of an inch of gravel or
fine sand. The traffic soon produces the rubber-like
surface. The California oils nearly all have an
asphaltic base; the lighter oils rapidly distil out,
leaving behind the thick residuum, which, under the
action of air, sun and water, rapidly oxidises into a
hard surface. But, made so lightly, it soon goes to
pieces. If the tracks were made as they are made
in Australia, and oil were used as a top dressing,
they should be perfect for a very long time, and
nearly dustless.
At the wells the price of oil varies. The usual
crude oil is worth 40 to 50 cents per barrel of 35
imperial gallons (42 American gallons). But in the
case of wells producing a lighter oil, such as the Ven-
tura district affords, the price may be as high as
$1.50 per barrel.
The opening of the Panama Canal will probably
be the cause of a greatly-increased consumption. It
seems to be the intention of the United States Gov-
ernment to establish supply depots on the canal for
the use of ships passing through, and oil depots are
sure to be one of the conveniences.
Vast quantities of gas are being wasted daily on
most of the oil fields. One well, owned by the
Honolulu Oil Co., in the Buena Vista Hill, California,
is estimated to be vomiting 35,000,000 cubic feet of
gas per day. It took fire not long ago. The
tongue of flame shot 400 feet into the air. The heat
was so great that no one could approach nearer
than 200 yards. It burnt for 10 days before the
flame was extinguished by using steam from six
boilers. A gas well pure and simple produces a
“dry” gas—that is, on compression it yields a very
small percentage of gasolene. The gas emitted from
a well yielding oil produces two gallons of gasolene
from every 1000 feet of gas.
Gil as fuel in furnaces is probably the most con-
venient that can be used. Any heat that coal will
produce can be obtained by merely turning the oil
tap ; there are no ashes to wheel away, and no coal
to sling into the fireboxes. About 120 gallons of
crude oil are reckoned equal to one ton of coal of the
best steam quality, so that the same power is stored
in much less than half the weight. In battleships it
means that the radius of action could be nearly
trebled. It is very evident, then, that oil will be
a very important factor in the future. The country
that hasn’t got it had better start looking for it.
Whether Australasia will ever become an oil pro-
ducer it is difficult to say; but it will be very strange
if it should be the only section of the world in which
oil is not fodnd. The discovery probably depends
on finding the right geological formation, with experi-
mental boring, which of course is always expensive,
and even in established oil fields is sometimes a lot-
tery. The Australian State Geologists should have
the tremendous importance of the matter impressed
upon them.
“Left Hand”: The man who said in a recent
Bulletin that the first-part of Richards’s shows is
indispensable from a box-office point of view can
never have sat out that part of the performance. In
order to keep my job I have repeatedly been forced
to attend these shows, and X can certify that never,
even when there has been a crowded house, has a
first-part item been encored ; and nearly all of them
get so little applause that you could drown it in the
Cotter. The first-part is taken good-humoredly be-
cause of the stuff that comes in the second. If it
were all first-part, Rickards would be looking for a
job. ‘

“Our Miss Gibbs” has gone. During the
past few months she has utterly demoralised
every newspaper office in Sydney. Fight
months or so back, when Gerald Marr
Thompson began to write about her in the
S. M. Herald, he was a large, tall, gay man,
with a heavy crop of hair. Since then the
strain of writing something fresh twice a
week about the monotonous female has
shrunk him to a small, spare, desperate man,
who has torn off almost every vestige of his
thatch. Every other theatrical critic is
more or less a wreck ; and it is giving away
no office secret to say that it was the sight
of these halt and maimed pressmen that
moved the management to do something for
the hospitals. . On the principle of giving a
hair of the dog that did the biting, J.C.W.
hands over to the repairing shops half the
last night’s gross proceeds. “Gibbs” is to
to be followed on Saturday night at the
Royal by “The Dollar Princess,” with Flor-
ence Young, Roberts, Olive Godwin, Lottie
Sargent, Bert Gilbert and other old comic-
op. favorites in the cast.

Give a girl a bad name and she’ll fill a
theatre, as George Marlow knows. If he
had opened the Adelphi at Sydney with a
play starred as “The Good Girl of the House-
hold,” or “The Best Girl in the Sunday-
school,” he’d have done poor business, and
Virtue would have been out of a job long
since; but instead of that he handed up “The
Bad Girl of the Family,” with the assurance
that she was “the most sensational bedroom
drama ever staged.” Six weeks have passed
since then, and the bad girl is still sinning
nightly to crowded houses. When she is no
longer able to earn the rent, the management
may introduce “The Worst Tart Since Cleo-
patra,” and introduce it as the greatest cor-
set drama of the age.
** * *
The seven juggling Perezoffs, at Sydney
Tivoli, score their loudest laugh when a
member of the company, dressed as a waiter,
hits a brother food-carrier sharply on the
waistcoat with a hammer and smashes a
concealed plate into fragments. A helplessly
indignant man, with broken crockery stream-
ing from his vest and rattling on the stage, is
calculated to shake a bray out of the most
doleful ass. It is so utterly absurd and un-
expected that the pitiless critic who rather
prides himself on never laughing at buffoon-
ery needs to be warned about it; otherwise he
may be surprised into uttering a clown-like
guffaw, and be dragged down to the level of
those who are amused by such antics. The
lively turn is full of humor and excellent by-
play. Cadwall and Verne, a youthful and
subdued comedy pair, whose turn has an
American flavor, rattle through some non-
sense and clogdance. Reichen’s acrobatic
dogs are scientifically interesting; but the
small clown who emerges from his box be-
tween tricks, and turns somersaults, is per-
haps the best part of the turn. Peggy Pryde,
Deli no, of the slack wire, Tom Dawson and
Black and White are the other noticeable
headliners of an entertaining bill.

Sousa’s band will commence to play in
Sydney at 8 p.m. next Monday, at the Town
Hall, and at that hour precisely “Yip-i-addy”
will be given notice to pass over its popularity
to “El Capitan” or “The Cotton King,” or
some musical celebrity created by the famous
composer and conductor. There are 60 in-
strumentalists in the band, and Sousa
reckons to let Australians hear his own favor-
ite march, “The Stars and Stripes for Ever,”
as they neyer heard it before.

Professor Walter Thomas Mills, M.A., au-
thor of those two remarkable books, “The
Struggle for Existence” and the “Science of
Politics,” will begin his lecture season in
Sydney at the Protestant Hall, Castlereagh-
street, on Saturday night. Mr. Mills is a
speaker of vast experience, and the stuff of
which his lectures are composed is the best
of mental meat. The course will run through-
out the week, and will cover practically every
branch of advanced political thought.
** * *
Glad, in the Mrs. Burnett drama, “The
Dawn of a To-morrow,” is an exponent of
faith whom the churches and little Willie
Judkins would hesitate to embrace. The
authoress sets up this primitive she-Wowser
of the slums as an example of the unfailing-
success of implicit confidence in the excel-
lent intentions of the Deity, and then forgets
herself and her principles so far as to per-
mit this young hussy to bargain with the
bold bad man of fashion in a shockingly
rdrty manner. Glad actually undertakes
to be his to have and to hold —minus the
marriage ceremony—in the event of her
latest and most important prayer not being-
answered within a brief time-limit. Ob-
viously in her own mind Glad’s bid for a
miracle is a good deal in the nature of a
gamble in which she has a bit both ways.
Mrs. F. PI. Burnett’s religious principles
don’t hang together, and her play is over-
taken by the fate that smites all attempts
at dramatic Wowsing. It is neither a co-
herent and comely drama nor a complete
and convincing church service. Still, in-
terest circulates freely around and about
“The Dawn of a To-morrow,” touching it in
many places, and just now the Princess’s is
deserving well of those people who ask for
earnest aims in the theatre, even if the bull’s-
eye is not smitten. “Arms and the Man”
is announced to be nearly fit for publica-
tion, and when “To-morrow” passes, the
piquant Pshaw will follow on.

The only thing to announce about “The
Belle of Brittany” at the Melbourne Royal
this week is that the University’s young men
will patronise a performance next Wednes-
day evening. The students are saving up
their yells and whistles, and are also making
ready to decorate the theatre with the usual
pretty designs and piquant devices.
W. Anderson unleashed “The Squatter's
Daughter” for another run at Melbourne
King’s last Saturday night. As may be re-
called, this is the play in which Ben Hall
roams through gullies and gums, the
while belligerent ants bite a maiden lady.
The ants were in great form on Saturday
night, and forced the lady, Miss Lillie Bryer,
into some wonderful imitations of a factory
whistle with a pain. Other humorous inci-
dents in the piece are furnished by Bert
Bailey, whose watch has to gallop to catch
up to the age of some of his jests. Rut-
land Beckett is Dudley Harrington, a villain
in quest of new sins; and Max Clifton dis-
guises his appearance in borrowed whiskers
and a swaggie’s moles. Ben Hall is Ned Duggan
in private life, and one notes that Ben finds
Ned’s Little Mary somewhat inconvenient
for strenuous bushranging. The lovely hero,
Tom Bathurst, is played by Roy Redgrave!
On Saturday his attempts at love-making
were foiled by the rather unresponsive acting
of Miss Helen Burdett. The young lady, in
the chief character of Violet Enderby, had
her respiration jarred by her long opening
speech in the first act, and seemed to spend
the rest of the evening in trying to make
her breathing regain its rhythm. The only
other member of the cast who may be men-
tioned is Miss Marie De Alton, as yet a little
green, but with good promise of a satisfac-
tory maturity.
** * *
Barrie’s “Alice-sit-by-the-Fire” was tried
by the Melbourne University Dramatic Club
at the Turn Verein Hall one evening last
week. The play, which is new to Aus-
tralia, represents Barrie in one of his moods
of Simple Sam my ism. In tlie first act lie
introduces Cosmo and Amy, prime samples
of artlessness and romantic immaturity.
Then, while we are listening to their play
of conversation, he intrudes the parents of
the pair, Colonel and Alice Grey, followed
by Mr. Stephen Rollo, the intimate of the
family. Presently the author prods the lot
with a stick of whimsicality, and sets the
most of them periorming mock-erotic evolu-
tions. Amy, who is addicted to “blow-
outs” on popular melodrama, is early mis-
construing her mother’s friendliness for
the unfortunate Mr. Rollo. On the other
hand, mother suspects that Amy has a large,
clandestine leaning towards the same young
man. Both, however, are innocent of giddy
intent, and, after one act of complexity, anil
another of explanation, the whole structure
is blown by Barrie’s gas to a rather stodgy
conclusion. Strictb’ speaking, the drama
is only a device on which Mr. Barrie strops
his dramatic invention. Some of the tricks
—as, for instance, the use of the gloves and
cupboard in the second act —look a little
old and stagey from the ingenious conceiver
of “The Admirable Crichton.” Moreover,
the bulk of the characters have not the ar-
resting qualities of vital portraits. They
are more in the nature of silhouettes, ob-
served through a haze of sentimentality.
But the parts of Amy and her mother are
exceptionally well drawn, and were attrac-
tively taken by Miss Unie Russell and Mrs.
Cornish respectively. Of the others Miss
Lucy Verdon gave a short, droll sketch of
the servant, Richardson; and Sol Bloom
tried to make Stephen Rollo something more
than a mere spectator of his fictitious pecca-
dilloes.
** * #
R. A. Roberts, accompanied by wigs,
clothes, and a charming copy of an inn in-
terior, dropped into the Opera House last
Saturday. The newcomer is the latest ex-
ponent of the quick-change business; he not
only swaps his clothes with striking rap-
idity, but with each new change assumes
fresh mannerisms, gaits and tricks of speech.
So he really seems to be not one being but
five. Altogether Roberts’s act is excellent,
and the arrangement of The Spaniards Inn
shows a fine talent for stage setting.
** * *
“What Shall We Do with Our Old?” is
the title of a striking picture shown at West’s
building, Melbourne. An old toiler is dis-
charged from his job on account of failing
energy and growing age. He and his missus
thereupon start to starve, and the poor old
woman perishes on a miserable bed in a
naked room. The picture stops there—-prob-
ably out of regard for the feelings of the Fat
gentlemen who don’t like to be reminded of
the fact that the _ home-wrecking, happiness-
destroying Socialists have already accom-
plished old-age pensions.
** * *
The Prahran Lyric (Vic.) continues to
show nothing so interesting as its dramas and
pictures oi travel. Last Saturday two good
color subjects were screened in a well-acted
version of “The Merchant of Venice” and a
study of some rapids in the Philippine
Islands. The latter showed exceedingly skil-
ful tinting: the scene, from the rushing water
to the boats and vegetation, was full of
wonderful effects.

Motor Street Cleaners.
The Renault Co. of Paris appear to be always
leading the world in automobile matters, and
their latest achievement is the supplying to the
city of Paris a number of metor-driven street
sweepers. The experiment is a distinct success,
and our city council would dp well to enquire into
such an invention.*

AT POVERTY POINT.
Ten thousand friends of George Rignold will give
a sympathetic thought to the old tragedian over the
death of his wife, which occurred last week. Before
her marriage she was Miss Georgina Coppin, a
daughter of another famous old George; and for
some time she was the only woman, as far as The
Bulletin knows, on the Board .of an Australian
public co.—the Melbourne Theatre Royal Proprietary.
She had previously been her father’s secretary.
“Lud”: When Irving first played “Charles I.” in
London, there was great democratic indignation over
Belmore’s Cromwell. Wills, the author, painted
Cromwell in the most atrocious aspect, and Belmore
filled the author’s instructions by making him as
ugly, mean and contemptible as possible, as a foil
to the divine Charles. One Colonel Richards had fore-
seen the situation, and was hawking round a play
about Cromwell, based on Victor Hugo’s, and pre-
senting the Protector in the most lofty and noble
manner. The Radicals seized on this, and produced
it, with George Rignold as Cromwell, and well he
embodied the part. Such thunders of applause as
small audiences could provide were there, and the
management at all events enjoyed the fun of putting
its hands in its pockets to vindicate old Noll. Of
course it soon ran out. Rignold was spurred on
through a curious happening. Bateman was at the
Lyceum, and after several failures, he came to the
conclusion that Irving was not the leading man the
house needed; so he tried to replace Irving with
George Rignold, from the Queen’s, where George had
done particularly well in Caliban and other parts.
Rignold asked the advice of the veteran Jack Ryder.
“Stop where you are,” says Ryder; “Bateman will
be in the Bankruptcy Court in a month.” Indeed,
things were shaping that way. But a new piece
arrived; it proved to be “The Bells,” and that was
the turning point of Irving’s fame. Irving was sum-
moned forth with shouts, and appeared bowing and
panting in his shirt-sleeves; and, instead of Bateman
going bankrupt, he at once put up Irving’s salary
from £l5 to £25 a week.
“W.F.”: William Hoskins’s nephew (B. 27/4/’l>)
apparently knows very little about his unme’s Lon-
don career. First, then, Macready, the greatest of
all the Richelieus, never acted at Sadlers Wells,
and William Hoskins, never led the business there. I
was at the house from 1843 to 1852, so 1 can speak
of what I know. Phelps and Greenwood had the
Wells from 1844 to 1861. Greenwood left in 1861
and Phelps a year later. Phelps, Henry Marston
and George Bennet were the leading tragedians most
of. that time, Phelps always. William Hoskins
joined the company in 1846 as light comedian; he
never played a leading tragic part there but once,
and that was on his benefit night, when Phelps let
him play Othello. Phelps played lago, Marston
Cassio. _ Morton’s “School of Reform” was the
other piece; Hoskins played Tyke. Nevertheless,
Hoskins was very popular at the Wells in general
comedy ; but such parts as Benedick, Mercutio and
Petruchio were always played by Henry Marston.
Later on he did good work, in Ballarat as manager
of the old Theatre Royal in Sturt-street, from its
opening on Boxing Night, 1858, and for some years
as lessee, producing in good style many of Shaks-
peare’s plays. Here he was supported by a com-
pany consisting of Henry Neil Warren, G. H.
Rogers, Hoskins himself, Stuart O’Brien, John Dunn,
Leslie and Rosa Dunn, Julia Harland, Mrs. Roy,
Miss Glyndon, Mrs. T. S. Bellair and others who
could hold their own with any combination in Aus-
tralia.
“L H P.”: Heartily endorse all that “Billy s
Nephew” (B. 27/4/’ll) says re his uncle, especially
as to “The Tempest” production in 1869 at the now
defunct Sydney Prince of Wales Opera House. That
was a glorious show, with scenery by Alfred Clint,
and “stage groupings” by Geo. Leopold, who nowa-
days would be called a “producer.” Hoskins, who
was responsible for the whole production, added the
sub-title “or the Enchanted Isle,” which looked very
alluring on the bills. He himself played Prospero,
and Florence Colville was Miranda, with the rest of
the cast thus: Ferdinand, 11. N. Douglas; Caliban,
Charles Hall; Alonzo, Holloway; Gonzala, J. J.
Welch; Stephano, Charles Young; Trinculo, Wil-
liam Andrews; Sebastian, J. H. Rainford; An-
tonio, Tom Leopold; Adrian, Hasker; Ariel and
Ceres, Kate Corcoran; Juno, Nellie Montague; and
Iris, Eleanor Carey. Hoskins made the opening
wreck, always an awkward thing to stage, a me-
chanical scene, which means that no dialogue was
used. Then he hurried on to the play proper at
Prospero’s cell, which he made do duty for Act 1.,
the wreck being billed as the “prologue.” Jack Renno
was mechanist, and Mrs. Stapleton supplied the
dresses for this rare Shakspearian revival —it has
only been done once since, in Australia, and that
was by George Rignold in Melbourne in the ’Bo’s.
Poverty Point in Adelaide has simply bristled
with Costs of late. First, A. E. Martin, formerly
editor of the now defunct Gadfly, sued the Casino
picture show for wrongful dismissal as manager, and
got damages. Then the Casino shut up suddenly,
whereupon numerous offensive documents dropped
into the letter-box. Mendel Finkelstein, of the
Wondergraph, won his test case against the Govern-
ment, Magistrate Gepp affirming that the Chief Sec.
could not shut up his Sunday show. The same
Finkefotein, in collaboration with M. Marcus, man-
ager of Rickards’s Tivoli, sued the Government for
disallowing them to use the Continental Gardens after
giving a lease, and they got £2OO. Finkelstein and
Marcus are now going for another £4OO. The Gov-
ernment’s burst of Wowerism is costing it more than
a Sabbath day’s collection. A lady in the “Gay
Gordons” sued Meynell and Gunn’s manager for
breach of contract, and lost the case, and the pro-
prietors of the Stadium sued the National (boxing)
Arena for £3O and won, though an appeal is pend-
ing. Also a Pierrot charged a theatrical agent
with attempted choking, or some such horrible prac-
tice, and a charge of blackmail was being prepared
when a friendly solicitor got the case settled on the
outskirts of the court.

“Petramos”: The theatrical treasurer who em-
ploys a genius of the prize ring is liable to lunacy
at any minute. He can get hardened to lawyer’s let-
ters, threats of arrest and the whole legal para-
phernalia, but a pugilist with the light of genius
flashing on his knuckles is apt to become nerve-
racking. A treasurer once had to deal with big
Peter Felix and another boxer who was merely white.
Peter and his confrere grew anxious about £l4
owing to, them and called on the treasurer, who was
counting the night’s takings. The white boxer ex-
plained the situation, and the treasurer informed
him that treasury day was “next Monday.” Peter
waved the white boxer to one side, and addressed
the treasurer. Said he: “Say, heeyali ! We want
our money. We want foahteen paounds or we
take the lot.” The treasurer considered. He
wasn’t in training. He looked at the big black’s
face and then at his fist, and decided it would be
undignified to “fight for the house.” He counted
14 sovereigns into the colored person’s palm and
went home without a fracture.
“Spotlight”: “Sinnicuss” refers (B. 4/5/’ll) to
the Williamson management’s “Caledonian charity”
in doubling the prices for the “Gibbs” last night,
and halving the proceeds with the Sydney Hospital.
In the first place, the prices were not doubled. The
ordinary booking price of stalls and dress-circle is
6,5. The last-night price was fixed at 10s., and there
were not enough seats at that to meet the demand.
The gallery early-door price is 2s. ; for the last
night it was made 3s. There is little doubt that the
increased charges could have been obtained without
any halving. Charity is notoriously the worst line
that can be put at the top of a bill. And when
mean motives are charged to those who place it
there, it won’t inspire managers to be in a hurry
to adopt it. It will be seen, however, that the
Williamson firm, in handing over half the gross
takings of the last night of “Gibbs,” lost on ordinary
charges by every seat sold, and has to foot the bill
(probably £150) for the night’s running expenses.
The Sydney Hospital association, it is safe to say,
never brought in a bob. The occasion was entirely
responsible for the house. In London and New
York it is a regular practice to raise the prices for
the last night. Instance “The Merry Widow,” when
as high as £lO was paid for a stall. That went into
the treasury of the management. Because a show
has a s ceessful run, there is no reason wry part of
its earnings should be disgorged. You don’t see any
other enterprise doing anything of the kind. And
there are concerns in Sydney making larger profits
than come the way of theatrical entrepreneurs. From
what I can see of the game, anyone who takes on
the risks attending theatrical management has a
rhighty solid claim on the fruits of the gamble. If,
incidentally, the manager can hit up a bit for a hos-
pital—not being so greedy as to pocket all he can, like
most people who are out for dollars—of course it is
only to be expected that somebody will take out in
squeal what he is asked to hand in “charity.” It
isn’t hard to spot where the Caledonian figures in
the matter, either.
London’s new immense West End music-hall, the
Palladium, has, after a short experience at the usual
London prices, suddenly introduced a popular scale,
with two “houses” every evening: one at 6.20, for
the “must be home by nine” crowd, and the other
at 9.10, presumably for the “can’t-get-away-till-I’ve-
washed-ups” and the “never-finish-dinner-before-
nines.” The power of the odd copper in England is
shown by the nicely-graduated prices—4d., 6d., 9d.,
Is., Is. 6d., 25., 2s. 6d. Any seat in the house can
be booked for 2d. or 3d. extra, and a fine programme,
including Matheson Lang and Hutin Britton, is
given for the money.
“Harrison O.”: Not only has the drama gone to
bed, but even the vaudeville show has got between
the sheets. The theatrical rage of Berlin is the
performance of Mimi Frohlich, who sings a song,
“Good night and good morning,” while lying in bed
clad in something ravishing in the way of a
nightie.
“Snell”: It has come! We have had love-songs
in comic-opera tuned to the amors of a sausage;
the love affairs of cats, barndoor poultry, and can-
aries have been set to song; in “The Geisha” they
even warbled the pathetic misfortunes of a love-lorn
gold-fish ; but in “The Belle of Brittany” they touch
limit with the ballad of the billvgoat, a love duet of
the common or garden he-goat, with his whiskers
and his peculiar atmosphere thick upon him, and the
twittering she-goat in a sentimental mood. Actor
and actress give Nature studies and life-like imita-
tions of the romantic goat, and nobody throws car-
rots, and turnips, and cabbage-heads, and similar
suitable compliments; although these vegetable pro-
ducts were never more called for on any stage.
“L.H.P.”: Now that there is trouble in Mexico,
someone ought' to give us a play or an opera with
a Mexican setting. It is curious how little use
the English stage has made of the country. Natur-
ally the playwrights of Uncle Sam’s land, being so
handy, have used Mexico over and over again, but
very few of these pieces stand the voyage across the
Pacific. Anyway, they are never seen here. Even
Australian playwrights, who are prone to rush to
London for their subjects, might give it a trial. Its
history simply reeks with ready-made romance.
“S.T.S.”: Surely it is not really “strange,” as
“Harefoot” suggests (B. 20/4/’ll), that Beerbohm
Tree should have selected an act from “Money” to
play on Coronation Night. That King George should
have plumped for Lytton’s old-fashioned work in its
entirety at Drury Lane, “as against the whole range
of the drama,” is strange. But the drawing-room
scene is notorious for bringing so many fat character-
parts on the stage that it has always been the pet
resource of any manager having more offers of help
from stars on a special occasion than he could well
find room for.
Nothing is more finely satirical in Charles Morice’s
romantic story of Christ in Paris than the climax of
it. The machinery of “Christian” civilisation being
brought to a sudden standstill by the influence of
Christ’s presence in Paris, the President of the Re-
public and the Prefect of Police decide to ask Him
to leave France, so He quits the city on Christmas
Day. Just when “Peace on Earth and Goodwill
Towards Men” is the topical text of hypocrisy, Jesus
is expelled by the official powers that be. The con-
trast is striking between the Frenchman’s startling
novel and the saccharine morality of “The Passing
of the Third Floor Back.” The play preaches the
Christianity of the Church, whose comfortable pro-
fessors cut their religion to suit commonplace,
boarding-house humanity, and deal only with the
surface of civilisation. The book strikes at its roots,
and satirises the Church’s pretensions to reconcile
Christian principles with time-saving practices.
The trials of the tragedian, as disclosed in a new
American story, “Jim Hands.” “There are only
about seven shows a year that play this town, and
about three are Uncle Tom’s Cabin companies,
where all the fellows that sit in the front row bring
meat to throw on the stage and make the blood-
hounds forget about the girl they’re chasing.”

MELBOURNE PRINCESS'S.
Picture of Apple Blossom Court, and one of the

“A Rickardsian” (from Fogtown) : There are very
few genuine music halls left in London, most of
them having lost their characters by putting on
rubbishy sketches and one-act plays by frozen-out
mummers. The old-time, merry music-hall pro-
gramme of song and dance has gone, and stupid
animal acts, changeless acrobatic turns, and babyish
bicycle feats, are mere padding for the silly sketch
and the futile farce. Only the Tivoli, in the Strand,
keeps up a high standard of music-hall business.
There were 20 turns last night, some good and
some Yankee, mostly singing and dancing, without
any play-acting. We Australian playgoers have
a lot to be thankful for in relation to our music-
halls.
“R. 8.” (from the Cape) : South Africans have
been remarking lately how few Africanders have
taken to mumming. Australians, on the other hand,
are many in the land arid are doing well. Grant
Fallews manages Leonard Rayne’s cos.; he has re-
sided 20 years in S’Africa. The Wheelers are the
thing in musical comedy, and came here from Aus-
tralia many years ago. The Steel-Payne family of
bellringers "always play to bumper houses, and must
have minted money. Howard Edie has been doing
good business as Press agent, piloting cos. through
South and East Africa and Egypt. He is now
ahead of Sousa’s Band, and is securing guarantees
from almost every town. Ada Crossley is also in
his care. Terry, the wood-chopper, did very well,
and his friend, Donaldson, the sprinter, has also
loomed large on the Rand. Most of the showmen of
the flickergraph are Australians, and a good per-
centage of the pressmen. At one time Australians
were discreetly told to conceal their nationality,
owing to the name won by the farm-burners; but
this prejudice is dying out, though a considerable
amount of jealousy still exists. Which is not to be
wondered at when you consider the positions Aus-
tralians invariably win for themselves in other
countries.
N.S. W. Probate Judge Street had to decide
a curious case the other day. One Abdy, who
married in 1842, left his wife in 1851, taking a son
with him. His wife took another child, a
daughter, and left Armidale (N.S. W.), since when
neither she nor the daughter have been heard of.
Subsequently Abdy turned up in Gympie (Q.)
with a new wife, and lived with her from 1855 to
1889, when he died. Twenty years later a legal
shindy broke out amongst the children concerning
his money, in the course of which frantic
efforts were made to find the original wife, dead
or alive. These failed as utterly as did inquiries
concerning the where and the when of the second
marriage. No proof of the marriage could be
found, and the casual Abdy had likewise forgotten
to make a will to ensure the position of several
children by the second wife. Justice Street
decided that onus of proof of decease of the first
wife and marriage of the second wife rested wholly
with the second family, and, as no proofs of either
could be produced, the estate was ordered to be-
divided between the two children by the original!
marriage, or as many of the two as can be foundi
—which is one.
During a recent inquest in Sydney on at
woman who was alleged to have died from an
illegal operation, the Crown produced several
copies of the Evening News, and, opening one of
March 14, read an advertisement, which said :
Ladies—Consult Nurse Blank, at Blank-street,
Redfern.
Mr. Mant (for the Crown) remarked that the daily
papers still inserted such advertisements in their
columns, although they had been requested not to
do it. Yet, if, as a result of one of these adver-
tisements, a woman went to an abortionist and
was killed, and if the Crown Law authorities then
indicted the newspaper as an accessory before the
fact, the proceedings would, no doubt, be con-
demned as a gross interference with Private
Enterprise. Still, some day even that might be
worth risking.
Adelaide talked much lately about the
“weakness” of Judge Homburg in allowing
counsel to drag out a commercial case from the
end of March to the beginning of May; but
when it ended he showed that he was not too
weak to use an axe. First, he gave his judgment
immediately the last counsel sat down ; and then
he got to work on the Costs. Paris Nesbit, K.C.„
and J. R. Anderson, K.C., on one side, with
Symon, K.C., and Mostyn Evan on the other,
had enjoyed themselves for 18 days over the case,
and it ought (said the Judge) to have been fin-
ished in eight. Costs accordingly. Plaintiff got
the £975 he claimed, and defendants the £lO5O
they counter claimed ; so, if this paper knows
anything of law (and it has bought some in its
time), both sides are now mighty sorry they did
not settle the matter by the spin of a coin when it
first cropped up. Even the Register, with all its
respect for Established Institutions, says that
‘ ‘ many people would almost prefer Purgatory to
a Court”; that “three level-headed men, with
the facts placed succinctly before them, could
surely have settled the case in a day or two ” ;
and that a Commission should be appointed to
inquire into “ the mystery of The System which
tends so often to empty men’s pockets and break
their hearts.”
The Ballarat police have just given up an
identification problem that should teach a lesson
to those who are cocksure of the identity of a
person they may have seen once for perhaps five
minutes. A man who had stayed a few days at a
Golden City hotel committed suicide, and when
the Coroner investigated he was faced with the
fact that the deceased not only owned six names,
but was apparently four or five different people.
Three persons identified him as Wilson, alias
“ Queensland Jack ”; two others called in and
said he was John Pierce, of Sydney ; and a lady
came in and wept over him as her dead brother,
one Sagar, whom she identified by certain tattoo
marks and clothing and missing teeth. After that
a reputable citizen dropped in and said the corpse
was his old pal Charles Blanchfield; he
described certain articles they had bought toge-
ther, and, sure enough, similar articles were
found in deceased's swag. Later still a Geelong
man said that Percy James and Sagar were one
and the same, but that “ Queensland Jack ’’ was
different. Finally, another identifier said that
deceased’s real name was Willis, and —but at
that stage the Law shut down, and buried the
departed as John Wilson, and left the tangle for
Judgment Day to straighten out.
A few days ago Invercargill, M.L.’s big-
gest “dry ” town, came out of its sly-grog hiding-
places and went the total animal in the open for
a change. Selecting Park Reserve, in the centre
of the town, about 30 men rolled large 25-gallon
kegs of beer into it on the Saturday night, and
proceeded to create a record in drunken orgies.
All through that night and Sunday they alter-
nately sang, cursed, fought and pursued blue
devils round the casks. When the Southland
Times called round before going to press with its
Monday morning issue it found things like this :
The park wore the appearance of a gory battlefield.
Several heroes lay prostrated in the pig pens, while
those able to maintain a position approaching the per-
pendicular were so besmeared with blood and candle
grease and other things as to be almost unrecognisable.
When the mail left, Invercargill was wearing ice
on its head and taking its solemn oath to put
more water in its prohibition.
“Harrison 0.” : The time is “ripe” for
somebody to protest against the impudent appeal
which is being made by the perpetrators of the
“Charles Dickens Testimonial.” Unlike many
writers, some of them of much greater tonnage,
Dickens received adequate financial recognition
in his lifetime, and when he died he left between
£70,000 and £BO,OOO. In spite of this the hat is
now being taken round on behalf of his descend-
ants, who were given a good start in life and have
not apparently done anything in particular.
Furthermore, I draw attention to the following
passage from Dickens’s will: —
I conjure my friends on no account to make me the
subject of any monument, memorial or testimonial what-
ever. I rest my claims to the remembrance of my
country upon my published works, and to the remem-
brance of my friends upon their experience of me in addi-
tion thereto.
So there is no apparent reason why Australians
should lose their wind in their rush to purchase
Dickens stamps.. In fact, there is no apparent
reason why the pennies which they feel impelled
to part with should not be handed over to the
descendants of some Australian writers who did
not receive financial recognition in their lifetime,
and were unable to leave 80,000 farthings to their
dependents.
WHERE HONOR’S DUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The play was quite a clever one ;
The season had been good. “B’ gee,”
Said Clarence Montrose (Private Bunn,
The walking gent.), “my boy, this run
Is my big triumph ! You just see
’Twill set them hot-foot after me.”
Miss Peggy Smitt, the sweet soubrette,
Exclaimed: “Old chappie, ain’t this gay?
It’s just my surest cinch as yet.
They’ve got my measure now, you bet.
I’ve filled the house up in this way
For seven weeks come Saturday.”
The heavy villain, black and bad,
Led me towards the bar. Purred he:
“You see how I have hit ’em, lad?
Five hundred quid a night, be Gad.
’Twas my big effort in Act 3
That saved this show from bankruptcy.”
The languid leading lady thought
That this success was by a mile
Her finest, and she guessed they ought
To raise her splendid screw a nought: . . .
The hero ’d made the boss’s pile, ....
So had the leading juvenile.
Up in the gallery one night
I met a youth they paid to rouse
The folks, and wedge them good and tight.
“Lor’ bli-me,” said he, “ain’t I right ?
There ain’t room left to swing a mouse.
It’s me’s the bloke what packs this house!”

SILAS SNELL.
HIS PRICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The actor thin, with beetling brow,
Leans gently on his beer,
And draws it softly home, and now
With elocution clear
He starts again his piece to speak:
“I have few equals, and I seek
Me six-and-thirty pounds a week.”
His hair is long and ragged, and
His coat is much the same;
He wears a straw without a band,
His boots are thjngs of shame;
For all the bar to hear, says he:
“There are cheap actors, but, siree,
I stand for price and dignitee.
“ ’Tis thirty-six or nix, and that
Is what I tell the crew
Of managers so sleek and fat.
Begad, I get it too!”
I think of quaint penurious tricks
And ask, “He gets the thirty-six?”
“No,” sighs his friend, “he gets the nix!”

THE OPTIMIST.
“Hie! Penny section , please .”
Ching Tin, a Celestial resident of a north-
ern (Australian) port, had not long joined the
local mission before he became a leading beacon,
and the first anniversary of his conversion was
specially marked the “No. 1 Jesus Man ” (as
the Chows term a bishop) presenting him with a
jlirge Bible. John was profuse in his thanks.
He boarded every vessel to spread the virtues of
the Good Book before his poor unlettered country-
men ; he read texts to the quartermaster at the
gangway, or to anybody else he met. Passengers
.gave him tips, and old ladies told him the story
of their lives —or as much of it as would appeal
to his simple nature. But one day a young and
hard-faced Customs officer was transferred to
that port, and the first time he met Ching coming
off the gangway with his large Bible under his
arm he insisted upon studying the Good Book
there and then. And, of course, it was a dummy
containing a few real leaves in the middle and
about 21b. of opium in the cavities on each side.
Just at present there is a vacant seat in the
Mission.
“Neptune”: “Klinker” (B. 27/4/T1)
neglected to mention an important matter in con-
nection with the management of inter-State boats,
namely, the neglect of boat and fire drill.
Writer is at present on a vessel which has not
had her machinery overhauled for four years, and
boat-drill is more honored in the breach than the
observance. I’m told, however, that on some
“ liners ” boat-drill is quite unknown, and that the
boats have' not been off the chocks for months.
In one instance, a fire broke out, and the water-
tight doors were so corroded with rust that it
was impossible to close them. Some responsible
Government department seems to be sadly neg-
lecting its duty.
* * * *
The S’ African Medical Gazette mentions
cartridge-eating as a legacy of the Boer war.
The British Tommies, when they had no matches,
took to pulling the cords from the Lee-Metford
cartridges with their teeth and using them to
light their pipes. They found that the contents
of the cartridges produced “delirious intoxica-
tion,” and now cartridge-eaters are almost as
common in military circles as beer-chewers. It
is appropriate that a war which drove staid,
elderly citizens to publicly stamp upon their hats,
and which gave the word “maffick” to the Eng-
lish language, should also be the means of supply-
ing the Bulldog breed with a brand new means of
getting deliriously tight.
* * * *
“Quiff-Stick” : With the railways under
one control and the army under another, what
would happen if the Real Trouble was around ? A
battery of artillery started to return from the
Easter Camp at Heathcote to Sydney. The dis-
tance is about 24 miles, and the time taken for the
journey was about 10 or 11 hours. The battery
was timed to leave the railway station at 11 a.m.
The men were there with all their guns and
horses promptly. The train wasn’t. It was two
hours late in arriving, and then only half of it
struggled tothe rendezvous, the trucks required for
the guns having been left behind. Possibly they
got mislaid, or the railway officials thought the
men could take the guns in the compartments in
their arms. Anyhow, the horses were trucked
and the procession moved off, leaving sufficient
gunners to entrain the guns when the trucks
arrived. The men and horses reached Sydney
about 5 p.m.—six hours to do 24 miles. By this
time both men and horses were feeling a bit
peckish, naturally ; but as there had been no cal-
culation that an hour’s journey might stretch into
a day, they had to remain hungry. The men, of
course, took turns at going in search of tucker;
but as the horses could not be taken along to the
refreshment room and fed on buns and sand-
wiches, they had to put up with it. The guns
finally arrived at something after 9 p.m.

“The Binnacle Boy” : The yellow peril
can do things on board “British” ships which
no white mariners would venture on. We left
Puget Sound for Cape Verde Islands with a crew
of Chinamen. On
the run down to
Magellan Straits
beri-beri broke out,
and the Old Man
ordered salts every
morning for all
hands. On the
third day the deck
crew refused to
“turn-to” as a
protest against the
white man’s medi-
cine. Next day
one man died. We
were making ready
for his burial at
sea, when all hands
came aft, with the
bo’s’n as spokes-
man, and said that
we must not bury
a Chinaman at sea;
if we did, there
would be “plenty
trouble.” It was
a direct challenge,
and the skipper
gave way. We
boxed the body up
and carried it on
deck to our coaling port, Monte Video. There the
ship was delayed six hours while they arranged
matters with Joss. If white men did things like
that, they would have to stand trial for mutiny ;
but the beloved Chink got off with, “ Oh, well, I
suppose it is their religion.”
* * * •
Something is being attempted to check
the sale of naughty books in Australia. Frank
Tudor, Minister for Customs, has come to the
conclusion that, if the Government merely pro-
hibited the landing of books bearing certain
titles, the same old muck would come in under titles
altered to meet the case ; and, anyhow, a black-
list would be a fine advt. for a prohibited book.
He is therefore asking the States to consider the
question of putting a heavy penalty on the sale of
wicked books, though he would bump against a
difficulty of vastly greater size and angularity if
he sat down and proceeded to say what books are
wicked and what are not. When the inventors
have produced the completely satisfactory wet-
wool tester, they might; be invited to set their
brains to the job of modifying it to meet the
requirements of this case. A sinometer is badly
needed, anyhow.

I shall return her erring husband."
From the Oldest Inhabitant’s under-
study :
At the south end of Swanston-street there is a hunch
of four pubs to wet the giraffe throat of Melbourne:
Young and Jackson’s, E. Champion’s Queen’s Arms, C.
Champion’s Town Hail, and Reynolds’s Cathedral. The
two Champions closed their doors a few days ago on
account of the death of their estimable mother, a vener-
able lady. All four pubs are ancients. E. G. Fitzgibbon
owned the Cathedral, and much indignation was
bestowed on such an eminent churchman for not calling
it the Pig and Whistle or something else respectful.
The excellent mother Champion was widow of Ben
Champion, who for so long carried on the Queen’s Arms.
Tom Monahan was there before him, and someone else
before old Tom, who worked up from being groom. The
first Melbourne Wesleyan Chapel, a tiny brick place, was
on that site. It was "used for a Sunday-school after the
white-plaster Wesleyan Chapel arose on the corner of
Collins and Queen streets—a block afterwards sold for
£40,000 to the Bank of Australasia. Wesley Church, an
awful blue elephant, came out of that. Meanwhile,
Monahan, as a good R. 0., utilised the Sunday-school
for his scullery, even as the R.C.’s bought the
Presbyterian Chalmers Church, and actually wrought
the very stones into a convent. The scullery Sunday-
school was only demolished when E. Champion made his
recent alterations, transforming the humble two-storey
Queen’s into a fine five-storeys. Through marriages of
heirs and heiresses, old Monahan’s money mingled with
that of Wallace, the mining man and boniface, who
owned so many pubs in the Ovens and Buckland district.
The lawyers afterwards sliced the coin over the divorce
case of Wallace v. Wallace. The original Wallace insisted
on wearing a jumper about Collins-street till he became
an M.L.C., when he condescended to a coat. Ben Cham-
pion gave his sons first-rate education at Trinity College
and the University, and never expected them to turn to
the beer-pump. But they knew where the money is.

The Bulletin received the following in
its mail the other day:—
Dear Sir I am the undertaker’s man, and this opens
up a wide field on scientific knowledge in The Bulletin
for inquiry about which there has always been a dispute
as to whether long-legged or long-bodied people live the
longest not in length I mean in years but I have much
pleasure in forwarding herewith the answer of the
inquiry for I asked the boss and he said he packed at
least 10 long-bodied people for one long-legged man or
woman the latter is very fatal to a long life if she has
a long body and the only one the boss knows with
long body who lived a very long time was Queen
Victoria whose legs were only about two feet
long but I dont know its no interest to me now as you
asked me I’ll respectfully reply that long bodies have too
much works also large quantities of meat, etcetra, sauce
and cups full of tea go down that won’t go down short
bodies that has no room to spare and as any scientific
undertaker knows its a long lane that has no turning
but you don’t want too many turnings hence it makes if
worth your while to have long legs and short body of
course it means less graft for the boss but its no interest
to me we’re trying to get a wages board and then he can
go to as far as that’s concerned and I’ll conclude hop-
ing you have long legs.
“Red Tape, junr.” : While Minister
Trefle was advising the students at the Hawkes-
bury (N.S.W.) Agricultural College to stick to
the science of the soil, and assuring them there
was big money in the game, the Public Service
Board was advertising for an “ experimentalist ”
for Hawkesbury who combined sound scientific
training with the ability to plan and carry out his
own agricultural experiments. For this he was
offered £l4—my mistake, £4 a week! I’ve
decided to hang on as lift-boy in the Department.
Our head messenger gets £4 a week ; and a head
messenger doesn’t have to work.

LLITTLE ISAAC : “ Fader , keep it dit to-morrow
and it'll go for a penny."
The S.A. Register, the most lady-like
daily paper in all Australia, has for Some time
been endeavoring' to coin a pretty name for the
man who blacklegs on his mates. “Loyalist”
was the term used for a long time, but now it is
“upper-class workers.” Later it may develop
into “ gentlemen workers,” and later still into
“gentlemen helps.” But when the strike is
over they will be shooed off the premises just the
same.
THE JOY RIDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Whizzin’ down a hill-side,
Fizzin’ up a lane.
Honkin’ round a corner,
Cut and come again ;
Open up the throttle,
Then “ advance the spark”—*
Out upon a joy-ride
For a bally lark !
Shavin’ of the herbage
On a cuttin’ side ;
Smuggled up beside me
Temporary bride.
Her enjoy it ? Rather !
Thinks that I’m the boss
(Boss he doesn’t “ hook ” ’em ;
That’s the old chap’s loss).
Let her have the “ three gear,”
Now she’s got the “ high,”
Flyin’ the macadam,
Tunnelin’ the sky !
Car? She is a bonzer !
Girl? She is a duck !
Sizzlin’ on the bridges,
Dashin’ through the muck ;
Swishin’ and a-swayin’
Through the flyin’ mud,
Girl she feels the corp’scles
Tinglin’in her blood.
Ilissin’ on a down-grade,
Hear her pantin’ faster ;
Girl in admiration
Feels I am her master.
Just a little joy-ride,
Merry half a day,
Rtpid girl-and-boy ride—
Clear the bally way !
Reach our destination ;
Little “camp” and then—
Not half so interestin’
Cornin’ back agen.

The Victorian Board of Health recently
adopted a regulation designed to change the
methods of the insanitarians who supply refresh-
ments in glasses “washed ” in a bath of contami-
nated fluid and wiped with a contaminated rag.
The regulation provides that all glasses, cups or-
other vessels, spoons, forks, etc., used in any
restaurant, bar, refreshment stall, booth, dining
room, etc., “shall be thoroughly cleansed by
means of clean running water, where such
is available or can be made available.” The
Board can’t post an inspector in every
hashhouse, pub, stall, booth, etc., so there
will probably still be some filthy beer-vessels set
up on the bar. But it can, anyhow, easily see
that the “ clean water under pressure” is made
available ; and when it is thus available the run-
ning fluid has a very good chance of being used.
“Muzzle Velocity” on something that
wants looking into :
There is an unpleasant odor arising from the Victorian
Defence Force. One Vassey, of the Submarine Miners,
claims to have been unjustly discharged from the service,
and is prepared to support his serious allegations with
many Oaths and affidavits. He clamors for a court-martial,
and that, anyhow, ought to be his right. Another branch
of the service is suffering from a different disease. In one
of the permanent regiments (and there are not more than
a dozen) there are four or five soldiers employed ex-
clusively as general servants whose duties range from
laundry worlc to “ boots.” One of these cheap lackeys
was recently enlisted (for household duties exclusively)
at the age of 58 or thereabouts. These men are as
innocent of the work for which the public pays as the
ordinary Ah Pong is of the Referendum ; and they are,
moreover, in a position to defy any order or legal demand
made upon them by the non-coms.

“ Vagabond ” : “Table Salt” (B. 4/4/11)
climbed up to the top deck of an ocean liner the
other day, and looking down from that great
height, left off wondering why ships occasionally
go missing. To cure bis misgivings let him now
investigate the down-stairs end of one of the
many-decked liners. As he keeps descending one
long spider-thread ladder after another, I think
liis spirits will keep rising ; and by the time he
gets right down into the bottom cellar, he will be
more inclined to worry as to whether there will
be water enough to keep his boat’s floor off the
bottom. And if he stands by and watches a
whole solid cargo being loaded into the ship, and
remembers that all that astounding bulk and
weight is going below the water line, he will begin
to be more afraid of the keel sagging in the middle
than of the contraption blowing over. Then let
him compare the biggest abortion of a piled-up
decker with the spead of canvas on any old wind-
jammer, and then note also their respective grips
on the water. Of course I know that the sailer
doesn’t have all her sails up in the big blows :
but even in the worst she carries some to “steady ”
her. So, one way and another, I think I’ll con-
tinue to sleep soundly in the top attic of the
tallest skycraper that swims, as long as I believe
she is sound in wind and limb.

It is 50 years ago this month since the
Gabriel’s Gully (Otago, M.L.) goldfield was dis-
covered, and the jubilee is to be celebrated at
Lawrence (formerly
“The Junction”) on
May 22, when the
old-tiniers will have a
night out and the once
famous fast packets
Young America, Red
Jacket, Blue Jacket
and others will sail
out of the past and
unload long proces-
sions of ghosts down
the gangway of
memory. The occasion
also provides a great
opportunity for the
M.L. Cold - Squash
party to celebrate the
fact that at a meeting
of the diggers held
on the field in July,
1861, Gabriel’s Gully
was declared the first
“dry”' district in M.L.
This was decided upon by a
committee appointed by
those on the field to draw
up rules and regulations for
the proper conduct of the
diggings, and was confirmed
at a general meeting, How-
ever, as it is to-day in M.L.’s
“dry” districts, so it was
then—high jinks and grog !
The favorite depot was the
Sportsman’s Arms in Wether-
stone’s, a neighboring gully,
where about a dozen Fairies set
the pace and the joyous cork popped
till dawn. At night the big dining-
room was turned into a dancing hall,
where the girls got a percentage on the
drinks they sold, and the rouseabout who carted
out the,dead marines died of a broken back six
times a week. But that was 50 years ago, and
Gabriel’s Gully isn’t a bit like it to-day.
“Tom Ess” : One of Sydney’s noblest
buildings is that of the Lands Department, a
magnificent specimen of the Italian Renaissance,
which has the rare privilege of standing clear on
its own block, with ample surrounding space to
show its fine proportions and artistic design. It
has four entrances, one for each frontage ; yet, to
gratify the whim of a Minister, a great hole has
been punched through the solid sandstone, to;
make still another entrance. It looks like a
hideous scab on a beautiful face. The permanent
disfigurement is to give more ready access to and
from a staircase which leads to the Minister’s
holy shrine, thus saving a walk of fully 60ft. to
80ft. To preserve a grand design, as an alterna-
tive, the inletting of a small iron gate in the
larger one close by was suggested. But a little
brief authority would have none of that. Some
day a little brief authority will be posting bills on
the golden gates of Paradise if Peter’s kicking-
foot isn’t in good working order.
The Wow,serous Edgar and Judkins, of
Melbourne, have been snarling at modern labor
unionism and trying to promote a new brotherly-
love association ; and at just about that point
Brother Thomas, P.M.G., who is also a Methodist,
dropped into the commotion. Edgar, said he,
belonged to the most exclusive trade union he
knew of. “If there is such a rush of candidates
that there would be difficulty in finding circuits
for them, the books are closed, because it is recog-
nised that for every candidate accepted into the
union (the ministry), a circuit must be found.”
Yet Thomas can’t remember that either Edgar or
Judkins has ever tried to alter that rule of
Methodism, and set up another prescribing free
and unlimited competition—with a dependence on
brotherly love and the other pinchgut virtues.
Willie Judkins, Wowser-in-chief, parson
A. R. Edgar, and a number of Right Thinking
persons have assembled in Melbourne, and started
the new industrial union of Dearly Beloved
Brethren. Clause Bof their fraternal and affec-
tionate declaration announces that the D. 8.8.
Union asserts “ the right of employers to employ
whom they choose (within just lines of wages and
conditions), and of employees to work for whom
they choose.” The student of industrial econom-
ics will appreciate those beautiful words, more
especially “ the right of employees to work for
whom they choose.” This paper has pleasing
visions of Bill Smith, clicker, and Andy Brown,
moulder, marching into the respective establish-
ments of Sprig, Insole, Eyelet and Co., and Bolt
Brothers, throwing off their coats and getting
down to work (“within just lines of wages and
conditions”) without the distressing preliminary
of cadging a billet. It will be seen that the new
(or is it pew?) unionism disposes of the problem
of unemployment in three lines: “Employers
may employ whom they choose, and employees
may work for whom they choose.” In the event
of a hitch, no doubt everybody is to pray, and
W. Judkins will take up a cold collection.
Naturally it will end in Wowsing Willie being
provided with a new flock —of geese.
Now that the last bill has been settled,
Victoria’s Railway Commissioners putH.be cost of
last year’s Richmond collision at .£127,229 over
and above the cost of repairing rolling stock,
permanent way, etc. There were 10 deaths and
480 persons were injured, as against 44 deaths and
417 injured at Sunshine. The cost of the Sunshine
horror was .£125,633. Sunshine’s “death”
account came to £27,837, an average of £633 per
corpse; Richmond’s 10 ran to an average of
£l2Ol. A greater discrepancy is shown in the
respective bills for legal and medical fees, etc. —
£14,075 at Sunshine, as against £19,414 at Rich-
mond .' There were harvesters at Richmond as
well as at the other place.
Melbourne University grads, have decided
to have a residential club of their own. There are
2000 of them, and a nucleus of 500 members is
required. To inaugurate the scheme nearly 200
grads, (a fourth of them women) dined in the
Grand Hotel a night or two ago and reminisced
till a late hour. The oldest grad, present was A.
W. Creswell, an Anglican parson, who took his
Arts degree in 1862. John Madden, the Chancellor,
who was not at the dinner, was the first to receive
the LL.D. degree. That was in 1869. The com-
mittee appointed to draft a scheme for the club
includes the first woman graduate—Miss Cherry.
The committee has its eye on the new arcade
buildings in Coll ins-street, between Elizabeth
and Swanston streets, and thinks it, might do
worse than hang up its hat in specially-designed
quarters there.
“The Religious Editor”: Sydney suf-
fered its annual hold-up in the name of Charity
on Saturday, when about £BOOO was raked in for
the debt-haunted institutions which heal the
sick. The clash of the collection box and the
smile of the more or less tactful female who
pushed it round aroused no thrills in this writer’s
chest. The whole business represents a hideous
waste of gold-stopped smiles and the turning of a
large and serious problem into a one-day bush-
ranging competition for some 3000 women, who
enjoy the novelty and excitement of waylaying
strange men, and penetrating into offices and
other places in search of the small contribution
which rattles like the teeth of the Lost when the.
first breath of winter steals through the engine,
rooms of Sheol. There is doubtless a lot of gen-
uine charity mixed up with the fuss, confusion
and social advertisement of the Hospital Saturday
raid ; but it is a degrading business in a civilised
State. Probably the temporary financial scoring-
board on the Bank of N.S.W. in George-street is
the chief reason why Sydney endures the raid.
So long as he has a scoring-board of some sort to
look at, the Australian will stand whatever game
it represents. And the board on the Bank of
N.S.W. represents the great gold-fanged game of
Hospital Saturday.

The other day, in a South Aus. “chapel”
—Methodist churches are still mostly “chapels”
in S. Aus.—the preacher announced the collection
in these words: “ Let us now praise God from the
bottom of our breeches’ pockets.” It may be all
right when Harems become universal ; but, mean-
time, the mostly-skirted congregation filled the
tabernacle with its deepest blush.
SAD!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I know a river where a willow dips
Its long green fingers in the waters brown,
So still and peaceful, though the near-by town
Is loud with shouting from her myriad lips.
I know a maiden, bright-eyed and so fair,
Who came one night and shared the skiff with me ;
We steered up-stream, and underneath the tree
We found Romance, and dallied with her there.
But, ah, it’s desolate and lonesome now !
Bright eyes can no more seek that spot with me.
She is not dead, or far across the sea.
Oh, no. We’ve merely had the usual row.
s. AUS.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
Pick”: “Wang” (B. 64/’ll) asks for
information about the highest-priced dingo corpse.
This may be it. In the late ’7o’s the squatters and
smaller sheep men of Southern Monaro (N.S.W.) of-
fered sums which aggregated £52 for the destruction
of a half-breed outlaw, to which they attributed all
the qualifications of Beelzebub for cunning, and the
villainy of 70 kinds of devils for the ruthlessness
with which it depleted their flocks. The inducement
caused a rush to the district, just as if a new gold-
field had broken out. The hunter who earned the
reward and the renown claimed to have shot the
dingo, after a fierce encounter, in a cave. Certainly,
it was said afterwards that the nark had found the
dog poisoned, the manager of Bibbenluke station
having inveigled him with a bait dipped in boiling
fat—a wrinkle that has often worked the oracle with
dogs or foxes shy of handled meat.
* * * *
“Gip”: That was an interesting par. of
“W.C.M.’s” (B. 6/4/’ll) about the black ‘magpie
and its co-operative building society. I’ve always
been puzzled about the plaintive black mags’ nesting
habits. I knew they clubbed together and built the
clay humpy, for I have seen and heard them at it;
but is the co-op. arrangement carried still further?
Does more than one hen bird deposit her eggs in the
big, trowelled pudding basin, and does the com-
munity continue its attentions to the young when
hatched? Have seen seven or eight round one nest,
looking mighty busy at a time of year when the
cradle was pretty certain to have young in it, but
have never been able to prove the dreadful charge of
socialism.

“Condamine” : Hath music charm to soothe
the savage frog ? I say yes, provided an accordeon
is admitted as a musical instrument. For some time
past I noticed that each evening when I played the
wind-jammer under a tree in front of my tent, a
green tree-frog perched himself on a limb overhead ;
and formed an attentive audience. To see if his
presence there was merely casual, or if he really ap-
preciated my rendering of Gounod and Mozart, I
shifted my quarters to the other side of the tree.
Froggie immediately climbed across until he once
more occupied a position right in the front row of
the dress-circle. There he sat until my mate seized
a hat to take up a collection, when he quietly, but
resolutely, hopped away.
“Vagabond”: When I see the struggles
of some selectors, especially in rough bush country,
I wonder that men can be found game enough to
tackle the job. I ran across one lot a month or
two back. The house (?) was built with scraps of
timber and tin and canvas. It stood, when it wasn’t
sitting or lying down, in a patch of a clearing so
small you could nearly stand in the middle and
touch the trees all round. There was a little cleared
strip along the banks of the creek, and a woman
was digging this over. She told me Jack was up
“the crick,” that this was their garden, and they’d
have some corn in this year. There were about a
dozen dejected and dilapidated fowls mournfully hud-
dled in the lee of the hut, and four kiddies, the eldest
about six, watched me with solemn eyes, and their
fingers in their mouths. I found the husband over-
looking his selection —a little valley with sides so
steep that it seemed to me he could never graze any-
thing but flies there. The hills all round were cov-
ered with the most fearsome tangle of brush and big
timber, and along the bottom, where two to three
acres of bush had been cut down, appeared the most
unholy and hopeless wrecked-up jumble of brush and
branches and rocks and roots and tree trunks that
ever a selector saw in a nightmare. Jack was down
there trying to get fires going. He looked like a
deserted ant in a railway accident, but after a short
conversation he told me to come along again in half
a dozen years, and I’d see a bunch of cows feeding
along there, and the cream cans waiting up af the
gate for a cart. I looked round at that landscape
and I took off my hat to him.

“Tas.” : The latest medical book, just to
hand, contains a list of all the ills that flesh is heir
to, right up-to-date. Moving-picture eye is among
them. Aviator’s head has made its appearance, but
good old-fashioned housemaid’s-knee has not changed
its name yet, I see. There are several species of
hearts, e.g., cigarette-smoker’s, chauffeur’s, mono-
railer’s. There are prize-fighter’s jaw and motorist’s
cramp. Rock-chopper’s phthisis is included; also
curvature of the spine (special brand —motor-
cyclist’s!). “State Ferry Fright” is to be inserted in
the next edition, under an unpronounceable name, and
“Banknotitis” is to be described as “an uncommon
illness to which certain fat people are subject.”
“Wowser’s nose” wilbappear under the: chapter headed
“Throat and Eye Troubles.” The cure is said to
be certain. It is to spend a week behind a bar, and
two weeks surfing at Manly.
“Vagabond”: The only thing that sur-
prises me in “Jimmie Pannikin’s” remarks about
Alliterative Adjectived .Manning (B. 6/4/’U) is that
any of the school kids knew any authors. Some
time ago some of us were knocking around the
country between Port Stephens and Manning River.
One man wanted something to read, and decided that
at the next store or township we struck he’d get a
sixpenny Nat Gould, or something equally soul-lift-
ing. First place he asked if they’d any sixpenny
novels. “No.” “Any Penny Bloods?” “No.” Any
weeklies? Any magazines or patent medicine book-
lets or store council reports, or morning or evening
papers, or any dash thing with print on it? Not a
scrap.

“Isaac Gumboil, J.P.”: Lemme advise
“Dunho” to be careful in his statements about places
and things (B. 20/4/’ll). I, too, am acquainted
v. ith that part of the Yarra below Abbotsford,
where “Teclining here in the cool on a still, sum-
mer’s day, through the heavy festoons of willow
foliage , that listlessly caress the limpid river’s sur-
face, he has seen into the shaded interior of the old
open-built distillery on the far banli.” Last time I
was there the river’s surface didn’t “limp.” It was
far too thick and bloated for that. It just crawled
cozily around the bend, like a mixture of liquid
pipeclay, treacle and bone-dust. The surface was
blobbed and aappled With various-sized carbuncles
of froth, matted wool and fat and scum from the
wool-scouring work 3 higher up. It also bore the over-
shoot from the Salv’army’s rubbish-tip still further
up-stream, mingling with the offerings from the
large and aggressive Nicholson-street drain—which
milks most' of Collingwood between Gipps-street and
Jdhnsoii-street. (Should, my topograpy be somewhat
amiss, I trust to some local alderman cr brother
J.P. to correct me.) That about the festoons of
willow foliage ain’t bad, either. When I was there,
also gazing, I saw a brewery-—with nice, newly-
built additions of red brick —turning out beer and
bringing its shareholders good dividends. Barring
the “shimmerin’ blue,” I like that bit what goes
—“Across the smooth expanse of shimmering blue
there drifted a breath of Bacchanalian fragrance,”
etc. But if he means the hum that hovers over
that slime and filth, which at low-tide shrieks its
shameless secrets into the soft rose-tinted twilight—
well, it don’t seem right to me. And then — “More
than one sad-souled childish dreamer of the slums
has here found a dreamland of rest and romance.”
No doubt they did. I went down one hot evening,
the night before a council election, in search of my
secretary, who used to spend most nights there,
; eeling and smoking. There must have been several
thousand slum kids there, bathing, splashing, yelling
and squabbling, whilst their dads lounged along
: the banks, smoking, arguing, cursing, dozing and
catching eels. The uproar was disgustin’, and when
. I endeavored to stem the flow of hobscenity, a eel
wrapped itself around my head and face, which I
took as an intimation that my hinterference was con-
sidered dee treppe. Still, I must admit there’s
much of interest in that locality. A now world-
famous singer once fished and swam there in Edenic
attire, with the small boys of her day. And a
famous Australian-American artist, in earlier and
less lucrative' days, conducted a humble art class in
the local public hall.

The Czar of Russia writes more in anger
than in sorrow:—
l • should the ear ly youth of “Jimmie Panni-
kin and ‘A. William-Cann” be inflicted on me week
by-week? I am not in the least interested in the
anecdotes of “J.P.” and Mr. Cann. You see, I
know them both. “J.P.” is a dapper youth who
asserts he is the Tenth Edition of the Encyclopaedia
Bntannica, and “A.W-.C.”—a burly brute with a
beard that badly wants felling and burning—has the

am the Czar of Russia, I don’t allow them to remin-
isce in my presence; but I find them both in

in Gladesville Asylum—at least, they were there yes-
terday. I know, because I’m there, too.
’‘Curfew”: “The Ancient’s” fish yarn (B.
20/4/’ll) reminded me of a couple of experiences. I
was fishing in the Yarra, when I hooked something
which broke my line, and got away with a hook. I said
“Damn,” and went home for dinner. In the after-
noon 1 put a new hook on my line, and went back
to the same pitch. Threw my line in, and almost
at once landed a fish. The hook I had lost was in
its mouth, and—-the hook on my line was hitched in
it! I had caught my own hook, with the fish at-
tached. I am not so fortunate as “The Ancient” in
regard to witnesses. But you can take my word for
that, anyway. The other experience was on the
Goillburn, near Shcpparton. A dissipated-looking
person was sitting on a log, fishing. “Good day,” I
said. He didn’t contradict me. “Got any fish?”
He rolled his pale blue eyes round in my direction,
and said, “Don’t be sarcaustic!”

“T..T.M.”: Could any gentleman or cove
tell me of an ointment, spray, salve, or other de-
coction that will vote “No, No” to blowflies on a
horse’s injured leg ? No one need apply with kero-
sene, phenyl, tar, so-and-so solution, or the other
chap’s embrocation; these have been weighed in the
balance and found unsatisfactory. Any other “sure
thing” can be passed up, provided, of bourse, it will
not prove disastrous to the horse.

Liarbird”: There are Australian families
whose chief supports do without beer during the
week in order to buy pork sausages for Sunday morn-
ing’s breakfast. Little do they know! If they
did, they would spend the money on beer; it might
be even better to put it by for missions to Chinese
or pork-butchers. In certain vessels of the pork-
sausage trade large quantities of bullock’s livers
may be seen floating at any hour of the day or night.
These livers float thus for 48 hours or so, until they
are bloodless and pallid; and the pale, pork-colored
result is then put through the sausage-foundry, to
be gloriously resurrected at tenpence per pound in
the form of pure pork sausages of the most unim-
peachable hue and porkiness. Dishonesty is evi-
dently the best pork policy, for this liver-soaking
business is an ancient trick of the sausage trade.
* * * *
“Silas” : How many men who use a ham-
mer on an anvil know the danger in striking a
grease patch ? A domestic tragedy I witnessed in a
mine smithy near Gordon (Vic.) some years ago
will explain. The blacksmith had caught a flea,
and in a sportive mood he fixed it to his anvil with
a drop of fat from a candle. Then he swung his
sled on to the fettered insect, with the result that
the steel face flew from the hammer, shot across
the shop, and struck a small boy in the pit of the
stomach. The flea escaped, but the small boy died
two days later.
* * * *
“Leo Lear” : Remember that Yankee slow-
train story? To the passengers who complained, the
guard said, “Well, git out and walk.” Whereupon
the passenger: “I would, but they don’t expect
me home till the train arrives.” But that train was
greased electricity compared with the one that mean-
ders from Maryborough to Gayndah (Q.). I’ve just
arrived by it, and as soon as I can find out what day
of the month it is, I’m going to enter the steam snail
for the Australian Chess championship.
* * * *
“Trotter”: Walter Goodfellow, chief of
the English Ornithological Society’s expedition to
Dutch New Guinea, contributes an interesting article
to the London Times concerning his outing. He
describes a hitherto wholly unknown region, called
Mimika, the inhabitants of which are absolutely in
the Stone Age, and use just the same kind of imple-
ments a 3 are unearthed in England. None of the
people cap count higher than three, and to give any
idea of number the members of the expedition had to
point to their fingers and toes. The people were tall,
light in color, and amiable. They would give any-
thing for an iron axe. Women are regarded abso-
lutely as inferior animals, ar.d do all the work.
Goodfellow stopped a native who was drowning a
woman with no more compunction than a dog. This
is far inland. On the coast there are similar people
much depraved by making grog out of a kind of
palm. None of the Mimika natives wear any clothes,
except mourning widows, who are covered with gar-
ments of leaves.

“Billy B.”: Barnum Powell, who threat-
ens to inflict himself on this long-suffering country,
owes me a bob, which reminds me that Acting Prime-
Minister Hughes owes
me a “brown.” I was
on the North Sydney
ferry months ago,
when I observed him
studying the sky with
rapt attention. “How
y’ getting on?” I in-
t e rjected. “ ’Tisn’t
that,” he said; “my
trouble is how am I
getting off? I’ve left
my money at home.
Lend’s a penny.” I
did, and he’s still got
it. But about Bar-
num. On the day
news of the relief of
Mafeking came
through, I was in
Wellington (M. L.),
and was invited into
a pub by a lunatic,
who had been kicking
his hat up the street.
The bar was crowded,
and the maniac sug-
gested that in the
commercial room we
could be served by the
waiter. When we , got
there we found it
crowded also ; and two
of the partv were
collecting shillings and
writing something on
a slip of paper. I
thought it was a
“shilling in,” so I
passed one, over to the
collector and murmur-
ed “Whisky, and soda”
to his clerk. Pre-
sently a fat person
with a terra-cotta face
rose and announced
that £2 19s. was the
total, and that it
would be handed to the
Mayor as the nucleus
of a fund to suitably recognise the great (cheers)
and noble (cheers) and unparalleled (cheers)
heroism of Barnum (tremendous applause).
Whereupon I rose and said: “Sir, do I understand
that this is not a bob in and the winner shout?”
“Sir,” he replied haughtily, “I hope this is a sword
of honor for a hero!” “Then, sir,” I retorted, it
is a swindle,” and tore my way through the hoots
to where the girls were serving cordials, 19 at a
time. I weep for that shilling still.
