“Jeroi” : If ironbark in the Northern Ter-
ritory is free, from white ants it must be different
from the Richmond River (N.S.W.) species. Four
years ago a fence of the wood was built along the
river, and to-day the pest is in every post. Some
of the posts have been eaten right away. Own ex-
perience is that bloodwood and red stringybark (or
red mahogany) are the best fencing timbers to resist
both the white ant and fire; and both-are long-last-
ing. True, I have split bloodwood, both green and
dead, and found the heart full of the ant; but the
animal or devil does not seem to touch it when it is
worked up. I have never seen white ant in scrub
box, but I don’t know what the timber is like for
fencing or building. Anybody tried it?
“Mimosa”: “Waring” (B. 23/3/’ll) says
he knows a thing or two about ostriches, but less
about emus. Please let me tell him he knows no-
thing about either. He kicks off by stating that at
the laying period the ostrich hen is a constant sitter,
that it is not until the end of the laying period that
the cock bird takes a turn, that then she has practi-
cally to drive him to the nest, and that, when she
gets him there, she forces him to sit a solid 23 out
of 24 hours. All rubbish! He states that the eggs
are 12 in number.. Wrong again! Ostriches “pair”
for some weeks before the hen commences laying, and -
she lays, according to her condition, from 14 to 24
eggs. The first four or five, which are unfertile, she
rolls out of the nest, to provide food for her
chicks when they arrive. Then she commences in
earnest, laying fertile eggs. Even if the unfertile
eggs outside the nest are removed, she will not roll
out fertile ones. But if the eggs in the nest are
handled during their absence,, especially by strangers,
the chances are that,. upon fhe. return of the birds,
they will kick the eggs and nest to smithereens.
Thenest is made by the birds lying flat on the
ground, and scratching the earth or 1 sand away/with
. the spur on the extreme point of the wing. The
emu is fitted with a similar wing-nail, which no. doubt
is used for the same purpose. When breeding season
commences the hour of the day or the nighf can
always be told in S’Africa by merely watching the
. nests. From 12 midnight to 4 a.m. the hen is ; sit-
ting ; 4 a.m. to 8 a.m., the cock ; and so on by four-
hour shifts. These times are more or less adhered
.to within half an hour. “Waring” states that in
. S’Africa half the eggs are “unfertile,” which he at-
tributes to the long distance the birds have to travel
for water. Wrong again ! Ostriches do better with-
out water —-—that is, if they can get their natural
food. Copious drinking spoils the feather value by
softening the quill. In one camp of 1000 acres, and
without artificial feeding, the birds living on the
natural foods (aloes, spek-boem and wacht en
beitje) I kept some 200 birds, and the only water
they got for two years at one stretch was the little
that. would lodge for a few hours after, the rare
showers, find the feathers during this period were
superior to those from birds in the breeding camps
i that had access to water.' In the Port Elizabeth
Park (Cape Colony) are two hens and a cock emu,
and last year one of the hens bred out six chicks
while in captivity. Being Australians like myself, I
took more than a passing interest in them. I
noticed that, these emus (even the one not matri-
monially inqlined) cared for the offspring from birth.
An attempt is being made to cross these emu chicks
with ostriches, and I am anxious to see if the
hybrid will show more “wing” that the emu does,
and whether They will drop their inside toes, so that
they may bear a greater resemblance to their rela-
tive ostrich.

“Leo Lear”: The par. (B. 23/3’/ll) re
the lack of sulphur in the language of bullock-
punchers nowatimes, saddles the blame on the wrong
animal. It is the bullocks, not the drivers, that
have deteriorated. No self-respecting puncher of
the twentieth century would waste lurid words on a
team composed of milkers’ calves, black-nosed cross-
breds, Ayrshire-enm-Jersey stags, and all the rest of
the motley crew that make- up 90 per cent, of the
working teams. The poor brutes wouldn’t under-
stand it. Does any “Abo” scribe know of a team of
24 “bawlics,” with language and driver to match,
within the four walls of the continent ?
“Bilbax”: Concerning the friendly wag-
tail (fantailed flycatcher), a girl-teacher, herself as
sweetly feminine as the wagtail, tells me delightedly
of one that every evening accompanies her home
from her bush school, flitting about her dainty feet
all the way. I’m never going to tell her that the
wagtails occasionally play round ifly No. 10 bluchers
in the same way; and I’ll never spoil her little
romance with the sordid explanation that the little
beggar dances attendance on anything—human, bo-
vine, or equine—that moves through the grass and
stirs up the insects.

THE LOST YOUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have a friend who likes to tell,
When beer has fashioned tears,
Of those who used to drink with him
Back in the bygone years.
“Where are the friends of years gone by?”
He weeps against the bar;
I sometimes can’t say truthfully
Exactly where we are.
And as I nod, and shake my head,
And sigh, and order drinks,
Then with his theme he goes ahead,
As through his tears he blinks:
“Where, where, are those I used to know
Why did they droop and fail?
For some are now in lieav’n, I hope,
And some are now in gaol;
“And some of them o’er dangerous seas
Have been long wont to roam;
And some of them are married men,
Who hurry early home;
“And some of them have signed the pledge”
(In grief he falls on me) ;
“But where’s the noblest of them all,
And who and what was he ?
‘Tis then I prop him up again,
And order drinks, and say
It was himself; but, as in pain,
He waves the thought away 1
“I see him now —a stately bit
Of young nobility!
It might have been me, I’ll admit,
But ah! it isn’t me!
“He was a young, pure lad when he
Was young and pure ; the fire
Of genius was in his soul
A man all might admire.
“And he is gone! The stately form
Has vanished from the path;
He was a blast of passion’s power,
I’m but the aftermath.”
lie orders drinks, and broods awhile;
I lean on him and weep ;
Then usually we are informed
It’s time for bed and sleep, v
And then we see each other home,
Blending our tears, while he
Describes in grand and flowing words,
His youth’s nobility.
I meet him sober in the day.
“Last night,” says he, “we had
A rare old time! But once, you know,
I was an awful cad.
“When young I was a sickly prig
A smug, conceited dunce,
Who thought no end about myself,
And never got drunk once.”

Trade Mark Case.
A WARNING.
It having come under our notice that various
substitutes are sometimes sold and passed off
as 0.T., notice is hereby given that this practice
is a breach of the Trade Marks Act, and con-
sequently illegal, as O.T. is a Registered Trade
Name, and applies only to the beverage made by
this Company.
After this date all persons who so continue to
pass off other drinks as O.T. will render them-
selves liable to proceedings under the “Common-
wealth Trades Marks Act.”
O.T. LIMITED,
F. Malvin Quinn,
Acting Managing Director
March 1, 1911.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
MR. JUSTICE ISAACS, of the High
Court of Australia, the Judge in the
Coal Vend Case.
The Victorian Western District recently
lost three of its ancients —Robert Beames, 93 ;
John Ellis, 79; John Rugman, 74. Beames was
an Australian native, born in Tasmania; Ellis
was one of the few who were saved from the
Schomberg, wrecked near Port Campbell in the
late ’so’s ; and Rugman named one of the steepest
hills between Cobden and Port Campbell.
John Melville, who was on Melbourne
Argus for nearly 50 years, died at Hawthorn a
day or two ago. Apart from his staff work, he
contributed to both Argus and Australasian,
using the penname “ Quivis.” He had a decent
style, and always had something interesting to
say. Adam Melville (Melville and Mullen) was
his brother.
“Locust”: When Symon, K.C., com-
plained of a nasty taste in the mouth, Piper,
K.C., suavely suggested (B 27/4/T1): “You’ve
bitten your tongue.” The witty remark is remin-
iscent of the story of the famous French actress
and wit, Sophie Arnould. She had been attacked
by a scurrilous writer, who was afterwards acci-
dentally poisoned. Said Sojjhie : “He must have
sucked his own pen.”
Amongst the unclaimed moneys in the
Barrack-street Savings Bank is said to be the sum
of £3OO collected for the Black Brother who was
with Kennedy the explorer, tended him when
speared, and subsequently protected his body
from mutilation and desecration.
Died the other day John Otton, of Nume-
rella, one of the best-known men and wisest stock-
breeders on the N.S.W. Soutli Coast. Once, at a
Bega Show, the Governor was on hand, and still
the “opening” did not take place. “What’s
wrong? Why the delay?” somebody asked.
“ Young man,” said the official addressed, “ you’re
a stranger here, or you’d know that John Otton
hasn’t come yet, and you’d know too that no show
is ever opened here without John Otton.”
The old N.S.W. Arbitration Court was
evidently not good for the health. Justice Cohen
was President for some years until he felt his
health going. Cruickshank, the employers’ repre-
sentative, became a wreck. Poor Sam Smith, the
employees’ rep., suffered even worse than death ;
he, by the way, is still alive. J. P. Wright, who
followed Cruickshank, also broke down : and the
last Court sitting he attended was held in his bed-
room. To him succeeded T. E. Spencer, builder
of houses and verses ; and, though he saw the
Court out, and thereafter sat as chairman of
numerous Wages Boards, the microbe had evi-
dently got to work in him, too, for he died very
suddenly on Saturday.
The death of Charles Tattam, of South
Yarra (Vic.), at 84, recalls the stirring times of
the Maryborough gold rush in the ’5O s. Police
protection was ineffective, so a vigilance com-
mittee was formed. Of this Tattam became an
active member. One of the “ articles of associa-
tion ” under which the committee worked read as
follows : “ That it be distinctlj 7 understood by the
members that this society does not advocate
lynching.” Nevertheless, there was some sum-
mary jurisdiction, and whoever fell into the
clutches of the vigilance committee had a sorry
time if found guilty. Moreover, the tribunal,
like the High Court, was not bound strictly by
the rules of evidence.
Dutton Green, who died in Adelaide the
other day, aged 60, put in his thumb and pulled
out a plum from many a private enterprise pie.
He was a director of the Glenelg Railway Co. till
the Government took the lines over ; a director of
the Gas Co.-, and of the S. A. Brewing Co., and
of John Hill and Co., and of the Metropolitan
Brick Co., and right along he was at the head of
a land agency business founded by his father three
years before George Dutton was born. In his one
spell in politics, 30 years or so back, he had John
Bray for a colleague. The two ran in double har-
ness, and placarded their district with this mas-
terpiece in verse :
Then vote for Dutton Green so gay,
And chippj, chirpy Johnny Bray.
And the dumbfounded natives voted accordingly.
J. W. Niesigh is the first N.S.W. press-
man to make a professional (or any other) air-
flight : he went with air-man Hammond from
Ascot racecourse to Liverpool (N.S.W.) for the
Sun the other morning. Originally on the News,
Niesigh attracted “Curly” Hutton’s attention
when that officer was in control of Australia’s
military forces. The pressman was made the
General’s secretary, subsequently rose to the rank
of captain, and helped in the South African raid.
Invalided home, he turned again to presswork,
joined the D.T., and gathered in more than one
“scoop” for the paper. Perhaps the best was in
connection with the wreck of the Annasona a
couple of years ago on Middleton Reef. Owing
to lack of space all press representatives had been
refused a passage on the tug sent out to the 7 wreck
from Sydney; but Niesigh stowed away, and
when he unearthed himself it was too late to put
him ashore. The skipper swore like 10 tomcats,
but a little thing like that didn’t stop the Tele-
graph coming out with about 10 columns of the
dreadful happening.
The Sydney dailies were all at sea con-
cerning the ancestry of J. F. Plunkett, who died
at Neutral Bay (N.S.W.) last week. His grand-
mother was not “one of the beautiful Misses
Gunning, whose portraits are historical,” as
alleged by S. M. Herald (4/4/T1). One of these
famous ladies married the Duke of Hamilton (and
when he died the Duke of Argyll), the other the
Earl of Coventry. A Major Plunkett who fled
from Ireland after the '9B rising did marry a Miss
Gunning. She was the only daughter of Colonel
John (not James, as the Herald has it) Gunning
by his marriage with Miss Minifie—the Marie
Corelli of her day. She was anything but a
beauty. Colonel Gunning was a pronounced
“dog” or “nut.” He eloped with the wife of
his tailor, and a writ for £5OOO damages found
him safely outside the British jurisdiction in
Naples. The Colonel’s wife was, amongst other
things, a tireless matchmaker, and her efforts to
get her daughter off occasioned infinite merriment
to the wits of the day. Walpole, that relentless
hater of all the Gunning family, was particularly
acid on the subject.
W. F. Buchanan rode off to the Last
Muster from Sydney last week, and nearly
10,000 square miles of station property, 100,000
cattle, 50,000 sheep, and much city estate
changed owners in Consequence. He was born in
Dublin in 1822, and came to Australia with his
father, an ex-69th Regiment officer, in the ’3o’s.
The elder Buchanan secured Dartmouth, on the
Hunter (N.S.W.), and later two other properties
in the Gunnedah and New England districts.
After his death W.E. got the lot by buying his
brothers out. In 1849 he did some mining, and
claimed to be the pioneer of the Dralla (New
England) goldfield. In succession he bought
Breelong, Warrana, Yoolundry, Coonamble and
Magometon stations, and then sold out and
bought Killarney, near Narrabri, which he kept
till he died. He also owned considerable slices of
Queensland and the Northern Territory, and
kept his nose continually to the grindstone until
he died, a lonely old man, perpetually distressed
by the thought that nobody wanted him except
for his money.
Every Australian who has read Mrs.
Aeneas Gunn’s book, “ We of the Never-Never,”
will be sorry to hear that Henry Peckham was lately
drowned at Campbell’s Springs, Victoria River
district (N.T.). Peckham, bushinan and mailman
in the Northern Territory for over 20 years, was
“The Fizzer” of the book. “It takes a bit of
fizzing through, all right,” was the hardest thing
he was ever known to say about the scorching
stages he had to battle through. And in the end
—drowned!
PROFESSIONAL PRIDE.
CLEAVER, M.D. : “ That’s a case of mine.

ROD, C.E.: “What with? Gas?”
One of the most successful unrehearsed
“hits” at a recent smoke concert of N.S.W.
Writers’ and Artists’ Union was the fiery speech
delivered by Bernard Ingleby, a well-known
Melbourne journalist. Bounding to the platform
like a kangaroo, he treated his amazed audience
to a fantastic disquisition on the defects of
Monarchy that would have made Deutscher Bill
and Alphonse the Rake writhe had they chanced
to have been “among those also present.”
Ingleby’s forthcoming publication, “The White
Republic”—an illustrated monthly—is due for its
first appearance shortly. It will agitate for a
Universal White Republic, led by Australia, as a
barrier to the further spread of the colored races.
Its editor announces that it will be run on
“ cosmic” lines, with the ultimate object of
attaining a White Man’s Universe. It should be
a remarkable production, to say the least, and the
solar system is anxiously awaiting the arrival of
the first issue, billed for June.
Dr. Malcolm Maclaren, the geologist who
was recently engaged reporting on Westralia’s
goldfields, has been revisiting his old home at the
Thames (M.L.), and has taken the opportunity to
repeat the warning he gave
Maoriland’s windy capital
four years ago. The geo-
logical expert considers the
possibility of a serious
earthquake in Wellington
to be so probable that he
says: “To build there at
all is folly; to continue
building in defiance of
the well-known principles
governing the construct on
of earthquake-proof build-
ings is criminal.” Mean-
while Wellington continues
to nail its jerry-built man-
sion to the side of a steep
hill, and puts its faith in
Providence and hard liquor.
C. H. Goode, the
youth who has lately been
elected president of the
Adelaide Y.M.C.A., was
an M.P. in the Wheat
State 46 years ago, and he
was nearly 40 then. He
has been toiling since he
was about 10 (just upon
three-quartersof a century),
and he has made much
money, which he gives
away freely. At 84 he is a portly, up-
right old man, full of energy, and never happier
than when he is with other youngsters with 60 or
65 years less to their credit than himself. His
firm deals in softgoods and makes straw hats.
That eminent little lawyer, N.S.W.
Equity-Judge Simpson, when he first took up
week-end residence at his Blue Mountain home,
\fras sorely troubled by the incursions of vagrant
cows, of which he at last complained to the
police. Now, it happened that, as his Honor, a
man of simple and unobtrusive ways, had arrived
at Blackheath without beat of drums, he was
personally unknown to the local force, and when
he visited the police station he was met in the off-
hand fashion in which official personages of almost
despotic power are wont to receive meek people
of no apparent importance. “ Luk here, me
little mahn,” said the gigantic senior-constable
pityingly, and placing his massive hand upon the
shoulder of his diminutive visitor, “ whin ye get
to know as much about the law as Oi know, ye’ll
be aware that ye can’t legally pound cows off un-
enclosed lands outside a municipality. Do ye
mind me now ? ”
Joel Eade, who has been widely accused
of being the father of technical education in
Vic., died at Collingwood last week, aged nearly
89. Having made a bit of money on the Beech-
worth and Daylesford diggings, he began business
as a builder in the city, and built a good deal of
old Collingwood. Thereabouts he established a
school of design in 1871, and two years later
organised a technical exhibition, and kept the pot
boiling until the State was ready to step in with
substantial help.
Australia lost a valuable man the other
day when O. A. Sayce, tdl recently assistant
lecturer on bacteriology in Mel-
bourne University, died suddenly
—it was on the very day on which
he was to have taken-up his new
work as director of the South Aus-
tralian Bacteriological Institute.
Though in a way he made a
specialty of the fresh-water Crus-
tacea of Australia, he was a very
versatile scientist. An important
contribution by him to the litera-
ture on micro-organisms in cere-
bral meningitis will shortly be pub-
lished.
Died at Parramatta (N.S.W.) last week,
John Ferguson, late editor and proprietor of the
Cumberland Times, aged 80. Mr. Ferguson
came to Sydney in the early ’so’s, and for many
years had a book-selling business and supplied
the Education Department with school books
and stationery. A pungent writer, he was fre-
quently in hot water ; but he seemed to like that
temperature best.
Wrecks seem to run in some families.
Among those drowned at the time of the loss of
I the Gothenburg, on the Queensland coast, was
Captain R. G. A. Pearce. His son, Tom Pearce,
was afterwards wrecked in the Eliza Ramsden,
off the entrance to Port Phillip, but was saved ;
and he was on the Loch Ard when that vessel went
ashore at The Caves, near Moonlight Head, on
the south coast of Victoria. Tom Pearce swam
ashore, and also saved Miss Eva Carmichael.
These two were the only survivors. Pearce then
joined another Loch vessel, which was wrecked
on the Irish coast. He is now master of a steamer
belonging to the Southampton Royal Mail Co.
His son—the old captain’s grandson—Thomas
Richard Pearce, had no sooner started on his
career than he was wrecked in the Loch Ven-
nacher.
Mostly about another George :
St. George’s Day having just passed, with George as
monarch vice Edward, it is curious to note that George
became England’s patron saint vice Edward. Edward, the
Saxon, was too monkish—too saintly. Gibbon traced St.
George to a rascal known as George of Cappadocia, killed
by an angry mob in 361 a. d. ; but his latest editor won’t
have it so. George, he says, was martyred at Lydda,
Palestine, in 303 a.d. According to this view, George was
a noble Roman officer, who tore down a copy of Diocle-
tian’s edict suppressing Christianity, and suffered for it.
Then came Constantine, who turned - Christian, and
sainted George. They did it quicker then. Nowittakes
400 years, and Joan of Arc is hardly put through vet.
When Richard I. was crusading in Palestine he was
greatly tickled with the fables concerning George, and
adopted the cry, “ St. George for England!” But not
till the reign of Edward 111. was the uncertain and ob-
scure gentleman made the complete national saint. The
dragon business is partly an application of the Perseus
and Andromeda legend, the locale of which was not far
from Joppa, and Joppa is not far from Lvdda. Perseus
saved Andromeda from the dragon. The explanation
probably is that George made a breakwater, and saved
many souls from the dragon-like sea.

the last boat.”
The dreadful Vic. parson Worrall pub-
lished a letter the other day about labor and the
Labor Government. They asked Hughes about it
next day. “I have never before read anything
like it in my life,” said Hughes. “Was that a
minister of Christ speaking ? I thought it was a
toad spitting.”
EUREKA!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Another Eureka veteran has passed out at .—Any
paper, almost any day.
When the grey campaigners gather just outside the
Gates of Gold,
Will the old Eureka veterans get a special warrior fold ?
When they roll up in their thousands, as they’re counted
here below,
Will the scoffers gibe and jeer them for their proud
Eureka “ blow ” ?
It’s a puzzler to the student who has read that Lalor’s
braves
Never numbered full three hundred—men who scorned to
live as slaves—
How it happens that each hamlet, from the Gulf to Mel-
bourne town,
Boasts a pair or two of battlers whom the Eeg’lars hunted
down ;
Hunted down the hills and valleys, while old Peter
wounded lay
On the field of our one battle, soiled with hero-blood and
clay ;
Hunted down with ball and bay’net, through the gloom
of Freedom’s night,
Just because those gallant ragers raised a rifle for the
right.
I’ve a great respect for heroes, for our heroes are but few.
And the old ones die off quickly, and vve seldom breed
’em new ;
But when ev’ry fiddling village breeds a round Eureka
score
I begin to feel a lusting for the high Eureka gore.
True, it may be Peter Lalor had five thousand men at
call,
Or it may be that the thousands come of tipple, talking
tall ;
But the fact is, as we wander down the placid paths of
Peace,
And the older men keep dying, so the Stockade boys
increase.
And if e’er we offer pensions to the warriors who survive
I’ve a notion they’ll be swarming like the bees about a
hive :
And the crowds who’ve fought with Peter will be famous
ne’er a jot,
And the most distinguished people will be those of us
who’ve not.
QUEENSLAND.
P. FLAM.

This bank is not pushing ahead very fast. Four
years ago, it will be seen, the profits were almost
equal to those of to-day. Now, that wouldn’t be
satisfactory if the advances, and consequently the
risks, showed a large growth. As a matter of fact,
they have moved on only from £2,718,914 to
£2(861,602. There has been a fairly large increase
in deposits—from £3,112,486 to £3,796,311. In-
stead of being lent out, on mortgages, etc., almost
the j whole' of this money has been added to liquid
assess. Plainly, then, this bank is playing for
safety. It has the money, and it could get business
at a price; but it does not believe that the return
is good enough in view of the risk. Values certainly
have been very high, and a shrinkage in almost every-
thing is pretty certain to come sooner or later. The
bank which took the risk and said that the slump
wasn t due yet, has, of course, made a heap of
money. But it might easily have been otherwise.
Also, it will be otherwise some day. When, therefore,
the. slow movement of this bank’s profits is noted,
let it also be noted that its risks have (so far as
one; can tell) had a very slow movement, too.
The most unsatisfactorv feature about it is the
wretched lay-by. The dividend should not have been
put; up past 6 p.c.—the reserves should have been
greatly strengthened first. As it is, with the re-
serves so small and growing so slowly, the bank can-
not afford to do much more advance business without
getting in more share capital. Already it has about
£7i 4s. of the public’s money for every £1 of its
own—and that is quite up to what is generally re-
garded as the limit of safety. Certainly it doesn’t
matter much what amount of public money a bank
hasi so long as it merely locks it up in a -vault. But
if it uses more than from 35 to 40 p.c.
of ; that money, it should not take more
than £7 from the public to every £1 of its
own —or, rather, it should get in more share capital,
or lay by more profits, if the proportion goes beyond
seven to one. At present, though the proportion ex-
ceeds seven to one, 42 p.c. of the public money is
m liquid assets; so the position is apparently quite
secure. But if the advances increase, then, in view
of the poor reserves, more capital should be got in.
It shouldn’t have .been necessary, but the premature
increase of the dividend will make It necessary.
Uecent movements are shown next:—

There is only one bank with head-6ffice and most of
thq shareholders in S. ,pnd this is.it.
Moreover, practically all' its interests are in the
State ; it has a London branch,’ but>nonej in: any part
of ;the Commonwealth outgide S, Aug. i This localisa-
tion no doubt has its disadvantages.- If S. Aus. is
hard hit by drought or other cause,.the bank ;has no
chance to even things up by doing well elsewhere.
But it has its distinct advantages, .too. The manage-.
meht can know all the tmsinfess • as : i f it were its
its own, and not a corporation’s. And, as it happens,
thp management is a wonderfully’ well-informed one
so ,far as S. Aus. is concerned.' It may go wrong as
to, the trend of politics; but it knows the State
through and through otherwise. Further, it corn-
mands unbounded local confidence. In all the ' cir-
cumstances it isn’t surprising that a large helping
of;the S. Australian fatness: of'the past seven years
hag landed on this bank’s plate. Between 1900 and
19(11 the visible profits have more than doubled ; and
on, a 10-year view the figures are almost as striking.
There is no mystery about it, either; the balance-
shifet totals explain it all:
j March, 1901. March, 1911.
; Liabilities .. .. £5,250,938 £5,437,119
i Liquid assets .. 1,322,535 2,613,110
j Advances .. 1,489,453 3,712,290
Premises .. .. 58,447 88,205
TI us the advances have considerably more than
doubled. Further, while,’it; is. .quite . possible that
thfcre were some lame ducks among the accounts 10
years ago, there should be nothing of the kind there
new. Certainly some of the accounts may go lame
by-and-bye. Farming land in S. Aus. has reached a
staggering price in parts; as much as £l4 an acre
is being paid. Old ideas as to what are legitimate
prices have been temporarily hung up. It is not
much use saying that land is not worth more than
£5 an acre for wheat-growing, when in hundreds of
cases men have cleared £2 10s. or £3 an acre a
year for six or seven years. But just as there have
been extra-fat years there will be extra-lean years.
When the latter come there will be trouble for the
new man who, not having had two or three good
seasons to stand on, has to pay interest on (say)
£lO an acre. How much a bank will lose will de-
pend on the margin it has insisted upon. How much
loss it will show will depend on the size of its con-
tingency fund or undisclosed reserve.
There is no way of telling what a bank’s inner
reserve amounts to. A hint may, however, some-
times be got by viewing the rate of its earnings
calculated on capital and visible reserves; if the
rate is high, it is a pretty good hint, as a rule,
that the bank has really more assets than it claims.
In this case the earnings are about £8 16s. Bd. p.c.
" 1 ( can t be stated exactly, because some new
capital has been got in during the year. Now, this
isn’t very high; but other things have to be taken
into account. For instance, ' the amount of the
public s money in use. The bigger the amount in
proportion to capital and assets, the bigger the risks
(especially if the money is loaned out), and the
bigger the profits ought to be. Here the total lia-
bilities are equal to £5 14s. for every £1 of the
shareholders’ funds; and that is a comparatively
small liability. Moreover, no less than 48 p.c. of
these public liabilities is in liquid assets. These
ngures point to a very strong position, looked at
from every point of view. They suggest, though ad-
mittedly they do not prove, a good inner reserve
to provide for a possible—even probable—shrinkage
m the value of securities.
One more comparison to show the movements of
the past year:—

which permitted this substantial addition to reserves.

Ihis is the one Australasian State-owned concern
doing a general life-assurance business. It is handi-
capped in some ways. It confines its operations to
Maoriland’ and therefore can’t show the big figures
which, though they may really be an element of
weakness, are an attraction to so many people
Moreover, its bonuses are not very big; and very
big bonuses are an attraction to "so many people,
though they may mean nothing either. Bonuses
arc a return of the money in excess of what is
really required for the purpose for which it was col-
lected ; so that the bigger the premiums the larger
the bonuses ought to be. The insurer may there-
fore be best served by an office which pays the
smallest bonuses, provided that its premiums are
smallest; wherefore, when big bonuses are traded
on, it is always well to turn up and compare pre-
miums charged. In the case of the Maoriland office,
they will be found to be unusually low.
The business is not done very cheaply—possibly
tor the reasons mentioned above. The State office
CO u m £ eteS ,^ or policies in the ordinary way, and
whether that isn’t a mistake is a matter on which
different opinions may be held by equally sane men.

Much of that expense is quite unnecessary. The
railways don’t spend hundreds of thousands of
pounds in snaring passengers and goods. When you
want to go from one railway town to another you
just go—the railways don’t spend 25 p.c. of the fare
in chasing you up, and arguing with you, and bring-
ing you up to the scratch. If they did they would
have to charge you 25 p.c. more than they do at
present. It could be exactly the same with a hundred
other things if the public chose to make it so. Ap-
parently, however, it has resolved to make this office
exist on a starvation diet; and though the office can
even then do good service to the country, it doesn’t
do anything like the service it might.

Standard-Waygood. Ltd., an amalga-
mation of two Sydney businesses, whose chief object
is to put in and look after electric lifts and other
electrical machinery (goodwill, £15,000 to start
with) :
Towards
Mar., Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.
1910. . £11,078 12£ p.c. =£5,750 £5323 £9672
Premiums on new shares .. 5825 3847
Reserves.
1911.. 17,273 12£ p.o. =lO,OBO 7193 3346
That is one of the most remarkable records in con-
nection with any Australian flotation of recent days.
Commencing only two years ago with a deficit of
£15,000, representing goodwill—which, of course, is
only a paper asset —it has already wiped that out,
and established a substantial reserve; and meantime
it has paid a dividend of 121 p.c. p.a. Some help
certainly has come from premiums on a new share
issue, but only £5825 so far. There was a first
issue of 50,000 £1 shares. The second issue was _
25,000 shares at 255. ; now there is to be a third ‘
issue of 25,000 shares at the same price. Altogether
£6675 will come in as premiums. This will formally
wipe out the goodwill, and leave the co. with re-
serves of £10,021 —without anything added as profit
from the current year’s business. It is a splendid
record, and all the better because the co. doesn’t
possess a monopoly, though certainly it has exclusive
rights to some machines and processes; and, more-
over, the large construction works in progress in
Sydney at present are quite abnormal. So far as the
lift and elevator business is concerned, the co. has
the competition of the Hydraulic Power Co. In
consequence of the rapid extension of the business,
it is quite likely that further capital will have to
be got in. There is the £31,250 to come from the
third issue; but large work-shop additions are called
for, and, to be on the safe side, shareholders are
asked to sanction the creation of 150,000 new shares.
That, however, doesn’t mean an immediate, or even
an early issue; in fact, there are many cos. which
never issue their authorised capital. But with the
rapid increase in the use of electrical appliances,
the issue of the new shares is a probability which is
well worth taking into account. The co. on March
31 owed a total of £18,877; in cash and invest-
ments it had £16,212; and that was apart from
book-debts, £29,945. Its financial position is, there-
fore, very sound. Stock and work in progress run to
£43,800 ; plant and tools to £5155. Depreciation
has been written off; but the amount of the pro-
vision is not specified.

* * * *
Aerated Bread Co. (Sydney), which was
formed early last year to take over the A.B.C. cafes,
and began with £17,965 of its capital represented
by goodwill, which, of course, is a purely paper
asset: —
Profits. Dividends. Written Off. Deficit.
Apr., 1910.. £17,965
Sept., 1910.. £-2347 6p.e.=£1050 £428 17,096
Mar., 1911.. 3384 8 „ ~ 1400 228 15,340
This puts the position in the best light*- Thus there
is a paper asset apart from goodwill—leasehold pro-
perty, which was £3750 to start with. This may,
however, be passed as a real asset, though it is a
wasting security. The amount shown as written off
is in reduction of the book-value of these leaseholds.
This is not strictly a writing off at all; it is more
truly rent. You take a lease at a comparatively low
price, payable in cash; but in addition to paying
the cash, you undertake to put up certain improve-
ments, which latter fall into the landlord’s maw at
the end of the lease. Clearly, then, the cost of the
improvements is part of the rent. In the foregoing
table the profits are, therefore, swollen by including
these amounts as profits. Except these amounts
nothing is written off. The provision for deprecia-
tion is included with the reserves. This depreciation
reserve amounts to £1000; if this means that plant
has had £lOOO chipped off it —which is highly pro-
bable —then the true profits for the year can be
stated as follows: —
Profit as above .. .. .. .. .. £5731
Less unexplained reduction of good-
will £l5
Leasehold sinking fund .. .. 656
Depreciation 1000
1671
True profit £4060
Much depends on the provision made to cover depre-
ciation. Wear and tear is heavy in a business of
this kind ; and the question is whether the year’s re-
newals, plus the £lOOO, cover the full depreciation
for the year. On that point each man must judge
for himself. The co. is launching out .into numerous
branches; and the directors report very satisfactory
progress. The heavy goodwill item is a severe handi-
cap ; but this class of business is so profitable that
the paper is being pretty rapidly replaced by cash.
There are two weak points about it. (1) A co. or
private person without a goodwill loading, could
say, “Well, if this concern has to pay interest on
£35,000, more than half of which is not repre-
sented by tangible assets, I can plainly sell cheaper
and still make good interest, because I only want in-
terest on my actual investment.” (2) The co.’s
shops are all leaseholds, and if a profitable business
is established in leasehold premises, the landlord has
a habit of saying at the expiration of the lease,
“Where do I come in ?” And then up goes the rent,
or out goes this tenant and in comes another —pro-
bably in the same line of business.

This co. has lately been putting substantial sums to
reserves ; for the past year the total lay-by is greater
than the dividend. But, on past records, the profits
are erratic, probably because commissions on the
corpus of estates are taken credit for in lumps. The
paid capital is £lO,OOO. This and the reserves are
by a £5OOO Treasury deposit, £13,577
advances on mortgage, and a few trifles. The interest
earnings ( £432 for the past half-year) are almost
sufficient to pay the dividend, leaving the profits
from the business to go to reserves, where, of course,
they form a fund to guarantee that the co. will do
the things which it undertakes—a. guarantee which
no private trustee by any chance ever offers. Be-
sides the paid capital, there is a further £40,000
for which the shareholders are liable; and that adds
to the guarantee.
The public subscriptions for Bananaland’s
new loan amounted to only 9 p.c., leaving over 90
p.e. to be taken by the underwriters. Of course the
usual yarn is told —the loan was a success, because
the State will get the money. True, but the market
has yet to absorb that 90 p.c., and until it is ab-
sorbed the chances of floating another loan are nil
or thereabouts. Moreover, the bigger the proportion
left with underwriters, the bigger the underwriters’
risk, and the heavier their charges on future loans.
One way and another Denham’s prospect of loading
the already overloaded State with another
£10,000,000 isn’t brilliant. J. Bull has evidently
got very tired of politicians who shriek about the
horrors of Socialism, while begging for money to
carry out Socialistic works.
* * * *
Bananaland finance is running as wild as
ever it did. “Trust” expenditure has leaped from
£345,000 for 10 months to £715,728 for the same
period—more than doubled. Loan expenditure has
bounded to £1,624,502 for the same term, an in-
crease of £362,659 on the corresponding 10 months
of the previous year. _ The two together total about
2f millions so far, an increase of nearly three-quarters
of a million on last year’s ten months’ record, with
a fair promise of mounting up to nearly £3,000,000
by the end of the financial year, 1910-1911. Bv the
way, Premier Denham was one of the old disaffected
Tory supporters who abandoned Philp in 1903, on
the ground that he was a financial incompetent, and
not to be trusted. Now Denham is knocking out
Philp’s worst record.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners make
a very important change, practically abolishing re-
turn tickets. Simultaneously they reduce the single
fare to about one-half the present return fare. This
and a few other modifications will, it is estimated,
mean a loss of £50,000 a year to the Department
—that is to say, a gain of £50,000 to the travelling
public. The return-ticket system will apply in future
only to suburban lines and the tourist, week-end, and
agricultural show business. The change goes the
right way. The State railways don’t have to compete
with other railways, and, therefore, there is no busi-
ness reason why they should offer inducements for
the passenger to come back over the State railways.
If he wants to come back from Bourke or Bathurst,
the passenger will inevitably come back by rail. If
he undertook to come back within a month, the rail-
ways have charged him (say) one-third .less than if
he took two single tickets, not being sure whether
he would come back in a month or nine months.
The man travelling to work will be particularly
benefited; hitherto he has been more or less sacri-
.ficed, while the holiday tripper has had an advantage.
The latter won’t be appreciably worse off now; the
other will be appreciably better off. Apparently the
change will not at present extend to inter-State
traffic. A railway conference is, however, pending,
at which probably the matter may be further dis-

has a coalfield estimated to be capable of putting
out 1,000,000 tons of good bituminous coking coal
a year for several centuries. Large coking
ovens have been installed. The co, employs 20,000
men at 3d. or 4d. per day per coolie, and Is 3d.
per day for the highly-skilled worker. Labor is so
cheap and plentiful that it does not pay to instal
labor-saving machinery, as they are constantly doing
at Pittsburg. Here in China there is the ideal com-
bination of factors for successful iron and steel pro-
duction —plentiful iron, coal, fluxes, and lots Of
cheap laborers, with an almost unlimited market in
the vicinity. If this development is causing un-
easiness in the United States, with how much greater
reason should it cause the Commonwealth Government
to think hard and act promptly! The Australian
iron industry will be in constant danger of its life
if the Commonwealth is not prepared to go to the
length of totally prohibiting Chinese or Japanese iron
or steel from coming into the White Continent,
A crisis has arrived in the affairs of the
Broken Hill South Blocks, and 8., M. and Co. are. in
consequence seeking earnestly for some process cap-
able of securing a high extraction at the lowest pos-
sible working cost. 8., M. and Co. Beldom leave any
avenue to success untried, and that is why they are
now having 100 tons of South Blocks ore put through
the Murex magnetic plant oh Block 14. The Murex
is a most attractive process so far as the first con-
centration goes. It will easily obtain an extraction
of 85 p.c. of the metal contents of ore. But when
the ore is Broken Hill silver-lead-zinc, a second
concentration is necessary. This is a much mope
complicated job than the first process; and it is
this part of the business that is still merged in
partial obscurity. No reliable results of the full
treatment are yet available) but it is time they were.
The South Blocks’ parcel of ore will be treated by
the complete process, and on the result of that ex-
periment will depend whether the Murex process
will be installed at the South Blocks or not. Owing
to the ore being pretty well beaten out of the South
Blocks down to the No. 4 level, the average grade of
the sulphides sent to the mill has fallen from 16.4
p.c. lead to 14.4 p.c., and as the silver doesn’t
amount to much, a high extraction is absolutely
necessary if the mine is to be run at a profit.
* * * *
So much stacking of zinc concentrates by
the Broken Hill producing companies looks as though
the Zinc Convention has been putting the brake on
production in order to hold up the market. In ad-
dition to about 40,000 tons paddocked alongside the
railway line at Burns, on the South Australian border
—the product of the De Bavay Co. and Zinc Cor-
poration—the Sulphide Corporation has about 20,000
tons of zincs stacked on the Central lease. A similar
quantity of zinc concentrates produced by the B. H.
Prop, is stacked on that mine. All this stacking of
material means that a big bill is being piled up
against someone. . At first, when the dumps started
to grow, a lack of ships was hinted at; but that
excuse has now been just about exploded.
* * * *
T. A. Rickard’s London Mining Maga-
zine says, apropos the tremendous litigation among
the zinc flotation cos., that “W. L. Baillieu goes
to London presumably to the Coronation, really, it
is believed, to try and bring about a consolidation
of the various flotation interests. It is said that he
expects a knighthood. Personally, I think he would
have tenfold more gratification in pulling off the
amalgamation.”
* * * *
Queen Bee, near Cobar (N.S.W. ), hopes
that prospecting below the 570 ft. level will re-
sult in a healthy addition to ore reserves. There is
talk of 60,000 tons of ore lying thereabouts; but
shareholders can only see such things with the eye
of faith, and the eye of faith is one of the finest
magnifying glasses yet invented. Exploratory work
at 570 ft. has certainly resulted in the discovery of
some rather patchy makes of ore; samples taken at
163 ft., 167 ft. and 172 ft. assayed respectively 7.96
p.c., 10.77 p.c. and 5.14 p.c. coppef. The
first was for a width of 3ft.,. and the other
two for a width of 12 inches. The '.'samp-
ling and assaying were done some time ago.
Near the surface the Queen Bee was a bulky, gos-
sanous formation, that made into rich ore at depth,
but when the sulphide zone was reached average ( values
declined, so it became difficult to keep up an average
extraction of 7 p.c. copper. Even that value was
only maintained by careful picking, and towards the
end of the smelting campaign did not prove profit-
able.
* * * *
Mount Bopp.v, the most fruitful gold mine
in N. S. Wales, made a net profit of £42,563 last
year, and distributed £42,350 of it. That meant
7s. per share, as against ss. 6d. in 1909. The ore
taken out was 78,690 tons; nevertheless, it is rec-
koned that the reserves were increased by 9000 tons.
Further, the two ore bodies came together in Taylor’s
shaft, and the junctioned body is going down very
strongly. The position as to reserves is very good;
so is the financial position. The one insect in the
honey-pot is the altering character of the stone; but
even that insect isn’t -as large as an albatross. The
change calls, however, for some new and modified
plant.
Harden (N.S.W.) is now on the wrong
side. Last month’s working costs ran to £1459,
while the total revenue was £1313. It is now a
question of scraping up ore, so mining costs are very
heavy. Things may look better when the co. can get
into the new Central workings, though prospects are
still only very moderate.
Following modern American practice, a
scheme is being arranged for the amalgamation of
some 10 or 12 copper mines near Tottenham, in the
central west of N.S.W. There are two groups of
mines in the district, separated by an unproductive
zone about 10 miles wide. One of these groups, that
at Tottenham, is of a basic nature, the mines there
producing a pyritic ore, carrying roughly 4 p.c.
iron, 4 p.c. sulphur, and 7 p.c. silica. The copper
content in this group, at the present depth (350 ft.),
is a shade over 4 p.c. The other group, at the
Albert Waterholes, carries about 45 p.c. silica, with
about 6 p.c. copper at the deepest point reached
(280 ft.). With the advent of the railway to Tot-
tenham—which the Government has undertaken to
construct, and which will connect these two groups
with the seaboard—the project should be a success.
The present method of ore treatment at Tottenham,
by reverberatory furnace, is expensive, and also
chemically wasteful. With an up-to-date plant and
water-jacket treatment, there should be fair profits,
as ore supplies are plentiful.
The Cessnock Collieries, Ltd., was lately
put on the London market, with a backing which
practically ensured a float. Capital, £350,000;
vendors take £50,000 in cash and £75,000 in
shares, leaving £225,000 for working capital. The
bulk of this is to go in building a railway to the
mine, which is in the South Maitland district. Dr.
Robertson estimates that there are 43,300,000 tons
of workable coal in the 2872 acres; and, when the
expectations of the promoters are realised, there will;
they say, be a profit of £97,500 a year:. The pro-
moters’ expectations are pretty large. East Greta,
about the most Successful of the Maitland collieries,
does very well to make a profit of a good deal less
than halt that much; # #
On February 28 last Lake View-Star
(W.A.) estimated its ore reserves as follows: Lake-
View leases, 156,555 tons, worth 28S. 3d. per ton;
Hannan’s Star leases, 246,763 toils- Worth 295. 6d.
per ton; total, 403,318 tons, valued at 295. per ton.
The Star leases Show art increase on last year’s esti-
mate) which was 166)500 tons of 325. 6d. ore; but
since then Morrison’s lode reserves below No. 3
level have been included. Lake View’s little lot has
diminished from 306,000 tons to the figures givert
above; but a Comforting feature about the present
estimate is that the value is greater.
Want of fresh water at Norseman (W.A.)
is seriously interfering. with Mararoa’S profits.
months ago, when the dams dried up and the scarcity
first made itself felt, the Co, substituted salt for
fresh water in the treatment of slimes. This altera-
tion played the mischief with the following month’s
extraction, ahd consequently the plant was shut
down. Mill and Sands plant have since been kept
going with the Salt fluid; but; what with the- in-
creased CoSt of working and the extra losses in the
residues; it is estimated that the return is between
£5OO artd £660 lesS than it ought to be. Unfor-
tunately the Co. Cannot Stbp the sands portion of
the plant Without Shutting down the whole treatment
works, artd rather than do this it prefers to carry on
under present conditions until ' rain comes to its
assistance. Under the circumstances, this is probably
the best thing it could do.

Bullfinch East (W.A. ) has come to. light
with a rich leader somewhere below 100 ft.,, and when
the last mail left the West the eo. was busy 1 bagging
ore. This show has hitherto been regarded as'a value-
less delusion stuck among the granite, consequently the
sight of gold in its workings has given rise to a
wild whoop of joy on the field. So: far the leader
only shows a ribbon width, but already inches are
being magnified into feet, and gold values have
been estimated at lOOoz. or thereabouts, ' Judging
by local exultation over the find, all that is now re-
quired to make Bullfinch take leave of its. senses is
for some of “the surrounding schist that carries
gold” and the “2ioz. hornblendic rock” to again
make its appearance in the Bull Ant.

“Oof Bird” ' (W.A.) : Re Bull Am.
“R.B.’s” ignorance of the circumstances surrounding
the flotation of this mine almost takes my breath
away. Either he doesn’t know enough about the
subject to even speak in his turn, or he is trying in
a clumsy sort of way to shield someone else. The
show was not floated on Grut’s report, but on that
of D. L. Doolette. Grut’s opinion was only used in
conjunction with some vague remarks made by Great
Boulder Hamilton about the Bullfinch field. Proof
of this is to be found in the chairman’s address at
the statutory meeting of shareholders. Isn’t it,
therefore, remarkable that such an oracle as “R. 8.”
should have heard of only Grut’s and Henderson’s
reports—the latter of which was not even mentioned
in the prospectus—and isn’t it also remarkable that
he should have never heard of Mines Inspector
Orabb’s report, dated November 21, 1910, which
was two days before the mine was placed on the
London market? That report read as follows: “On
the Bull Ant, which is situated a few chains from
the Bullfinch, a little development has been done on
a quartz vein that, may possibly average five inches
in width to a depth of 40ft., and from which, I have
been given to understand, some samples taken from
the walls gave encouraging results.” Had the gist
of this report been published in London before the
float came off, the guarantors of the issue would have
been stuck with, most of the shares; but unfortu-
nately, the widows, orphans, country curates and
other gullible persons who made a wild rush to get
in did not hear about it until it was too late. Ap-
parently there are many things about Bullfinch that
“R. 8.” doesn’t know; but for one who has had so
much to say about the subject, his want of know-
ledge about the Bull Ant passes all understanding.
* * * *
The great profits that have been soaked
out of some Victorian tailings heaps, both battery
and alluvial, have led to a rush for tailings, the con-
sequence being that heaps which were regarded as
valueless a couple of years ago are now being
worked, and even 2s. stuff is being tackled. At
present it is about as easy to find a profitable un-
worked heap of tailings in the State as to stumble
on an alluvial bonanza; but every successful attempt
to knock a penny or so off the costs leads to a fresh
attack on a crop of slightly poorer stuff. One of
the most striking features of the industry has been
the development of the art of selling “interests”
or shares in ventures. Many enterprising individuals,
by preserving a judicious secrecy about their real
returns, and cutting a dash in the eye of the public,
make a good thing by taking up dog-poor heaps, and
generously selling a part-share to an unsophisticated
stranger at such a price that the original holder
“stands on velvet,” no matter how returns go. By
estimating expenses on one day’s running, and allow-
ing nothing for wear and tear and the loss of time
and profit during the wet Victorian winter, and by
reckoning probable returns on the highest possible
returns shown by small bottle tests, almost- any
heap may be made to appear a bonanza to a man
who doesn’t know the game, and who judges it
simply by paper estimates. Thus many profitable
attacks have been made on the pockets’ of usually
shrewd Victorian business men. Worked on legiti-
mate lines, the cyanide business in Victoria still
offers many openings for the investment of capital,
but the devious methods of some of the partner-
cyaniding experts tend to an absurd price being
paid for heaps, which, in itself, tends to the dis-
advantage of cyaniders who work the process as a
legitimate industrial undertaking.

crushed 742 tons for £2092, which is poorer than
recent returns. Reserves are being drawn upofi at

* * * 4
. Mills United, Charters Towers, crushed
1038 tons for £3422. The directors hold pretty fast
to a cash credit of £41,729, irrespective of the Bur-
dekin mill account, which is good for £9618; so
some of the shareholders ask what about a dividend.
Still they have the candid admission that the upper
levels are pretty well worked out, and that for the
future the Co. has to depend on the somewhat specu-
lative Chance of locating an isolated shoot of min-
eralised Stone 2400 ft. from the surface and some
1000 ft. . eist of the main hauling shaft. Sinking
below No. 14 level to prove this deep shoot will be
done about midway between the main underlay and
the Freehold’s boundary. Prospecting at depth in
the western section has not so far been successful,
but crosscutting between the parallel formations may
prove something. One thing can be said in the co.’s
favor—isolated makes of payable stone in a system
of parallel reefs are characteristic of the Day Dawn.
* * * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, is send-
ing larger crushings to the mill, but the returns are
not as good as were expected. Last year the average
was over £7 per ton, but that value was based on
small crushings of picked stone. Prospects have
been good in places, but they have been extremely
variable. There is no solid reason for thinking that
the Mexican, when tested on an output of 10,000
tons, will shape better than its close neighbors, the
.N. B. Freehold and Mills United, did. In that
Case the Mexican will have to be satisfied with the
value disclosed by- its last crushing of 492 tons for
£2261, or an average of £4 11s. lOd.; and even
that is better than Day Dawn crushings usually are.
Admittedly it is risky to estimate future profits and
dividends in a half-developed show; but if the Mexi-
can comes. up to the ideas of its enthusiastic ad-
mirers, it will certainly require a big slice of luck.

* * * *
Tongkah Harbor tin, the Siamese dredging
concern, after being under a cloud, gets rather
liberal backing again. Last month’s return of 92
tons of black tin is just ahead of Mount Bisehoff’s
regular 90 tons. Tongkah doesn’t, however, trouble
about sending the assay of its concentrates, which
may contain anything from 55 p.c. to 70 p.c. metal.
There is no more certainty about the future than
ever there was; and whether the co. can go on
dredging at this rate for months or for years is
quite a matter of guesswork.
“Phoenix” : Except for the bids of the ‘hut-
toners” the sale was very dull. Indeed, had it not been
for the buttoners’ nods, the Semetic-looking gentleman
would scarcely have been able to knock down any-
thing. Then a young fellow, wearing a new hat, a
crumpled suit and a coat of Outback sun-tanning,
strolled in. He was “spotted” at once. “Now, the
next article I have to offer is a solid gold, keyless,
hunting, presentation watch. The movements are
in perfect working order; compensation balance,
jewelled, and guaranteed to keep correct time. Now,
what shall I say? Gimme a start. It must be
sold to the highest bidder. One pound—one pound
—one pound I’m bid. Twenty-one, twenty-one,
twenty-one! ” Meantime the assistant was handing
it round for inspection and sidling his way to the
Outback. “Look ’ere, gents, my instructions are to
sell without reserve. It must be sold. Twenty-two,
twenty-two—twenty-three shillings. Any advance on
twenty-three shillings? I’ll knock it down, ’pon my
soul I will, although I don’t like sacrificing it.”
Just as the assistant reached the intended victim a
confederate bid twenty-four shillings, and after a
few earnest words from the young fellow to the
countryman, the latter was induced to bid twenty-
five shillings, and the watch was knocked down Ito
him. He proudly paid the cash and pocketed his
purchase. “The next article 1 have is a solid gold
clustered diamond ring, and I give you my honest
word that every stone is genuine. Now then, gimme
a start.” Then a similar routine was gone through
by the gang, until the same young fellow who pur-
chased the watch was induced to top the market by
bidding twenty-seven shillings. Some years ago
these bogus auctioneers were treated as spielers, and
the police hunted them. Lately, however, they are
more numerous than ever, and appear to be flour-
ishing.

* * * *
“10-2-4” : Light was cast on the ways of the
Gent One in a London case last month, when W. B.
Blackwell, of the well-known legal firm of that name,
sued J. Moir, an accountant, for £l4 125., due on a
promissory-note. The defendant said he had paid the
firm 10 guineas to defend him on an embezzlement
charge. In the middle of his trial Blackwell’s clerk
handed him the P.N. in dispute, saying that, unless
it was signed immediately, Blackwell would throw
up the case. Of course he signed it. The Judge
bumped out Mr. Blackwell, and said many harsh
things to him while doing so. It recalls the fact
that London is the hunting ground par excellence
of attorneys who, under a smug mask of sanctity, are
ready and willing to perpetrate any old villainy.
Australia’s most blasphemous, illiterate and generally
awful Gent One specimens are mere circumstances by
comparison. In fancy, I can see—and smell—a stair-
way within easy hail of the Strand which is the
regular haunt of most of the idle and disorderly
persons in London who have had the good luck to,
fall off ’buses, or over dogs, or down areas, or
(chiefest stroke of fortune) under motor-cars. The
legal firm which inhabits the stairway specialises in
“speculative cases.” Many of those awaiting aud-
ience are old and respected clients—sturdy fakers
who, by reason of some easily dislocatable elbow or
collar-bone, or as a result of a knee in which the
lightest touch produces sine vitis, are enabled to
spend their lives having painless and profitable acci-
dents. They have the various County Court areas
carefully mapped out, and starting with an accident
in No. 1 Jurisdiction are wont to go methodically
through, their list. By the time they get back to
No. 1 they are forgotten, and the whole business re-
commences. The enthusiastic way a few legal firms
hurl themeselves into the list on behalf of their
swindler proteges is amazing to observe. Probably
there is nothing in London to approach a typical
Sydney police-court practitioner in the matter of
disreputableness of appearance, appalling diction and
physical insanitariness. But so far as cool, cynical
unserupulousness goes, the Londoners have our men
beaten out of sight.
* * * *
Almost every week in Australian police courts
two or three traders are lined up for using unjust
scales, and although every lying one of them swears
(1) that the weighing machine had been tested and
found correct quite recently; (2) that the inspector
must have done something to the machine’s internal
organs to throw it off the straight line of honesty;
and (3) that no man could account for the vagaries’of
the infernal thing, hot a case has yet coine to light
in which the advantage of the deal was with the pur-
chaser. The robbery is really encouraged by the
trumpery punishment which follows detection—a
fine ranging from half a dollar and costs to rarely
beyond a couple of pounds. It is profitable at the
price, and boils down to a commercial arrangement
between the law and the thieves, the latter obtaining
permission to carry on by occasionally paying a
license-fee, or whatever you like to call it, into the
office of the former. They had a surer method of
checking the evil over 600 years ago, for it is re-
corded by John Stowe,- the historian, that “in the
fourth year of Edward ll.j Richard Reffeham being
Mayor, a baker named John, of Stratford, for making
bread less than the assize was, with a fool’s hood on
his head, and loaves of bread about his neck, drawn
on a hurdle through the streets of the city” (Lon-
don). That Seems an uncouth method, but it would
certainly have the advantage over that which merely
asks the thief to hand over a small percentage of
his ill-got gains. But since the old homely remedies
have gone out of date; what’s wrong with cold,
whitewashed gaol ?

The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair
than Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is
of the well-known Blake type, but has some ad-
ditional improvements which have justified in prac-
tice all the claims advanced by the patentee. The
work of crushing is done on the down-stroke of the
pitman instead of the up-stroke; there is no strain
on the pitman cap; two large side rods resist the
thrust of the toggles, and enable the jaws to be
regulated within a large range; the back toggle is
so made as to resist all legitimate strains of crush-
ing, but will shear under exceptional conditions and
save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings
are self-lubricating; the jaws are convex and con-
cave, and with the cheek plates are made of steel
and are reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have installed a great
number of these machines, and hold the sole manu-
facturing rights for the Commonwealth.* [3
