ROBUR
Can’t you stop when I tell
you? you know it’s my duty to
drop like a ton of bricks on
people doing wrong, but it’s
also my duty to pull them up
before they go wrong, if pos-
sible.
Now, upon the question of
tea, let me put you right, and
nobody that I know except
my old woman is better able
to do it than I am, for tea’s
the only thing she lets me
drink before I go on duty, and
as I seem to be always going
on duty I reckon I ought to
know something about it.
They do say the Govern-
ment Analyst is a great judge
of most things, well, I must
say he seems to know some-
thing about tea, because he
uses Robur in his home the
same as I do in mine.
It’s the No. i Grade that I
say’s the t:a to use. I’ll back
it against any tea you can put
up against it at the price.
The Policeman
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 8, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
The ’Varsity Women’s Corroboree, about
200 strong, held in the great hall of the
University, is another challenge flung down
at the patent-leather feet of Club Man, who,
for several centuries, has had a monopoly
of dining in herds. There doesn’t seem to
be any solid reason why women shouldn't
rejoice together over mock-turtle soup, or
mingle sentiment with quails on toast.
Hitherto a cup of tea and a scone has been
a popular cement for feminine friendship;
but such mild junketing is passing out of
date. Still, even this corroboree didn’t give
entire satisfaction; Dr. Evelyn Dickinson
is accused of having said at the Women’s
dinner: “A party of women might be full
of brains, but it lacked body. A dinner-
party of men reminds me of a cage full of
monkeys, all arms and legs. The ideal
dinner-party is male and female, as God
created them.”
“All Nations Fair” is said to be still sit-
ting heavily on the chests of some of its
women workers; and they are alleged to
have been, in consequence, less nimble in
pursuit of flying pence on Hospital Satur-
day. If so, I didn’t meet them. Walking
two city blocks was an extravagant tourist-
trip, punctuated by the deafening crash of
money boxes. A feature of this year’s cam-
paign was the increased attention collectors
Paid to their own sex. Last May, many of
those who assaulted the public purse in the
cause of charity turned a callous profile to
the mere woman. oome genius has discov-
ered, since then, that Woman is now a wage-
earning animal. She’s getting out of the
chrysalis stage of having threepenny-bits
handed out to her by the traditional bread-
winner, Man. In the first flutter of her in-
dependence sne’s liable to give quite two-
pence to a really good cause.
. Theatrical ladies failed to achieve the
high-class massacre for which the Lily Bray-
ton collecting box was responsible last year.
But they gave solid help. “The Bad Girl
of the Family” turned over a new leaf, and
made a gilt-edged reformation at the corner
of George and Regent streets ; and “The Wo-
man in the Case” forsook her heartless car-
eer, and temporarily collected charity shil-
lings instead of diamond pendants. The
grand total realised during the day was about
£8000; there are a few returns yet to come
in.
The Girls’ Realm Exhibition was buried
in a Bruce auction on Saturday night. This
final flutter occurred in the Dairy section.
There’s a highly-respectable law which for-
bids any other kind of auction to show its
nose out after sundown. A Bruce auction,
however, may appear by gaslight without a
chaperon. It’s an untamed liammer-rally
that begins by asking about 14 times the
value of a cream cheese, and falls down
without a blush, before the approximate
price. Mr. W. L. Stillman was the hon.
hammer-wielder or Bruce-smith.
A passing wag wrote “dairymaids” at the
end of the list of farm produce advertised for
sale. Another frivolous person furtively
pinned “Sold” on to the back of a good-
looking damsel in the buttermilk section.
There was no riot. A few mild, non-explo-
sive jokes ambled round the auctioneer’s
hammer, and business was brisk.
Mrs. King-Hall wanted to buy a quaint
sampler in the aboriginal ladies’ stall of the
“What-to-do” Exhibition. There was some
technical objection to selling this exhibit,
which travelled down to Sydney from an
out-back mission station. The Duchess of
Bedford also cast a longing glance at this
queer effusion in cross-stitch. Mrs. (Essie
Jenyns) Wood was another victim to its
weird charm. The facts behind this little
patch of crude needlework captured the
feminine imagination. An aboriginal girl
up country had been taught the ant-like in-
dustry of stabbing canvas with cross-stitch;
and out of her inner consciousness she
evolved this strange sampler. The early-
Victorian child worked samplers because she
had to. She watered them with salt tears,
and prayed hard in cross-stitch at the age of
eight (or thereabouts) that her many sins
might be forgiven. She generally finished
up with a staggering garden-path, and an
aggressive clump of lumpy rose bushes. For
some astounding reason this Australian
aboriginal has worked a sampler because she
wanted to. If it be true that no one helped
her, the effort, which is a rough fac-simile of
the early-Victorian species—Noah’s Ark
house, fat rose bushes, curly garden path—
ought to be burnt alive for witchcraft. If
the Mission people can climb over an ob-
scure conscience clause that prevented
their selling it, the incantation in needle-
work will be eventually lodged at Admiralty
House.
One of the male pillars of the Girls’ Exhi-
bition worked so late in the clearing-up riot
in the Town Hall on Saturday that
midnight found him alone with the night
watchman and the prize poultry. He was
too late to catch anything but a cold in his
head, so he looked round for a likely camp.
The Medical Mission bed had a chronic
dummy with a white wig curled up under
the quilt. He gave up his designs in that
quarter, and now desires to express his
thanks to the Civil Ambulance for the rest
its stretcher offered his aching bones.
When Sunday morning came he hunted for
breakfast. He was going to lead the simple
life with some rags of shredded wheat and a
sniff of proteid tea, when a prize fowl os-
tentatiously laid an egg. The male pillar
ate it. The Ladies’ Poultry Club may now
have the satisfaction of knowing that it is
a life-saving institution. It nobly lays
eggs, worth about 2s. 6d. each, for starving
young men.
Mr. Waterhouse, who wears several
learned letters after his name, caught some
of them in sprinting for butterflies. He lec-
tured with enthusiasm at the Girls’ Exhibi-
tion, on the wings, legs, breakfast habits,
etc., of these frivolous beauties. In the
same assembly, Mr. W. W. Froggatt, the
Entomologist, and Mr. B. Gurney—both
much-decorated scientists —introduced in-
sects to poiite society. Mr. D. G.
Stead discoursed on fish, but said nothing
about its getting any cheaper. Various
housemothers present longed to ask why
plain mullet, when offered for sale at the
back-door step, should sometimes wear an
entirely different name. In Maoriland I
once met, at lunch, a well-grown mullet
that was called rainbow trout. Mr.
Harrison, lecturing to girls on his favorite
bird-land, nearly reduced his audience to a
tearful pledge not to wear winged millinery.
The Union Club knew wealthy grazier
Buchanan, who died last week, better than
most people. When it thought of him it was
always as “Bluey,” though nobody seemed
to know quite why it teas “Bluey.” Until
about three months ago he was a hale old
man, who lived in a cottage at Rose Bay ;
but after that he failed fast. The thought
of having to pay some land-tax on the
great Killarney station, which for years
had strangled Narrabri, was one of his wor-
ries. He had five children—a son and
four daughters, two of the latter married,
one to another Buchanan. There will be
enough all round to keep the wolf several
miles away from the doorstep.
According to an evening paper, James
Francis Plunkett, who died last week, chiefly
of ripe old age, had Walpoles and Gunnings
amongst his ancestral skeletons. John Gun-
ning, a briefless Irish barrister, father of
the two famous beauties, Maria and Eliza-
beth, may therefore account for the love of
law which rages in the Plunkett blood. Mrs.
Gunning was the wide-awake partner in
those obscure days in tumble-down Castle
Coote. Seton Valentine lays a tributary
ink-drop on the tomb of this managing mo-
ther. Her courage in scraping together
her slender resources, and taking her young
and lovely daughters across to London, was
one of the Society sensations of 1751. As
everyone knows, Elizabeth married the Duke
of Hamilton with the brass ring of an ad-
jacent curtain, and had the audacity to go
on turning heads till she was considerably
over 50. Maria acquired the Coventry
coronet. The late Mr. Plunkett’s grand-
mother is said to have been a member of this
Gunning family.
A gorgeous flower-trimmed ball was given
last week by the Arthur Allens, at Carrara,
Rose Bay, to introduce their daughter Joyce
to the world of chiffons. It was a brilliant
reply to the pessimist’s plaint, that modern
Society always dines in public restaurants,
and dances in hired halls. The homeless
rich are beginning to rediscover their own
bricks and mortar.
Miss Hope Lamb, who “came out” at the
Carrara ball, is a daughter of Mr. Harry
Lamb, who cultivates the home-grown in-
dustry of wool up Glen Innes way. She is
a dark-haired young witch, with more than
her fair share of good looks. For two gen-
erations womenkind in the Lamb family
have been joyfully copyrighting complexions
that won’t wash off.
Mr. and Mrs. A. W. McCarthy’s young
daughter was another Society first nighter
at the Allens’ ball. She’s in the brunette
beauty line, too, and, like Miss Hope Lamb,
gave the belles of older growth occasion to
have a heart-to-heart talk with their own
attractions. Carrara, by the way, belongs
to the Allen family. It is one of the older
and best-built mansions of Sydney’s habitu-
ally-rich. The Garvans, who rent it, are at
present overhauling Europe’s capital cities
for new sensations and traveller’s lore. The
Hoskins, long ago, spilt a good deal of money
in the upkeep of this Rose Bay residence,
which adds a magnificent bird’s-eye view of
Sydney’s much-advertised water-front to its
other gilt-edged charms.
No hostess who hopes to get her enter-
tainments talked about is likely to hanker
again for artificial flowers to bloom on her
ballroom walls. The beauty of the Arthur
Allen floral display, at the recent gathering,
and the good taste of roses and smilax, which
had the decency to die in secret, after the
first blush of dawn, scored a triumph for
the florist responsible.
A juvenile engagement has its headquar-
ters at Orme, Darling Point. He is Perci-
val Davenport, one of Mr. Frank Daven-
port’s two tall sons. She is Miss Maud
Friend, a slip of a girl, catalogued on the
domestic hearth as “little brick.” Percival
is a medical student. He has apparently
found a few spare moments to tear his
thoughts away from a stony-hearted Senate.
The calming effect of buying an engagement
ring for a nice girl is an undergrad.’s best
recipe for keeping his head cool when heated
remarks career round the Coinmem. door-
mat.
Mr. Horace Muston, who married Miss
Brenda Wilson at Mosraan the other even-
ing^—daylight weddings being on the wane—
is connected with the really-reliable brand
of N.S.W. Bulls, who have been in mercan-
tile pursuits here for about half a century.
Mr. Muston was supported in his cheerful
pilgrimage to the altar by two of the Bull
family, and a noble rally of the name, in-
cluding Mr. and Mrs. John Bull, Mr. and
Mrs. A. J. Bull, and Mr. and Mrs. F. R.
Bull, lined up at the wedding guest-parade
in Mosman’s Town Hall.
A Sydney girl, the daughter of Henry
Moses, is alleged (by the Oldest Inhabitant)
to have married a baronet who finishes up
his name with D.S.O. He caught the last
complaint in the S’African squabble. Horace
Westropp McMahon is the man. He is a
nephew of the late Speaker McMahon, of
the Leg. Assembly, Victoria. Uncle
McMahon was formerly a captain in the
Tenth Hussars. He arrived in Melbourne
in the ’so’s, and followed the drab occupa-
tion of Commissioner of Police, a homely
billet after the gay Hussar lady-killing busi-
ness. The Religious Editor says that the
beautiful Hussar, in the ’4o’s, who didn’t
run away with his colonel’s wife (or try to)
was unmindful of the duty he owed to good
society.
The Arts and Crafts Society recently had
a meeting to assure itself that it is still very
much alive. Mildred Creed, Mary Makin-
son and Berenice Edwell are amongst its
members who were notable for their clever
designs at the Girls’ Exhibition. Miss
Creed’s beaten copper, set with great eyes
of green and blue enamel, is early-Egyptian
in effect. Ra, the sun god, could have
worn such ornaments at Sunday services in
Theban palm groves.
The management of Prince Alfred Hospi-
tal is prepared to shed scones and musical
items on the necks of those who took an
able-bodied part in recent All Nations’ Fair.
This joyous bubble of gratitude will burst
in the Town Plall on Friday.
“Jack and the Beanstalk” arrests the pub-
lic attention at Her Majesty’s. A refresh-
ing change from the type of purple-nosed fe-
male usually laid on to pantomimes by the
light-weight comedian is supplied by Mr.
Neil Kenyon. He has a Percy Grainger
smile, a corn-colored plaster of hair, and a
speckled taste in millinery. Kenyon turns on a
genuine Scottish over-time accent that whis-
tles like a shriek from the bagpipes. A
tired old world is liable to retract its rudest
remarks about pantomime plots when Dame
Trot Kenyon does the washing and dis-
courses on domestic life.
The Girl Scout had better see the smart
military chorus in “Jack and the Bean-
stalk.” After that, she ought to go home
and rearrange her present warlike costume
by putting it under the wash-house copper.
It might set the fire going on Monday morn-
ing, but that is the only kind of blaze it is
likely to achieve. If it be necessary for the
peace of this continent to dress the telescopic
limbs of growing girls in military upholstery,
something smarter than sloppy-looking
skirts and mongrel blouses might be dis-
covered by taking an inventory of panto-
mime warriors.
The short evening skirt, now careering
round Sydney ballrooms, is more useful
than beautiful. No man can put his foot in
its hem. Spurs, which occasionally garnish
the variegated pursuit of two-step time
when uniforms are flying across a polished
floor, used to worry through a good many
yards of silk and lace. In spite of these
drawbacks, the trailing nuisance of long
draperies was better to look at than thick
ankles.
The matinee hat, according to Treves, the
famous English surgeon, comes from Lake
Victoria Nyanza, where the Smart Set wears
nothing else. (Treves published a book
about a holiday trip to Uganda some few
months back.) The Kavirondo dandy, who
is very chic, begins and ends his toilet with
a huge head-piece trimmed with flowers and
feathers. The latest models are quite 3ft.
high. The Kavirondo belle, like most col-
ored ladies, has the dull occupation of being
merely a foil to her splendid lord, and goes
to fashionable bone-feasts clad in a blue-bead
necklace.
Miss Marie Stuart, now taking a small
part at the Adelphi, is an English actress,
who formerly played under Beerbolim Tree’s
management, and wife of Mr. 11. W. Varna.
Marie had the misfortune to introduce her-
self to a Sydney tram, by coming down to
earth while the car moved on. An injured
ankle is her memento of this heartless con-
duct on the part of N.S.W. rolling-stock.
Her brief appearance on the stage is another
tearful aftermath. Her original and im-
portant place in George Marlow’s co.
sprained its engagement in consequence of
the unlucky ankle accident. However, Miss
Stuart thinks that Mr. Marlow is made of
much better stuff than a city tram line.
11. B. Irving, who opens here on June 24,
is noted for his special brand of hat. There
is no other in London like it. Another
mummer, now in Sydney, thinks that the
Australian boy won’t stand the millinery,
but will follow a.nd pelt it with rude re-
marks.
The silk-worm industry is spinning its
way into the affections of women in this
State. A N.S.W. branch of the Queensland
Society has now gathered itself together in
Sydney. The Minister for Agriculture has
taken the cocoon under his official wing; and
Under-Secretary Anderson turns a friendly
countenance to the boom in mulberry leaves.
When this reaches you on Thursday morning
the N.S.W. Wormeries will be taking the
cold chill off their new regulations, by
meeting in the Botanic Gardens. Each lady
will wear a mulberry leaf and other drapery.
Miss Nisbitt is hon. sec. during the first
wriggles of the infantine Silk Society.
Mrs. Forrest, a Queensland scribe and
writer of dainty' verse, is at present con-
cealing herself somewhere in this city.
J. T. Donovan, who has personally in-
quired into the vocal chords of visiting stars
for a longer period of hard labor than
most musical critics here, was married the
other day to Miss Nellie Dwyer. Mr. Dono-
van used to be a melodious singer of note.
He has always been a confirmed student of
matters musical, and now wields an informa-
tive pen on the Sunday Times.
Edith Ralph has been getting her hair
pulled for some of her remarks about this
continent. She calls her book “Australia,”
and swishes her skirts round Captain Cook
and other early trippers to these shores. The
British Australasian has hurled a pebble
at Miss Ralph for mentioning the Austra-
lian native cherry as “a tiny fruit with the
stone outside.” It says there’s no native-
born cherry. It’s a good old bush tradition,
anyhow, and it used to be followed by a
dose of castor-oil.
“Old” girls of the Clergy Daughters’ School
mingled rainbow-cake and reminiscences in
a tea-party to their former Principal, Miss
Helen Phillips. This jamboree occurred in
the chaste precincts of the Victoria Club.
Mrs. It. Mackinnon allowed flier thoughts to
stray from the political situation, and vigor-
ously supported the old-girl demonstration.
Miss Phillips intends to retaliate by open-
ing a Pedlar’s Fair, in the school premises,
on Friday. This will incite a flow of cash
towards the beginning of a school library.
Every old-girl has sworn by the beard of
Lindley Murray, or the braids of St. Cather-
ine, to contribute a book to the Pedlar’s
Fair.
The Photographic Society’s Show closed
with a satisfactory click. The list of awards
included two medals and a certificate of
merit to Lionel Lindsay. Arthur H. Adams
carried off two flattering documents; N. C.
Deck (N.S.W.) was laden with a medal and
a cab-load of certificates; and E. Edwards
(N.S.W.), Gustav Feil (M.L.), E. C.
Joshua (Vic.), and L. L. Raymond
(N.S.W.) are also certificated fiends. J.
Temple Stephens (Vic.) snared a plaque for
his fine photograph, “The Sandhills of Peter-
borough.”
Apropos of the descent of Longstaff on
this town, do you know the square-inch
value of notable pictures? Maurice Brock-
well, who seems to have a dazzling head for
figures, has treated the Old Masters like a
problem in algebra, and worked them out to
a shilling. Holbein’s portrait of Christina,
Duchess of Milan, is estimated at the tidy
sum of £37 10s. per square inch. The Marl-
borough family got a mere £70,00 for
Raphael’s “Ansidei Madonna,” in 1885.
This is said to have worked out at a paltry
£l4 18s. per square inch. The Churchill
family, ivho have smart business ways, must
have bitten their thumb-nails over this sacri-
fice. When the picture had been sold, Sir
Frederic Burton, one of the British National
Gallery directors, cruelly remarked, “It was
worth 110,000 guineas.”
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

THE DASHWOODS.
By STEELE RUDD. With 15 full-page illustrations by CLAUDE MAKQUET.
Price l/= Postage 3d.
When the Dash woods visit the city the fun begins, for Old John and Young Peter seem
incapable of keeping out of mischief, and manage somehow to entangle the whole family in
their ludicrous escapades, until Mother and the girls begin to long for home. For pure merri-
ment and good humor the author has never written anything better than “The Dashwoods,”
which is sure to prove a favorite.
Billy Pagan, Mining Engineer.
By RANDOLPH BEDFORD. With four half-tone illustra-
tions by PERCY F. SPENCE. Price 1/- Postage 3d.
The mining expert sent out by the syndicate to see if
the mine under offer is “salted” or otherwise, has often to pit
his wits against the low cunning of underground engineers, and
is not always successful.
He must be as observant as Sherlock Holmes, as wary as a
tiger and as brave as a lion, for, when baulked, the vendors are
apt to become dangerous.
Mr. Bedford has intimate knowledge of the things whereof
he writes and the power to depict situations and sketch character
in a most masterly manner.
The cases cited here are both thrilling and amusing.

THAT DROLL LADY.
By THOS. E. SPENCER, author of “How McDouall
Topped the Score,” “The Surprising Adventures of Mrs.
Bridget McSweeney,” etc. With 13 full-page illustrations
by Marquet. Price, 1/- Postage 3d.
As Mrs. Bridget McSweeney is the droll lady referred to,

money, for our genial Irish friend is funnier than ever. Her
further adventures are brimful of delightful humor, and lend

ful tonic, Bridget is irresistible. Don’t hesitate about seeking

DAN KELLY—OUTLAW.
Being the Memoirs of Daniel Kelly (brother of Ned Kelly, leader of the Kelly Gang). Edited
by AMBROSE PRATT. With four full-page illustrations by Percy Spence.
Price, i/- Postage Id.
In the whole history of Australian bushranging no gang was more notorious than that cap-
tained by Ned Kelly. Various accounts of the doings of this gang have appeared, but none
bears the hall-mark of truth so pointedly as these leaves from a dead man’s diary, as edited by
Mr. Pratt, who has presented the story so convincingly that, for tense interest and exciting
events, has few, if any, equals.
Dan Kelly was the only member of the gang who escaped the clutches of the law, and his
account of the last stand at Glenrowan is a veritable human document.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, Mat 8, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Caulfield, on Saturday, drew a comfortable
crowd of race-goers, mostly of the motored classes.
Governor Carmichael and his Lady made it their
farewell visit to the V.A.T.C., so the stewards
tendered them a cosy farewell luncheon decor-
ated with roses grown on the premises.
Ihe rose bushes had a show on their own over
the fences of the Petrol paddock, and the vice-
regal pen on the stand was almost hidden beneath
beautiful blooms. The Carmichaels duetted in
grey—Lady Carmichael in iron grey_and lace, and
his Ex. in pepper and salt. A niece muffled regal
ermine round her white-cloth togs.
Mrs. Reggie Power floated about in an absinthe-
colored frock of ninon-veiled crepe and a cape of
manyitailed fur. Tall Vera Clarke, in an all-grey
turp-out, matched the Carmichaels nicely. Mrs.
Alan Currie’s hat demanded notice. It was built
of black velvet, covered about an acre and a-half,
and had as trimming ' a royal , blue feather.
None of the fashionables wore any hair visible
to the naked eye. Nowadays your looks must
be plastered, flattened and tucked away out
of sight. It is hard lines on the woman.who
possesses hair worth showing. Mrs. Ryan, who
was with Amy Castles, had her beautiful corn-
colored glory concealed under' a fashionable
granny. Mrs. Roberts, the niece of our home-
grown Countess of Darnley, has Titian locks which
have been pictured by artist Fox on several can-
vases. And, of course, in accordance with Fashion’s
decree, she tucked them away under a perfect
duck of a hat that sat down firmly on her head
and extended its wings over her ears. To' be
fashionable, the only features necessary at the
moment are a chin (dimpled for preference),
a Cupids bow of a mouth, and the least trace of a
tip-tilted nose. Ears are not to be seen at all,
and eyes are only just visible in the shadow. The
would-be fashionable with a prominent nose prays
hard every night that the fashion may' change
soon. . .
The trailing race-dresses of yesterday are now
all packed up for the ole-clo woman. The ex-
tremely skimpy and ankle-high skirts of to-day
are more serviceable, particularly to the motored
and those who go near the snorting oil-dragons.
The carriage paddocks at Flemington and
Caulfield these times have about one pair of horses
to a couple of hundred motors. The time must
now be ripe for the re-christening of the
enclosure. Henceforward let it be the garage
No trailing gown could last long in the vicinity of
the petrol chariots. It would pick up the oil
by the tankful. The short-skirted dame and
maid trip securely over the grease-specked grass
with several inches clear between their togs and
the destructive motor trail.
The people who see to the comfortable housing
of State Governors were worried when they dis"
covered that Stonington could not contain the
Fuller household. The difficulty was overcome
by securing an adjacent villa for the children and
some of the gentlemen helps.
Departing Excellency Carmichael continues to
eat the teal-on-toast of the farewelled. The Mel-
bourne Club dined him on Saturday night, after
Caulfield, and in return he gave the haw-Club
the most Scotchy-humorous stories he could dig
up from his collection. Men declare that over-the-
table yarns are as excellent for the digestion as
a fine liqueur. There should have been no indi-
gestion that night, for Carmick is a good spinner
His stories are lengthy, ’tis true, and also mur-
mured; but they mostly reach a fine smart
ending—something usually with a surprise hidden
m the drawl.
Since Walker, London,” the first Barrie play
produced years ago by Brough and Boucicault at
the Bijou, Melbourne playgoers have seen, among
others, “The Professor’s Love Story,” “The
Little Minister,” “The Admirable Crichton ”
“Quality Street,” and “ Peter Pan.” Not one of
these could be classed as a commercial success •
nevertheless each left a sweet taste in the mouth.’
When the University Dramatic Club selected
Alice-sit-by-the-Fire for its second venture, there
was consequent rejoicing in the land, and a
crowded audience filled the Turn Yerein Hall to
witness the club’s version of the quaintly-named
thing. Stage-manager Harry Hill had drilled the
amateurs for some weeks, and the result, all told,
was a success. There were omissions of detail in
the setting—a not unimportant feature in a
Barrie play but elaborate setting means ex-
pense, and the University players are moving
cautiously.
In “Alice-sit-by-the-Fire” the schoolgirl
daughter and little son of an Anglo-Indian
colonel and his spoilt young wife meet their
exiled parents after a lapse of vears. Before the
family group get to know one another, the school-
girl daughter and her chum, who have gained a
complete knowledge of life by five attendances
at a melodrama, surprise the thoughtless
mother flirting with a young officer. To the un-
suspecting officer they allot the part of the
designing villain, and, after hearing the couple
agree to meet at his rooms, the daughter, who has
no idea that it is a family excursion and that her
father is also going, decides she must save her
innocent mother by bearding the villain and
securing from him the usual incriminating letters,
dhe reaches the officer’s rooms ahead of the rest
of the party, with a dagger concealed in her dress,
and succeeds in thoroughly mystifying the unfor-
tunate soldier, who comes to the conclusion that
she must be mad. The mother, on arrival, fears
there is something between the pair, while
the Colonel cannot make up his mind whether his
wife or his daughter is involved with his host.
Things are further complicated by the daughter’s
friend projecting herself into the limelight with a
noble offer to sacrifice herself “ even to the altar’s
mouth ” to save her friend. The title of the
play is explained in the end when, all having
been straightened out, Alice renounces the frivoli-
ties of life, and determines for the future to sit by
the fire with her children.
The women in the cast dressed their parts
artistically. Mrs. Cornish achieved an excellent
effect with an all purple turn-out. Later she wore
a pretty evening gown of Mediterranean blue
(which really might as well be Sydney Harbor
blue). Unie Russell, as her daughter Amy,
threw an evening cloak of the same exquisite
color over a white dress and pink roses.
Professor Osborne, youthful-looking and pink-
cheeked, is president of the University Dramatic
Club. He looked in at the last act only ; the
early part of the evening found him lecturing at
the Masonic Hall on the connection between cli-
mate and disease. It is his favorite subject.
“Any disease can live in Australia,” he thun-
dered. Quarantine and cleanliness have kept the
country pure ; but at any time an Amerioan or an
African or a next-door Asiatic horror may take
permanent lodging here. We started with a
pretty clean bill, and have done more in the way
of prevention than perhaps any other nation.
“Let us keep-up and increase the quarantine and
prevention methods,’’ says he. Australia, he
fears, is beginning to slacken, and suffer from
swelled head, and put the immunity from
disease down to the climate; and he thunders
his warning accordingly.
By the way, the Professor speaks of our
“ virtue as tea drinkers.” Not, he adds, that the
tea matters much, but the water-boiling does;
and we probably wouldn’t boil the water unless it
was to add the tea.
A strong local party is working for Walter Mur-
doch, lecturer in I /glish Literature at Melbourne
University, in his candidature for the Uni’s vacant
professorship of Modern Literature. But even if
an importation gets the position, the choice will
probably fall on an Australian and a graduate of
Melbourne to boot. This is Sedgfield, one of the
few Victorian graduates possessing the'Litt. D.
.degree.. This degree of Doctor of Letters, which
Melbourne alone among Australian Universities
awards, is not confined to “ literature,” but covers
practically every branch of the Arts faculty.
Sedgfield is now lecturer in Manchester Uni-
versity.
The Alfred Hospital Bazaar is over, and.all the
raffles drawn without so much as a growl from
the Law that hunts Tatt.’s from island to island.
Excited crowds watched the drawings, and the
gambling spirit was uncorked with great enthu-
siasm. Everything, from a doll apd a baby’s robe
*to a set of furniture and a motor, has been
handed over in exchange for a winning ticket.
The dining-room furniture was raffled bn penny
tickets; but the careless owner of the winning
card has not been found yet. The motor was left
as a last and crowning morsel, and the rafflers got
£2OOO in shillings for it. George McLeod is the
lucky man who will skid round in the machine.
Elorrie Young was responsible for a charity
party at Her Majesty’s last week. The lounge,
to which it had been bidden, became so packed
that the flock was let into another yard—the
dress-circle was opened, and the musical pro-:
gramme was projected at it from J. C. Wil :
liamsoh’s box. “Merry Widow” followers I
made song for the multitude or spoke a
piece. Miss Hume Black, who wails soul-
fully on a ’cello, brought, forward a string band
called the Southern Gross Orchestra, which fully
justified its existence. Someone set up as a
magician in a Marsovian-decorated corner of the
lounge, and simply coined shillings. The show
was to help the Alfred. Hospital. Nan and Betty
O’Hara, with other experienced programme
sellers, bailed up the surging crowd as it
mounted the stairs, and added to the money heap.
Opposition theatricals came along for a how-de-
do and to buy posies. . The bits of butter from
Brittany were in their most fascinating frocks.
There were others, too. Mrs. Mayoress Davey
waved big feathers above a black garb, and wore
a diamond star. The decoration had been pre-
sented to her mother, Mrs. Westley, after that
lady had been thrice Mayoress of Haw-hawthorn.
The capacity for being a Mayoress seems to run
in this family. Amy Castles, in an adorable black
velvet dress, had a welcome on her own. Mrs.
Tallis had royal blue in her black and white top.
Hostess Florrie Young wore grey, like a com-
mandeered cloud, and decked her head with a hat
that would turn heads in a crowd—a grey satin
two-decker with plumes.
The threatened £5O fine for incorrect census
statements is said to be burying Mr. Knibbs in
written confessions. Happy is the woman whose
birth is registered in a distant land. I know of
one who allowed herself a discount of three years
or so. She isn’t worrying,' because she first saw
the light in France. The not-so-young maids who
know the terrible truth is written within a mile
or two of them are mostly in a state of panic, and
have begged for mercy by post. As the poet
thoughtfully remarks: “They youth adore, but
love their money more.”
One of the sad events of last Week was the
death of the 14-year-old son of the Justice
Cussens. The Judge left some weeks back with
part of the Coronation contingent, and had just
reached England when the sorrowful news went
through. The returning boat brings him back to
Australia.
The mysterious airship has appeared again, this
time over Melbourne Cricket Ground. But even
the stories that it has been seen, decorated with
colored lights, high in the evening sky, fails to
arouse the suburbanite. The flying machine is
already commonplace. In a year or so that sweet
old appeal, “ Fly with me!” will cease to have
any matrimonial significance, and the maiden
addressed will probably retort in a snappish
manner, “Not on that dirty old ’plane you had
last week. Get something that will travel with-
out making a noise like a tin band and I will go
for a spin with you ; but I’m not going out to be
laughed at by that Smith girl again if I can help
it.”
The very latest “mysterious airship’’ alarm
reminds me of the two bushmen who saw what
they thought was a flying machine sailing majes-
tically along, and at intervals bending its planes
gracefully. -They followed it for some distance,
and were amazed to see it disintegrating in mid-
air. . The supposed machine was a flock of star-
lings flying very close together, as is, their habit.
These feathered larrikins in flight form graceful
mathematical forms ; occasionally a “push ” will
form a complete circle, which looks to the eye as
solid as a cartwheel. The aero form rhight have
been a raw experiment in a new flying formation ;
but, if raw, it deceived the bushmen and other
people who observed it from more remote points.
Curious are the freaks of mankind. Every first
night, at a Florry Young show, the observant
might have noticed amongst the “ floral tributes,”
a curious little bunch of cheap flowers, tied
up with string and freely interspersed with fern-
just the old-fashioned, tight bunch of flowers
which you buy at the Eastern Market for Is. And
every last night the same person might have spied
an old clothes-basket full of any one particular
flower—violets, when they were in season, or a
luxury of exquisite roses. The little flower-play
happens regularly ; for 15 years the same person
has sent Miss Young the same kind of shilling
bouquet on her first night and the same clothes-
basketful on her last.
A new field is to be opened up to the earnest
feminine church worker. No longer need she hem
handkerchiefs for the heathen, or embitter her
friends by pursuing them with subscription lists.
Nurseries are to be provided in or about church
buildings where Gladys and Roy will be amused
with toys and comforted with boiled lollies whilst
Mum gets some points about the Day of Judg-
ment and other approaching functions. Someone
must look after the child ; so the maiden who leans
against the kirk may put by her work basket and
learn the useful art pf entertaining the youngs
For my own part I shall miss the infant who
lightens a long and unoriginal sermon by trying to
eat his mother’s handbag, or loudly informing his
father that he should have brushed his hat before
he came out.
Will somebody please invent a masculine
equivalent of “Airs.”? One pretty little* resi-
dent of southern parts has special reason to
bemoan the lack of a matrimonial tag, so far as
man is concerned. Some months ago all her
friends were simultaneously envying and con-
gratulating her on her engagement to a fine-
looking man with money and a motor-car. The
home was given up so that she might be near her
fiance, and all was going happy as an engage-
ment ring when one day there appeared in
northern papers the wife’s advertisement for her
missing husband. Still she counts herself lucky
in escaping the worse fate of Miss Caspers, and
finding herself no-wife instead of no-fiancee.
Artist Arthur Streeton has now the cares of a
father on his easel. The new arrival is a boy.
Amy Austin, whose people own land and jum-
bucks at Lara, has married Dr. AlcKay, who has
been “assistant” at the Geelong Hospital, but
now goes north to Cairns (Q.). The wedding,
which occurred at Christ Church, Geelong, was
very quiet, and the Voice didn’t rise above a
whisper.
Mrs. O’Donnell, who died at the alleged age of
100-and-something at Maffra (Vic.), the other day,
knew more than most people about Victoria’s
earliest moments. Away back in the colony’s
infancy, almost before it even had a cradle, her
husband was book-keeper in the employ of the
first-family Hentys, at Portland.
Airs. Marshj who belongs to the Reidy clan, is
very proud of her youthful son, who has been tem-
porarily holding the official reins in New Britain
in the intervals of looking for copra. He goes to
Berlin shortly ifor a combined business-and-plea-
sure trip ; and thither his mother is to follow him,
since they cannot holiday together in Australia.
The young man is a grandson of Governor Reidy,
of the convict days.

Maud Grantham’s recital of “Alice in Wonder-
land,” in aid of the surgical ward of the Children’s'
Hospital, deservedly scored a packed house at the
. Masonic Hall., Lovers of Lewis Carroll tramped
along in battalions. Aliss. Grantham, robed in
maize satin, made a graceful figure on the plat-
form, which was set as a drawing-room, and held
her sympathetic audience all the way. Liza Leh-
mann’s nonsense songs were sung by Violet
Somerset, Franklin Thomas, Radcliffe Hawley,
and Miss Grantham herself. The Mad Tea Party
made the house rock, and a small admirer after-
wards handed up a stuffed white rabbit and a fan.
I am still wondering, though, why the Cheshire
Cat was credited with a Noo Yark twang.
Violet Somerset’s rendering of “ Speak Roughly
Lo Your Little Child” and a sneezing quartet
were the best of the musical numbers. Amongst
the audience was a.man with a huge laugh and a
blessed sense of humor who should have had his
entrance money refunded, so valuable was he as a
master of merriment. When the children were
not absorbed in Alice’s adventures, they were
shrieking with joy at the big boy’s joyous guffaw.
A Coronator, writing from Cairo, says he ‘
stayed at what was once the palace of Ismail
Pasha. It appears that should an Egyptian
prince or other royal person so far forget himself
as to die in his palace, the edifice immediately
goes out of business as such and is devoted to
meaner uses. In this case it has been trans-
formed into a gorgeous hotel, and the marble
staircases, silver baths and (shades of the time-
payment man !) gold furniture, are given over to
the cursed but remunerative unbeliever. The
same Coronator passionately mentions his laun-
dry bill, the receipt of which nearly made him
rush out and throw moth-eaten camels at the
Sphinx. For the washing of one pair of rainbow
socks the unfortunate tripper was invited to
pay ss. Hooray for Allah ! The mad infidel
who dares to ask old dusty Egypt to attend to his
hosiery bites the dust of the insolvency court
before the mad charges of the dervish !
Amy Castles met an earthquake while she was
in Alaoriland. She was concerting at Napier, and
was in the middle of a crooning love song when
the North Island was so moved that it tried to
bring down the house. The accompanist fled the
platform, and there was a jumble amongst the
rushing audience. Amy, who is unacquainted |
with earth tremors, did not know what '
the trouble was, and stood amazed at the
sudden confusion. A few nights later another <
’quake shook the hotel, and the beds rocked
like cradles. The singer is Sadly looking forward to
her departure from the Commonwealth in a week or
two. The Castles family loves Australia, and Mrs.
Castles is for ever bewailing the world-wanderings
of her children, and longing to have them all under
one roof again. It is the penalty of rearing a nest
of songbirds. Brother George, with “The
“Merry Widow” and D. B. O’Connor, is doing
well in the country parts of N.S. W. ; Dolly is too
tied up with engagements in London to slip
away for a while ; and Eileen comes to warble
with the Melba Grand Opera Co.
The independence of the modern bachelor-girl
amazes and shocks the gra-ndmothers-of the race ;
but, of course, the world progresses only by amaz-
ing and shocking its, grandmother. Gwladys
Chirnside, soon after her mother’s second mar-
riage, set off on a world tour, lived for some time
in a London flat, and thfen'made'for her Southern
homeland. She is now cosily settled in a flat at
South Yarra, which is a much better way of
living than being one of a crowd in a boarding-
mansion. Another girl who prefers a place of
her own to the feverish existence of a paying
guestery is May Parker, the very young heiress of
dead shipping magnate Parker. She has a whole
South Yarra house to herself, commands a staff
of maids, and keeps a motor-car or two.
The St. Kilda Bachelors, to the number of about
20, are to give a dance in the immense local Town
Hall on June 2. Invitations are just fluttering
out, and the girls are now engaged plotting out
frocks. The occasion is too important for second-
best chiffons. The eligibles are expected to be
there in great numbers, and she who is not pre-
pared to dress to the nines had better stay at
home. The leading instigator of the function is
Joe Gunn, private secretary to the woolly Fair-
bairn Brothers. The invitation cards are a dream
| n themselves. They present a reflective young
bachelor seated in a lounge chair, with a bowl of
roses at his beautiful feet, blowing smoke-faces of
pretty girls.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Certain globe-trotters have accused us of owning one
of the “ most efficient tramway services in the world.” But
even these sayers of polite nothings have not cracked up
our railways to any extent. Adelaide’s average sub-
urban train possesses the speed capacity of a steam-roller
or thereabouts. The sight of the Glenelg train strolling
casually down our main thoroughfare (what time the
guard tolls a cow-bell to warn stray dogs off the line)
suggests the back ages of steam traction. A traveller
was puzzled recently by the steam hearse’s sudden halt
in mid-street. “ Hadn’t we better get along to the
station '?” he queried, eyeing his watch. He was told it
was the station, and his guide indicated the pigeon-hole
whence tickets are distributed. Even then he wasn’t
satisfied, and prattled of “going through to the plat-
form.” When he did grasp the true inwardness of the
situation his face was a study. “ Good Heaven ! Does the
darned thing run in the gutter ? ” Yet, when a change was
suggested Glenelgites waxed hysteric and wrote columns
to both dailies ; they dreaded the idea of a possible addi-
tional 20 yards’ walk to their offices.
Both town and country are gasping for rain and a
smack of seasonable cold. The earth is baked to bricks
everywhere. Nevertheless the 'chrysanthemum show
was a success. The blooms were huge, and wonderful as
to color. One of the best features of this village is its
love of flowers. In spring and autumn the smallest sub-
urban gardens are a blaze, and it is a very back-alley
cottage that doesn’t boast some show of vegetation.
On Saturday the supporters of the rival Beds and Blues
ranged themselves in solid massesjat the Oval, and yelled
themselves hoarse over contesting “Saints” and
The former came out on top with a good
margin to spare. The presence of Lady Bosanquet lent
the necessary “tone” to the proceedings, though, to do
him justice, the average schoolboy doesn’t give tuppence
for “tone,” and our Admiral takes an interest in the
youngsters’ performances because he really likes to.
The “ Gay Gordons ” who were hurt in a motor smash
have been released from the North Adelaide Hospital,
whole but still shaky. There are rumors of an aftermath
for the tramway contractor into whose excavation the
car dived.
Since Swanport rose to suddeti fame by discovering
itself as an ancient deposit for ithe bones of defunct
blacks, suburban Fulham has put in a similar plea for
notoriety. It may be taken for granted that anyone who
discovers an old beef bone in his back yard will, for the
next six months, send frantically for the authorities to
come and view his allotment of aboriginal cemetery.

much brighter. The dreadful pains and

matism now.

POLITICAL
POINTS
On Referendum Day, the rulers supplied
to the poll clerks at Broken Hill—and, pre-
sumably, to the poll clerks elsewhere —were
branded “Made in Japan.” And this at a
poll supposed to be conducted by a Govern-
ment which was pleading for the ascendency
of Australia! There are a lot of things
that Federal Ministers might speak earn-
estly to Kink O’Malley about Avith regard to
the conduct of that ballot.
SAVED!
THE RECUMCBENT BEAT : “ Tell you what,
Slouch, if the ‘ Yesses ’ had won 1D have
left the country!”
South Australia has had a Labor Premier
and a wholly or mostly-Labor Government
longer than any other State in the world,
so far as The Bulletin knows. And, in-
stead of the promised devastation and ruin,
it has within one week supplied these two
proofs, from opposite ends of the scale, of
abounding prosperity: (1) The Bank of
Adelaide pays 10 per cent., and increases
all its clerks’ salaries ; and the usually mat-
ter-of-fact George Downer, chairman of di-
rectors, quotes with approval the remark of
some person unnamed that “the reports of
the bank read like fairy-tales.” (2) An
old and hard-working charity, the Eastern
Suburban Clothing Guild, has formally de-
clared itself dead. It can’t find any poor
to befriend !
The busy man entirely during the Refer-
endum campaign wasn’t Hugh Mahon,
M H.R. for Coolgardie. On Eight Hours
Day Mahon was in Melbourne, not
in Coolgardie. But Avhere\ r er lie was,
the ex-Minister was as enthusiastic on
behalf of Labor as a lump of frozen beef,
and as \ociferous on the platform as a dumb
Avaiter. The grumps are no good whatever
to Australia; wherefore, Mahon must get a
move on —or out.
One of the features of the Referendum
excitement in Kooyong electorate (Vic.) was
the reappearance in public, after an interval
of years, of the one-horse shay which Knox,
ex-M.H.R., bought when he first struck car-
riage form. All the Nicest’s. motor-cars
were, of course, pressed into service, but, lest
the motor-cars wouldn’t go round, the old
family-waggon was sent out to scour ivoo-
yong —in the interests, no doubt, ot Mount
Lyell manures and the poor farmer.
The other day Beeby, Minister for Labor
in the Ma State Government, was asked by
a deputation from the employees in the mill-
ing trades to insert a clause In Ins new Ar-
bitration Bill to prohibit any employer re-
ducing the number of Ins employees in the
event of a rise in wages ordered by the Corn t
or Wages Board. Nothing was said by the
deputation about a clause to prevent em-
ployees leaving in the event of,, wages
being reduced, so Beeby pained the
deputation in the mildest way he could, and
told it to get hence.
No apologies have so far been made to
Andrew Fisher, or to the Commonwealth
Government, by those who cackled so in-
sultingly at the Prime Minister s absurdly
low” land-tax estimate of one million soys.
The Federal land-tax commissioner, who
has just spoken on the matter after receiv-
ing the returns, says that the tax for IJIU-
*II will bring a revenue of about a million,
and the newspapers which sneered at r isher
studiously refrain from making any remarks
which might be taken as a compliment to
Fisher’s judgment or a recognition or the
financial ability and judgment ot the Labor
party. Fair play has been frozen out of the
columns of the Tory press.
Just uoav, Victorian fruit-growers are
chinking hard that everything is not alto-
gether right with Private Enterprise. There
lias been a good apple and pear season, but
prices realised by the growers from the
Avholsale agents have to be subsidised to
buy even one drink all round. According to
the agents, first-class apples average only
about Is. Gd. per case and pears Is. per case ;
and from that various charges have to be
deducted and freight and the cases them-
selves paid for. On-the other hand, the
consumer pays about 10s. a case tor the
fruit, and he wants to know what the coun-
try is coming to. It’s time that he learned
that the country isn’t coming to anything—
anyway, not voluntarily. When lat cries,
“Yo-ho, no-no!” the consuming ass pushes it.

struggling to get a
footing, and deadly
afraid that a “Yes”
vote would rob him
of the chances of de-
veloping into a re-
spected Fat Man, got
a practical example of

of the Monopoly con-
trolling his power-sup-
ply called with a pair
of tweezers and a bill
for £2. In the event
of spot cash not being
produced, he was to
use the tweezers, cut
off the supply, and
stop the little man’s
machinery. The Mon-
opoly had received
some hundreds of
pounds for past tran-
sactions from the lit-
tle man, and he had
naturally expected a
demand for an over-

Only rarely does an R.C. priest do as the
Wowsers do; but Father Nolan, of Colac
(Vic.), lately rose to the sublime heights
where Willie Judkins roams. “The Federal
Government talks Socialism,” said Father
Nolan. “Give these gentry an inch, and
they will take an ell. It may be well to
consider if the aforesaid Socialism will not
end in nationalising the wives and daugh-
ters of this country.” Of course, and it may
be well to consider if the enfranchisement or
women won’t lead to the nationalisation of
the husbands and sons of this country, You
never know your luck. Anyhow, if the
fathers and sons of this country can t be
trusted to give the wives and daughters a
fair deal under one form of government,
why should, they be expected to be particu-
larly lenient and loving under another
form? Forms of government express a
people’s moral and social attitude —they
don’t dictate it.
The Argus sniffles: “Evidently the Act-
ing-Prime Minister has no respect for the
country’s emphatic declaration against con-
stitution-wrecking.” To Avliich Hughes’s
unprinted reply is, “Oh, blow your Noes!”
THE VICTORY OF THE BACK-YARD
PARTY.
Holman, Acting-Premier of N.S.W., is
asking the other Stales to “voluntarily sur-
render” certain powers to the Commonwealth,
What powers? Less powers than the Com-
monwealth asked for, and which Holman
helped to prevent the Commonwealth get-
ting? If so, Holman needn’t bother —less
powers are no good to the Commonwealth.
The same powers? If so, who authorised
Holman to “voluntarily surrender,” on be-
half of N.S.W., what N.S.W. itself deliber-
ately refused to “voluntarily surrender” only
a fortnight ago In fact, who authorised
Holman to “voluntarily surrender” any-
thing?
The ice-producers of Adelaide are howling
at the Government for selling ice at 20 per
cent, less than current Private Enterprise
rates, and throwing in some free, storage into
the bargain. The earnestness with which the
Government proceeded to show that it was
all a horrible lie is distressing. The purpose
of State enterprise in a case of this sort is
to provide hundreds or thousands of people
with a necessary at cost price. And if the
State can’t supply the goods at less than
Private Enterprise’s price, or of better qual-
ity, there is really no reason why the State
should go into that particular business at
all —not, anyhow, until it has dealt with a
good many others.
An Adelaide Tory daily made a mistake
the other day—that is, it looked like a mis-
take. In one column were black headings
full of triumph for the . defeat of the Re-
ferenda proposals, and right alongside were
the less conspicuous headings —“Italian fruit
ring; enormous profits in Sydney.’ I he
first column expressed delight that power to
deal with combines had been refused to the
Commonwealth Government; the second
complained that, •though apples in Sydney
had been forced up to as much as od. apiece
by a Dago combine, South Australian
growers could find no market there.
The Sydney “Liberal” papers have lately
been aghast at the idea of the Senate being
given a larger say in industrial legislation
—this Senate in which Tasmania and New
South Wales are equally represented! Hav-
ing managed to defeat that proposal, the
“Liberal” reformers may now be expected
to look closer home. Nobbs, the “Liberal”
Whip, for instance, sits for a district whose
voting strength last October was less than
one-lialf that of Northumberland, which is
represented by a Labor member. The Whip
of the “Liberals” may therefore now be re-
lied upon to round up his party in order to
wipe out the anomaly by which he has a
seat in the House at all.
Along with the misleading No No ballot-
paper. which the “Liberal” crowd advertised
all through the country press, it sent out
slabs of reading matter to be printed along
with the advt. Most of the hungry country
papers swallowed what independence they
have, and printed the screed. When it came
to the turn of the Richmond River Times
(N.S.W.), that sturdy little paper got it
half-wav down, and there it stuck. So the
whole discreditable business was given away
by the publication of this “note” at the foot
of the “reading matter” supplied with the
advt.: —
The above is not given as our view on the subject,
but is published in conjunction with a paid advt.
The Cow man is a strange, unreasoning
animal. The Alstonville (N.S.W.) Agricul-
tural Society on April 25 printed this in
its annual report:—
During the year the tick question was discussed,
and the committee decided to ask the Federal Gov-
ernment to take over the control of the question.
The recent appearance of tick in the district has
given increased prominence to the matter.
Having thus decided that State control was
a failure and a fraud, Alstonville next day
had a chance to increase the power of the
Commonwealth. But, instead of voting to
strengthen the hands of the Federal au-
thority, so that it might do such things as
Alstonville wants it to do, the Referendum
there showed: —-Yes, 100; Noes, 550. On
the same principle, no doubt, when an Al-
stonville cow-cocky wants an extra-big
month’s cheque, he sends an extra-small
quantity of cream to the factory.

According to a correspondent,
whose name ought to be good
enough, the N.S.W. Lands Depart-
ment has strange ways of helping
along closer settlement after the
Commonwealth progressive land-tax
has given it a start. The owners
of a Southern estate thought it
Avas time to get in out of the wet,
so they sold to a man who was in
the subdivision business. For the
purpose of selling the subdivisions
lie required copies of the Govern-
ment plans of two parishes:
and though he says _ he has
made frequent applications, he. is
still without the plans after nine
months’ waiting. In desperation, he has
at last written to the Minister, asking
whether a consignment of blasting powdei
would do any good.

SPORTINAG
NOTIONS
The Inter-State Eights race on the Parra-
matta was a walkover for N.S.W. crew. It led from
start to finish, and won by two and a-half lengths,
paddling easily. The real race of the day was for second
place, and a gruelling one it was, too. The first
start was a failure, but the second one was as near
perfect as could be wished for. After the dip West-
ralia got going first, with Victoria a shade later,
and then, barring- Queensland, they drew level for a
moment, Queensland a canvas behind. When N.S.W.
got its lead, South Aus. went in pursuit, with Tas-
mania inches behind, and Victoria inches behind that.
For a mile these three crews were practically in a
line, battling like Trojans, and it was a thousand
. pities so fine a race was marred by the boats getting
close together till the sweeps of Victoria and Tas-
mania clashed, and the Tasmanian stroke lost part
of his blade. Victoria made a gallant attempt to
pull out and avoid the clash, but failed; and then
South Aus. and Tasmania just managed to swing
clear of one another. At the two-mile point all
three were showing signs of wear and tear, but Vic-
toria was obviously wearing the best, although Tas-
mania kept making dangerous spurts. South Aus.
was in a bad way, and steadily dropping to the rear,
till it was a length behind Tasmania, which was
about the same behind Victoria. Then Tasmania
made a fine spurt, and South Aus. flashed after it;
but the positions remained unaltered, only the dis-
tances were reduced. With half a mile to go every
crew lifted itself for one supreme effort, and after
a splendid struggle Tasmania drew level with Vic-
toria, and South Aus. maintained its position. Tas-
mania went to the front; Victoria came at it again
and again, and in the final rush got quarter of a
length in front, and sailed over the line second,
with Tasmania third, South Aus. fourth, Westralia
fifth and struggling Queensland sixth.
* * * *
The Inter-State Single Sculls was a
mediocre race over 211 miles, in which C. McVilly
represented Tasmania, J. R. Towns N.S.W., and H.
E. Stevens Victoria. McVilly, who was defending
his title, bounded away to a beautiful start. Then
Towns left Stevens, even as McVilly had left Towns.
The weights were, McVilly 12st. 61b., Towns 13st.
41b., and Stevens list. 71b. McVilly’s work was
superb. Towns, though apparently bigger and
stronger, was too elephantine, and his work was not
in the same street. Stevens was never in it. For
that matter, neither was Towns, whom the Tas-
manian held with perfect ease, and at one time led
by eight lengths. The time was 18min. 50see.
* * * *
Dick Arnst is having a new boat made,
presumably for his match with Harry Pearce, who
is waiting on the front doorstep with £lOO deposit
for a match for £5OO a-side and the championship
of the world. Arnst is understood to be awaiting the
arrival of funds from Maoriland backers before he
opens the door. And while he keeps the door shut,
Barry, of England, is clamoring' at the back step for
a match on the Thames. Richard’s reply is: “All right;
£750, and Barry can have all the gate.” .When that
arrogant reply reaches the Cold Land it is almost
certain that English sportsmen will despatch a com-
missioner to see what size Arnst takes in hats, and
if possible secure his head for the British Museum.
A man who makes terms like those certainly deserves
a place in some museum.
* * * *
Saturday’s hoofball around Melbourne
was mostly job-lines, as the shopwalker would put it.
At Prince’s Park, where Carlton was to collide
with South, a strong northerly wind spoilt play.
“Skinner’s hirelings” have not mended their manners
since last season, and charging and slinging were
freely indulged in. The Blues, on the other hand,
played a civilised, though disconcerted, game, so by
half-time South had 37 points to Carlton’s 21. Some-
one then evidently drew the Blues’ attention to the
fact that they possessed a few weighty masses of
brawn, wherefore, on resuming, the 14-stoners col-
lided with Skinner’s hirelings, and for a while things
were willing. Then the two teams realised that
there was nothing in the rough-and-tumble business,
and began to play football. Bv better marking and
cleaner handling, Carlton steadily drew level, and a
hard, determined scuffle ended in a draw—lo goals
six behinds all. Geelong conducted a genteel dis-
cussion with Melbourne, which also ended in a draw
—seven goals 12 behinds each. Collingwood visited
’Roy, and fancying that anything was good enough
for that re-organised club, piffled till half-time.
Then it woke up to its risks, settled down to system-
atic play, and finished up with seven goals seven
behinds to four goals 10 behinds. The Students en-
countered the Saints, and the Saints once more came
second —69 points to 43. The Essehdon-Richmond
game showed that thus early in the season the
yellow-and-blacks have struck the internal trouble
that spells disaster. For 15 minutes Essendon waited
patiently on the field till Richmond appeared, and
when they did do so it was only to show they might
as well have stopped off. At half-time the tale was
—’Dons, 53 points; Richmond, four! In the spell
the committee no doubt spoke harsh words to the
“players,” and in the second half the locals put on
25 points to Essendon’s 23. But of course the game
was gone long before that. If Richmond’s com-
mittee can buy, borrow or steal an axe, it is hereby
advised to do so, and use it.
The most cheerful Ass. episode was the
uprising of Footscray, which gave the victorious
North a fright, and only missed victory by two
points—s 2 to North’s 54. The rising Ports totted
up 30 points to ’Don’s 49 ; Williamstown jumped on
Preston with 98 jumps to 34 ; and Brighton scored a
win with little glory about it, by raking in 30 points
to Northcote’s 28. Also, P’ran walloped Brunswick,
in a struggle notable for an almost overwhelming
finish. At lemon-time P’ran had 71 points to
’Wick’s 27, and its barrackers were delirious. But
the brickies shut their teeth and scored, five goals
seven behinds to P’ran’s two goals. Only faulty
shooting lost them a meritorious win—’Wick, -64;
P’ran, 83 points. A few minor casualties decorated
the disturbances; and, as there is an all-round ten-
dency to go in for heavy-weights, if umpires don’t
discourage the charging tactics someone will pre-
sently get hurt.
The ruling powers in the Vic. League are
determined to have open professionalism, and if
they can’t get the statutory three-fourths majority
to compass it, the latest idea is for the six stronger
clubs to drop Melbourne, ’Varsity, ’Roy and St.
Kilda, and take in North, Brunswick, P’ran and Es-
scndon (A.). Anyhow, if the League continues the
folly of nominally upholding the “amateur” rule
while most of the clubs openly break it, and of re-
fusing to foregather with the Ass. to bring about
district football, there is a probability of a large
explosion presently.
* * * *
’Bailer A. J. Curran, one of the Ass. dele-
gates from North Melb., bogged in vigorously to the
business of reforming sub rosa professionalism, and
was the chief cause of North’s declaration for open
professionalism. The Ass. said not a word to North
for having admittedly illegally-paid players, but
passed Curran out “for all time.” Curran has been
in the local police for 15 years, and has a splendid
record; but, apparently, somebody didn’t approve of
his “reformative” actions, so when the Ass. fired him
out, his superiors notified him that they thought a
spell in the Backblocks would be advantageous to
his health. He resigned.
* * * *
Saturday in Sydney was warm for foot-
ball, and the tongues of the players hung down
on the shoulders of the opposing side in the scrums.
In League circles the principal disturbance was at
Wentworth Park, where South Sydney triumphed
over Glebe, commonly known as the Dirty Reds.
Notwithstanding the bull-like rushes of the Glebe for-
wards (conspicuous among whom were the Burge
Brothers, erst of South), the machine-like passing of
the splendid backs of the Red and Green side wore
down the opposition and landed South a winner bv
11 to seven. “Billy” Cann (no relation to either “A
William Cann” or “Jimmie Pannikin”), captain of
the South, essayed to play the roving role of Mes-
senger in the first half, and made a hash of it. He
did better in the second spell, but did not altogether
atone for the fact that he had previously mulled one
try and presented Glebe with another. Eastern Sub-
urbs, taking things easy at the Sports Ground, al-
lowed North Sydney at half-time to hold seven to 10,
and then found it had to play with the sun in its
face. Little things like that get East Sydney going,
and it pranced to such an extent that it added 12
points while North put on two. Messenger’s boot
was very busy, and accounted for no less than 10 of
the Eastern points. The Angel of Death played on
the side of Newtown against the unfortunate Western
Suburbs, and the Western men were obliterated by
25 to two. The principal feature of the Balmairi-
Annandale upheaval at Birchgrove was the stoush
dealt out by the Balmainiac forward mob, who, evi-
dently fearing they would be unable to win by fair
means, adopted the foul. Right, however, once more
triumphed, and ’Dale won through with IS points to
eight.
* * * *
The other brand of football, known as
Union Rugby, dug itself out of the ditch and opened
its competitions. Owing to the fearsome stampede
of a few weeks back, when the prominent players
seemed to bolt en masse to the League, almost every
team has undergone reconstruction, and the barracker
hardly knew where he were. Newtown, premier last
year, let itself loose on Manly, and wiped it out by
22 to six. A little less disgraceful was the dishing
served up to Eastern Suburbs at Sydney Cricket
Ground by University. This crowd, evidently dis-
appointed at not being able to run baresark through
the Sydney streets in' the morning, ran riot and
kicked up 25 on the scoring board while East labori-
ously piled up a paltry dozen. About 12 people,
fleeing from the hospital ladies with the boxes, fled
to where they could see Glebe scramble home against
North Sydney with one poor point to spare. Broken-
backed South Sydney, one of' last year’s best teams,
got trampled on by Western Suburbs by 17 to 11,
and Balmain just managed to rub out St. George by
eight to five.
* * * *
Although badly scrunched in the matter
of grounds, the Australian Leaguers bobbed up ser-
enely with three first-grade fixtures on Moore Park
for the opening of their competition. The Training Col-
lege was taken in hand by Sydney, and after having
had its nose rubbed in the dust, departed in a broken
state, the figures being 114 to 16. Newtown scored
just twice as fast as North Sydney, 64 to 32. The
most even thing on the day was Eastern Suburbs’
defeat of Paddington by 32 to 28. The match on
the enclosed ground at Erskineville was that of
Y.M.C.A. versus South Sydney. The proceedings
were more decisive than Christian, South being
donkey-licked by 86 to 30.
* * * *
The Sydney Soccer crowd fell on Newtown
at Moore Park and registered six goals to two, which
at this game is ghastly. West Sydney made a draw
with The Navy at Lyne Bark with one all, and
Balmain and Canterbury arrived at the same result
with three each. The horrible scramble between
Newtown A and Glebe B, at Ivogarah, also ended in
a draw, each side registering two each. In fact, it
was drawing day in the Soccer sphere.
Footballers are beginning to wonder if an-
other Messenger is about to appear in the person of
W. H. Graham, of Newtown League Rugby team,
whom the League has, after much hard fishing, suc-
ceeded in luring from the Union ranks. Graham
played in his old position, in the three-quarter line,
and put up a frittering performance by kicking
five. goals and registering one try. Newtown: scored
25 points in that game, and Graham was responsible
for more than half of them. He is a powerful, well-
set animal whom 26 years of hard work and play
have toughened into a solid mass of bone and
muscle.
* * * *
The briefest record in existence was prob-
ably that put up by J. S. Gulline, of the Victorian
Motor Cycle Club, who, on Melbourne Eight-Hour
Day, covered the- 100 miles in 2hr. llmin. 30sec.
from the 15min. handicap. The record stood for
about four, minutes. Then the third , man in the
race (H. Jenkins) arrived, and fractured the already-
broken record by running through in 2hr. llmin.
20see., or lOsec. less than Gulline. The previous
best was the 2hr. 25min. 45sec. of L. Benn, winner
of last year’s race. Both Gulline and Jenkins rode
Dunlop-shod Triumphs, and had to maintain an
average speed of 50 miles per hour to make up for
the slowing down when negotiating three turning
points. The course was 25 miles long, and had to be
traversed twice. It was a great speed to maintain on
an ordinary Victorian highway, although the Sydney -
ro'ad, on which the run took place, was said to be in
“fair” order. “Fair order” on a Victorian road is
what a decently-roaded community would call darned
bad.
F. White, the cyclist who tackled Birtles’s
record from Fremantle to Sydney, had a. tough time
when traversing the sandy patch between Eucla and
Fowler’s Bay. The patch, said to be not more than
30, or at most 40, miles long, had stretched out to
60, and was so difficult that White had to pad the
hoof over most of it; and, one way and another, he
calculated that he walked 100 miles after leaving
Eucla. Water was scarce right along the track, and
more than once he got lost, which wasted more valu-
able time. In addition, he had a puncture, and a
fall which twisted his back wheel and compelled him
to heave-to the best part of a day. When he arrived
at Port Augusta he was footsore; nevertheless, he
proceeded to make a series of forced rides under
light conditions to make up for the lost time.' When
he reached Adelaide, though he was two days behind
time, he was in much better spirits.
W. H. GRAHAM,

There was wild hilarity at Warnngah
(Sydney) on Saturday, when N.S.W. Remonstrated
ye Ancient Game of 'Bowls to some merry men from
Ballarat (Vic.). Between whiskies and many good
yarns, the visitors were rubbed out by 31 points, or
117 to 86. The most 1 fearsome spectacle of the
flare-up was when Thorpe’s N.S.W. rink fell on that
of Trotman, with the sinful score of 40 to 14.
Redgrave, the ex-Sydneyite, did some
mighty hitting for his club, South Brisbane, against
Nundah in the final round of the Brisbane cricket
premiership. In his score of 141——he got the first
hundred of them in 40 minutes —there were 13
sixers.
Frank Tarrant, cricketer, of Melbourne
and Middlesex, conducted a slaughter in India lately.
Kashmir was playing Patiala, with the local Mahar-
ajah captaining each side, and all the players were
natives, -except that Patiala ran Tarrant into . the
team. He rattled the other side out for 98 and 124,
taking seven and six wickets himself, and hit up
261 off his own bat out of his side’s total 0f,449,
*** < *
Brisbane’s cricket premiership has been
won by. North Brisbane, which finished three points
ahead of Fortitude Valley. The batting of Jen-
. nings, the ex-South Australian, and the bowling of
McCaffrey and Barstow were the chief factors in
North’s success.
* * * *
Ever since the early part of April, the exe-
cutive. of the Cyclists’ Union has been struggling to
get to the bottom of sundry doings at the Swift
Club’s carnival at the Sydney Sports Ground; but
the gentlemen who were examined were too swift for
the executive, and last week that august body got
tired, and with a gesture of impatience ended, it.
The race over. wdiich the row took place was wiped
off, and declared no race. Officially the clock of time
was put back, and the thing that was isn’t, and,
moreover, never has been. The gentlemen who did
the bluffing overdid it, and fell in.
* * * *
If the times put up by champion Jack
Donaldson, at Deniliquin (N.S.W.), are correct
then John is a wonder, indeed. He romped in first
in the Sheffield Handicap of 130 yards in 11 3-ssec.,
and tore over the 220 yards in 20 4-ssec., starting
in both cases from the four yards mark. The times
may be correct, but like the Scottish lordling, The
average pace follower remarks, “I hae me doots!”
Some tote divvies at the South Australian

Handicap, £26 95.; Kalos, in the Hurdles, £l7 35.;
and Ipidi’s S.A. Stakes, £ll 2s.
“Snell”: Clarence Weber explains why
he insists that the boxer who meets him in the
mixed serim of his .own proposing, must wear a
jacket: “As in ordinary life a coat is worn, I stipu-
lated that a jacket should be used ; in self-defence a
coat would be of considerable assistance.” “Con-
siderable assistance” to the wrestler, Clarence means
of course. Might 1 suggest that it would also be
of considerable assistance to the other fellow if the
party in the coat kept his hands in the pockets?
In ordinary “all 4n” street scraps,' your opponent
does not wear gloves thickly padded with horsehair,
whereas in most cases he does tear off his coat be-
fore hurling his fore-hoof at the other fellow’s coun-
tenance. Young Mr. Weber is too ingenuous; his
artlessness in persisting in having his pug. trussed
is eloquent testimony to his great respect for boxing
as a means of self-defence.
* * * *
On Saturday night Tahmmy’s protegd,
Jack Lester, will meet Bill Lang, at Sydney Stadium.
Lester is, of course, an unknown quantity as far as
Australia is. concerned, but some of the best Yankee
judges think he has a future. Wrote 'Sam C. Austin,
a well-known authority, recently commenting on
Lester’s knock-out of Denever Ed. Martin in four
rounds: “Some time ago I saw Martin in training
and in the ring in several fights. lie was a
wonderfully clever fellow, as big and strong and
quick as Jack Johnson. Martin’s only fault was a
weak jaw. Two or three hard smashes made him go
groggy. For all that I consider Lester’s quick vic-
tory over Martin a sign of great promise. Martin,
by the way, was the big smoke who took all the fight
out of Dr. Roller, and made him come to the sudden
conclusion that he Was a wrestler and not a fighter.”
That remark about the man Lester knocked out being
as big, strong- and quick as Johnson, implies that in
Austin’s opinion Lester may be “the Hope.” How-
ever, we’ll see for ourselves on Saturday night.
Colored champion Mistah Johnson is a
busy man these days; or, if he is not busy, he is
Yankee enough to always appear so. Johnson’s colos-
sal task is to keep before tire public. The latest
break-out is the threat that, after wiping the floor
with some unknown calling himself O’Brien, and
collecting the £2060 offered for the delicate atten-
tion, he will start for the Coronation, where he has
booked “good seats.” Arthur will dispose of all his
motors, and whack £lO,OOO into a diamond tiara for
Madame Johnson to wear at the function. God save
the King! Also the Queen"!
The Frank Picato-Arthur Douglas agita-
tion at Sydney Stadium on Saturday night was not
a staggering affair, and Douglas, the South Africin,
was easily out-pointed after going the whole term.
Except during the first two and one subsequent
round, when the Boer pulled himself together and
fizzed somewhat, there was only one in it, and it was
Picato. He is anything but a pretty fighter; he
moves with elephantine motion, which on Saturday
contrasted hideously with the neat footwork of
Douglas in the opening rounds. He just bogged in,
and bogged in all the time —a sort of fighting which
does not at all seem to agree with Arthur Douglas.
Come to think of it, these fellows who just bog in
have a lot to answer for.

Soccer football is gaining ground rapidly
in Maoriland, to the detriment of the gate receipts
of Rugby, and the cause is likely to be boosted up
considerably by the visit of a Canadian team. The
M.L. Association has guaranteed the Kanuks 80 per
ecnt. of the gate money.
* * , * *
. Like the N.S7W. Rugby Union, that of
Maoriland had a rocky time on the financial road
last season, and ended up with a loss of £56 on the
year’s doings. But the Union is not in any diffi-
culty, as it has an accumulated fund of £11,312. It
hadn’t plunged its good hard coin on white elephants
of the Epping Ground sort. The Epping “bloomer”
is likely to be the death of the Sydney body.
That was a dandy seore with which Rand-
wiok (Sydney) Rifle Club wiped out Goulbum last
week. It was a 10 men a-side match, with seven
shots at 200, 500, and 600 yards. Out of a pos-
sible of 1050 the men from the horsey suburb notched
967, and left Goulbum lamenting, but consoled by
the fact that they had only gone down to a record
performance. Granville Technical College held the
previous best with 952.

Captain Livingstone, formerly commander
of the Yic. Government steamer Lady Loch, has
srone aloft, after being a gallant mariner for nearly
half a century. As a boy of 10 he joined the old
Government steamer Empire, and later worked
on H.M.Y.S. Pharos and sundry ships of the
Aberdeen line. When the Lady Loch was
launched he was made chief officer, and when his
skipper skipped to a bigger post he was appointed
to the vacancy with suitable ceremony. Living-
stone, who directed the Lady Loch from 1888 to
1903, once possessed an accent which he brought
with his humor from Ayrshire.
John Bull seems a bit afraid of his young-
sters these days. About the.middleof lastyearit was
announced that the Bakers’ and Allied Traders’
Society, which is holding a big exhibition in Lon-
don in September, this year, was offering prizes for
competition among flours milled from Australian
wheats. The usual correspondence passed on the
subject through G. Reid and other exalted per-
sonages. Then no more was heard of the project
until the other day, when it was discovered that,
through the influence of British millers, the whole
thing had been dropped.
