Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets.
A Safe, Sure and Reliable Remedy for the
Eradication of Worms in Children
and Adults.
Worms, by their movements, and the interrup-
tion they offer to the contents of the bowels,
generally occasion pain or uneasiness in the abdo-
men, sometimes spasmodic, sometimes gnawing or
biting, and not infrequently of a vague, indescrib-
able character, and yet very distressing. Sympa-
thetic with this is a sensation of itching at the
anus and at the nostrils, producing a disposition
to scratch the fundament and pick the nose,
which is characteristic of this affection. The
bowels are often disordered, sometimes consti-
pated or stopped, and sometimes relaxed with
occasional bloody discharges, as in dysentery. The
discharge is sometimes in shreds or flakes, which
are not infrequently mistaken for fragments of
partially-digested food. It is not uncommon for
portions of undigested food to pass with the dis-
charges. The appetite is exceedingly variable; in
some cases natural, in others deficient, craving
or depraved; one condition not infrequently
alternating with another. The abdomen is often
hard, swollen and tympanetie, the breath heavy
or foetid, and tongue furred, with disagreeable
taste, and a copious flow of saliva ; swelling of the
upper lip, bleeding of the nostrils, and disposition
to grind the teeth during sleep are other charac-
teristic symptoms. The effects, however, often
extend beyond the alimentary canal, and various
derangements of health are experienced in conse-
quence either of the direct irritation of the worms
or of the disordered digestion which they occa-
sion. Among the most common of these are
nervous affections, such as fretfulness, irritation
of temper, wakefulness, or disposition to exces-
sive sleep, disturbed sleep, sudden starting out of
sleep, as if from fright, dizziness, spasmodic
movements of the eyelids, dilated pupils, per-
verted vision, temporary blindness and deafness.
General convulsions are not infrequent in chil-
dren, and symptoms strongly resembling “Water
on the Brain ” have been ascribed to worms and
have ceased on their expulsion.
Cases of St. Vitus’ dance, melancholia and ner-
vous diseases, and even insanity have been attri-
buted, with the most apparent reason, to the
same cause. In addition to these affections may
be mentioned obstinate cough, spasm of the
glottis, palpitation, languid circulation, pale and
yellowish skin, sunken eyes, a livid circle about
the eyelids, and a general emaciation. This con-
dition is often accompanied by fever, and is then
called worm fever. Persons in apparently perfect
health may be afflicted with worms, and "the only
symptoms may be periods of irritableness or a
constant desire for something to eat, which may
last for months until the system breaks down, and
the worms manifest their presence in some other
manner.
Worms have frequently heen described by
medical men as “The Children’s Enemy.” Un-
doubtedly there is no greater enemy to health,
happiness and comfort of children than the
presence of worms in the stomach and intes-
tines; causing an indescribable irritation and
gnawing in the bowels, which upsets its victim,
impairs the health, spoils the temper, and drives
the mother to distraction, and earns for the little
innocent the harsh and undeserved reputation of
being “ a cross child.”
Unfortunately many mothers fail to recognb
the fact that their little ones are suffering intense
pain and distress, and are practically irresponsible
for the outbursts of temper, restlessness and
peevishness, and until some more serious symp-
toms appear nothing is done to alleviate the dis-
tress. The symptoms of this disease are some-
what variable, but generally they are so charac-
teristic that no mother need have any doubts.
The best proof of worms is worms, and if the
general conduct of the child is such as to give the
slightest suspicion, an examination of the stools
(evacuation from the bowels) will afford ample
proof.
A child who is affected with Thread Worms
will often appear to the eye to be in an indifferent
state of health. It becomes peevish and irritable
in its temper, and complains of itching of the nose,
whilst the same symptom may be very prominent
indeed in the lower part of the bowel. Another
point to which the attention of mothers should be
specially directed is that the child is apt to start
in its sleep, its repose being in this way of a dis-
turbed nature. Note also that many children who
are in the habit of wetting their beds do so by
reason of the irritation which these worms produce
in the body, the irritation extending to the bladder
and causing it involuntarily to part with its
contents.
In every heap of decaying matter there is al-
ways to be found a nest of worms. They breed
and multiply in impure matter. And so it is in
the human system. The accumulation of partially
digested food in the intestines, which becomes
putrid when the bowels are constipated, induces
the presence of worms. A few doses of Comstock’s
“ Dead Shot ” Worm Pellets, followed by a dose
or two of Dr. Morse’s Indian Hoot Pills, will dis-
turb them in their nest and drive them out of the
system.
Pin Worm, Thread Worm, or Seat Worm.
Is an Inhabitant of the Lower Bowel.
The Long Thread Worm measures from one
inch to two inches in length, the upper portion
being thin, and the lower two-thirds about twice
as thick. This variety also infests the large
intestines and the rectum.
The common Thread Worm derives its name
from its thread-like appearance. The average
length is about one-third of an inch, this descrip-
tion applying to the female worm. The male
worms are much smaller, and do not exhibit
half the length of the females. The favorite situ-
ation of the Thread Worm is the large bowel
which forms the terminal portion of the intestines.
These worms cause severe itching at the lower
passage and picking at the nose. Anaamia, rest-
lessness, irritability and loss of appetite are also
frequent symptoms. A child or adult so affected
can find no relief from the terrible itching, and
consequently becomes restless and upset at the
slightest provocation. A purgative medicine will
sometimes afford temporary relief by the expulsion
of some of the Worms congregated near the
rectum ; but the Worms multiply so quickly that
the trouble returns. To get rid of the Wormsand
their eggs, their nests also must be removed, and
that requires a specific remedy. Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets are unfailing in
their results.
Round, or Stomach Worms.
The Round Worm in its appearance, save in
color of course, somewhat resembles the earth-worm
in shape. As in the case of the Thread Worms,
the female worm very much exceeds the male
worm in size. Whilst the Thread Worm inhabits
the large intestine of children, the Round Worm
is found in the small bowel, or that portion of the
intestine which immediately comes off from the
stomach and makes up the succeeding 20ft. in
length of the digestive tube. Adults, of course,
are frequently also affected with Round Worms,
and it may be added that they are also subject
to be troubled with Thread Worms. The danger
of the Round Worm appears to be that in some
cases the parasite has the habit of making its way
through the walls of the intestine. Occasionally
it will pass into the stomach from the bowel itself,
ami it has also been known to cause liver troubles,
when one of these parasites has contrived to pass
from the bowel into the duct or tube which leads
the bile from the liver into the intestine.
If the worm penetrates from the bowel into the
general cavity of the belly, inflammation of a fatal
kind may be set up. The symptoms are very
much like those produced by the presence of the
Thread Worm. In addition, however, a good
deal of thirst represented in patients, along with
a disturbance of sleep, with a debased appetite,
the patient developing a liking for all sorts of
strange and unwonted foods. Itching of the
nose may also be here a prominent symptom.
All Traces of Worms Eradicated.
Mrs. 11. Campbell, Corbett Street, West End,
Brisbane, Queensland, writes: “My little girl,
aged five years, suffered from thread worms for
some months. I took her to the doctor, who pre-
scribed a syrup, but found it did not do much
good, as the symptoms still remained. I saw
Comstock’s ‘ Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets advertised,
and procured a packet, which proved very effec-
tual. They greatly relieved her and she immedi-
ately seemed to lose all trace of the trouble. The
dry cough and the restlessness at night and other
symptoms left her, and she is now in splendid
health. You may use this, if you wish, as a testi-
monial.”

feeling of sickness, during this time using all sorts

Shot’ Worm Pellets, and after taking the third

The Use of Purgatives.
Although the use of medicines which are simply
purgatives may cause the discharge of some email
worms, such medicines are useless to thoroughly
eradicate the pest. Physics which do not kill the
worms and their eggs have no permanent results,
as the worms pass through the matter in the
intestines and reach the upper portion of the
bowels or the stomach, or fasten themselves to
the membranes of the intestines until the effect of
the purgative is over. Worms require a specific
medicine, and there is no remedy so effective as
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets. They
kill the worms, destroy their eggs and dislodge
their nests.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets are a-
scientific preparation, prepared in the form of a
sweet that children eagerly take. They are
packed in glass tubes with full directions in each
and are for sale by chemists and stores generally at
2s. 9d. per packet, or six packets for 15s. ; and if
unable to obtain locally, will be mailed post free
on receipt of price. Prepared only by The W.
H. Comstock Co., Ltd., 23 Lang-street, Sydney,
N.S.W. ; and Farish-street, Wellington, N.Z.

describes the trip as follows:-
the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. Ihe
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.’
CAIRNS
is picturesquely situated, has a healthy
climate, and the temperature in winter
seldom falls below 50. First-class
hotel accommodation at Cairns and
Kuranda (Barron Falls) can be
obtained at a reasonable tariff, and the
fishing at Cairns is excellent. A trip
to the Barron Falls and gorgeous
scenery about that part of North
Queensland should not be missed.

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, N.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made bv the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season. ' .
Medlow Bath is an ideal'resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of* the picturesque. Arrangements can be-made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours.
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office—or your own residence—direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views jin the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds.
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Oaves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Castlereagh Street
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“0.K.”: Amidst all the talk about Government or
municipal action for providing the public with cheap
fresh fish, one hears nothing about the fish which is
smoked. “New Zealand cod” has risen 50 per cent,
in Melbourne —from 7d. to lid. per lb. —-within 20
vears; and, presumably, the men who catch the fish
are not all the richer for that increase of price.
It looks as though the ring of dealers kept, the
supply lower than the demand in order to force the
price upwards. . ,
“B. de O.”: Re the Barrier barber’s demand for.
preference to unionists. I discovered in an old book
of records the other day, that in June, 1799, several
barbers were prosecuted for exercising their trade
in the city of London without being members of the
Honorable Company of Barbers. ' They were fined-
various amounts. Preference was first granted to
these unionists in the sixteenth century by King
Harry the Eighth. The charter grant was the
ancient equivalent of registration under the Arbitra-
tion Act.,
* * * #
“Jimby”: Visited the Annual show at Mulga Flat,
and during my peregrinations came across a Brum,
jewellery joint. The jointer was trying to sell a
Brum, watch to Mulga Bill. Bill, being a bit sus-
picious, demanded a guarantee. The jointer, not ■a
bit dismayed, wrote out a guarantee to keep the
watch in repair for five years. The form completed,
jointer pushed the fountain pen in Bill’s hand and said
briskly, “Sign here!” Bill obediently did it, thus
guaranteeing the up-keep of the ticker himself. And
the jointer never moved an eyelid. . ' '*•>
“Ki au Mohio”: Re the Maoriland plan of using
prison labor in tree-planting (B. 30/3/’ll). If the
Vic. M.L.C. made the statements quoted in the par.
he was totally wrong. ; Take the largest plantations
—for instance, those in Rotorua districts. Prisoners
plant, each man, from 2000 to 2500 trees a day ; but
the planting does not last 150 days, as was calcu-
lated. Some two and a half months' in the winter
suffice to plant the output of the Rotorua Nursery,
usually 5,000,000 a year. He is also wrong in stat-
ing that pinus radiata (insignis ) is planted for
timber. A few thousands are raised and planted
for shelter belts, but the principal trees grown are
European larch, Austrian pine, Corsican pine, and
the best of the American pines, such as strobus
ponderosa (bull pine), etc. Although 2722 trees
are planted to the acre, only about 200 comprise
the final crop. This consequent on the severe
thinning necessary. It is expected that the trees
abovementioned, together with hardwoods, eucalypts,
etc., which are also planted in large numbers, will
mature in from 45 tb 50 years from date of plant-
ing; therefore the estimate of monetary value quoted
is quite erroneous.
“Marloo”: Wonderful what a gullible person the
North Coast (N.S.W.) cow cocky is. Apparently,
the only information in the monthly returns which
troubles him is the price per pound paid for butter.
The- directors and managers, being aware of this, use
the tickler on the right spot. Thus, during the
quarter ending December, the price realised for
butter ranged from ,9J)s. to 945., with an average of
925. 4d. per cwt. According to published reports,
the factories paid suppliers 9d. per pound for butter.
Add to this lid., cost of manufacture per pounds
10id. So we find that the factories paid 98s. and re-
ceived 925. 4d. for a cwt. of butter! Of course,
there are no such giant philanthropists floating
around these days; ahd this is the simple explana-
tion of the mystery. Cocky’s test is faked, and he
is credited with less butter than he actually supplies.
For instance, suppose Durrum supplies 75 pounds of
cream, and his true test is 4.2, This will produce
35 pounds of butter. At Bjd. a pound he Would re-
ceive 245. 9id. But Durrum is, we’ll say, credited
with a 4.0 test. The 75 pounds of cream will then
make 33 pounds of butter; and that is the quantity
he gets paid for. In this way Durrum, while he gets
the 245. 9Jd., to which he is entitled, gets it as
for 33 pounds, which works out at a shade over 9d.
per pound. And if Durrum doesn’t happen to be
bound to any special factory, the best faker gets his
custom.
* * * *
“The Prodigal”: No one is so frequently taken
down in the bush as the poor parson. Leaving the
few genuine cases aside, and taking only the
tricksters, I class them into four—alcoholic beggars,
religious beggars, smart beggars and will-makers.
One of the first lot recently came to me. He could
hardly keep his feet; and when I rebuked him he
challenged me to smell his breath. I hung my hat
on it instead. jAnother, when advised to give up
whisky if he wished to live a long life, replied:
“To-day, your Reverence, I had no whisky, and it has
been the longest day of my life.” I paid on that.
Another turned upon me and said: “You be not
above taking a drop yourself, your Reverence, and
what’s yours is good enough for me.” He collected
also. One day I reached a bush pub, with a group
outside. “Hello,” I said, as I came up, “waiting
for the shearing to start at C -n?” “No,” re-
plied the spokesman, “just waiting for your Rever-
ence to save us from fasting on a Thursday.” I
have scarcely met a genuine religious beggar in
Queensland. The worst rascals are in this group.
I have even been led at times to agree with the man
who wrote “Religion is the last refuge of the scoun-
drel.” Then there is the will-maker. In an assumed
burst of repentance he wills his alleged and distant
property to the Church. This paves the way for a
note to the proprietor of a pub, setting out that the
will-maker is a fit and proper person to be given
credit for a few days. Then one day the stranger
disappears, and the will is found to be bogus. One
man worked that Swindle in three adjoining towns
—and my town, I grieve to say, was one of them.
* * * *
“Broomite”: “G.R.F.,” who writes (B. 2/2/’ll)
about nor’-west pearling, is a back number. Things
have changed a great deal in the last decade. The
diver can nowadays command advances ranging from
£5O up to as high as £lBO and £2OO. There are
no doubt wasters and men anxious to go “try-div-
ing” to be had for from £2O to £BO ; but .a good diver
always obtains a big advance in hard cash. Further,
whatever they may have done ortce, the Asiatics do
not now mostly take out all their wages in “slops.”
They prefer the cash: I have known men who did
not spend £1 in three yesjrs on the boats. One man
I knew only bought a sixpenny stamp in three years.
He drew all his wages in' cash, and either spent it
ashore or took it away with him. As to the accom-
modation provided on board, it has to be remem-
bered that pearling luggers are vessels of no more
than about 12 to 13 tons. Vessels of this size are the
most suitable for “drifting” ; which is a great’ con-
sideration, for the diver at times tramps-many miles
on the sea bottom in search after shell. The accom-
modation on such vessels is equal to that on fishing
boats in any part of the world ; and men who under-
take the work know they must not expect saloon
mail-boat accommodation. Nevertheless, what they
get is equal, if not superior, to that available on
board many of the steam tubs engaged in the pas-
senger coastal traffic in the Eastern States. There
is nothing cheap about Asiatic labor; nevertheless,
my own view is that the Legislature would be touch-
ing on dangerous ground if it prohibited Asiatics or
colored labor in the pearling business; not that 1
love the colored labor, but I believe that, if it were
driven out of Australian vessels, foreigners would
use the same labor and work the industry close to
our shores, but beyond the Australian territorial
waters, where the greatest quantity of pearl-shell is
at present obtained by the Australian pearlers.
TROUSERETTES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hawker came to Hogan’s door,
And business had been bad;
A figure drear, in trousers queer,
Of flaring shepherd’s plaid
Caine out. The hawker looked it o’er;
In grave perplexity he swore,
And dropped the heavy pack he bore.
“Good-day, sir. Is the missus well?”
He ventured in his doubt.
The figure grim replied to him:
“I am the missus, lout!”
Then said the hawker: “Will you tell
Your man to answer to the bell?
It’s petticoats I have to sell!”
VICTORIA.

will be damp-proof and vermin-proof. The use of Damp Course insures dry,
healthv, sanitary cellars at insignificant cost.

BRISBANE, TOWNSVILLE, TOOWOOMBA and LONDON.
IN BANANA LAND
Digby Denham has campaigned at Rock-
hampton and Dalby during the State Parlia-
ment’s recess. The Tory majority at Dalby
has fallen from nearly a thousand to about
140, while Rockhampton has gone over defi-
nitely to the enemy. Denham is welcome to
be as proud of the results as he says he is.
From the St. George Beacon :
Time table for next week. Monday—Horse sale ;
Racing Club nominations at 8 p.m.; Dramatic Club
performance. Tuesday—First day show; annual
hospital ball. Wednesday—Second day of show;
Tennis Club social. Thursday—First day races;
committee meeting of Amateur Picnic Racing Club.
Friday—Second day races; annual general meeting
of Racing Club. Saturday—Trap shooting. These
are the main events requiring attention; there are
a few other minor events to be considered, one of
them being the Referendum poll.
God save Australia!
The whirligig of politics. During the late
Dalby election the local Leader quoted
Vowles’s election speeches in 1908, in which
that versatile candidate remarked about his
opponent, the late Speaker: “He really
thought that if Joshua Peter Bell came down
from Heaven for half an hour, he
would pole-axe his son!” Yet the same
Vowles professed during the recent elec-
tion to be anxious to follow in the late
son’s footsteps, and the funniest part of the
business was that the Dalby Herald pub-
lished a letter from Mrs. Bell (widow of the
late member) appealing to the electors to
support the candidate who, three years ago,
made that allusion to the pole-axe.
Apropos the humorous yarn that the No
majority the other day represented “the
intelligent vote of Australia,” this sentence
is worth quoting. It is from the appeal of
the Queensland Women’s Electoral League
to its “fellow women electors”
Even if j r ou fail to understand, make it a point'
of honor to go to the poll and vote “No.”
Some day, perhaps, women will get on
to juries, whereupon the hired advocate will
get up and say—“ Ladies, you probably don’t
understand the dispute between the plain-
tiff and the defendant. If you did under-
stand it, you might find for the plaintiff.
But that doesn’t matter —as you don’t un-
derstand it, you will of course make it a
point of honor to find for the defendant.” It
is a curious sort of honor; still, it is the
only sort that would fit the “Liberal” case.
“Quart Pot Charlie”: The Bulletin a
while back said that in the Hughenden dis-
trict 160 blacks have £1282 stowed up in the
bank.” Yes, it is stowed up with a ven-
geance : as far as I can find, the Binghis
are never allowed to draw out one penny. In
the Boulia district the blacks of both sexes
pay half their wages to the Department, and
some of them have from £4O to £5O to their
credit. When they want to “go walk-
about,” and request a withdrawal through
the manager, they are met by a stern refusal.
I have never known a blackfellow or gin to
regain all his or her wages after the Depart-
ment has closed in on them. The scandal,
indeed, is so odorous that some mana-
gers are now refusing to pay half the blacks’
wages to the local police, who are the col-
lectors, and are risking the consequences.
Authority doesn’t seem game to take the
matter to court.
The Port Douglas and Mossman Record
had its office knocked into a heap by a re-
cent cyclone, and it took five hard weeks
to sort the scattered type out from among
the twisted iron and the splintered timber,
and patch up other premises. When that
job had been done the boss sat down to
write a leader. He reckoned it was no
more use crying over pied type than spilt
milk; but, if anybody felt sympathetic “a
subscription in advance is the only proof we
ask.” It’s a long while since The Bulle-
tin saw it put better.
REFRESHING MEMORIES.
EXAMINER (severely): “Haven't seen you before?”
WITNESS (brightening up): “Often, y’r Worship. Em glad you remember our iolly
old times! ”
“Andax” : At an election not long ago a
polling-place was gazetted at a station run
by a Ministerial henchman. The Labor
party sent out as scrutineer a young man
employed on a neighboring squattage. Un-
der the protecting wing of the presiding offi-
cer the scrutineer was introduced to the
manager, who told him he would get no food
on the station, nor a bed to sleep in. The
young fellow got no food until the night of
the election, some 24 hours after he arrived,
and then it was smuggled to him by an em-
ployee, together with a blanket. The scrut-
ineer left breakfastless on the day after the
election, and when he arrived at the town
was told that he need not go back to his
station—the manager did not want labor agi-
tators there. This is the way some hangers-
on of the Fat party fight a political issue,
an fl then they wonder why men turn against
them every day.
Among the flotsam washed ashore from the
Yongala was a P.D. corset. Its story is im-
pathetic. It was not the relic of a young
torn from honeymoon joys by the rag-
]ng geaS- it was simply being conveyed to
a leader of fashion in Northern Queensland,
And its perfect state of preservation after
ma ny days’ buffeting by the salt seas is elo-
qUent testimony to the perfection of make
a nd ma terial which modern methods demand
even j n the most popular articles of fashion-
able a ttire.*
Crossed the bar, at Rockhampton (Q.),
M. S. Rundle, sea captain, aged 81. He was
a navigating lieutenant at 21, and was on
the Albion, one of the largest British war-
ships, when Athens was blockaded —which,
by the way, turns the history book a good
many pages back. He caught the Victorian
gold fever, but wasn’t a Eureka Stockader.
Afterwards, in ’53, he was appointed signal-
master at Sydney Heads, and was finally
sent north to survey the River Fitzroy in
’5B. And there he settled, serving as har-
bor master till he was pensioned in 1880.
“Quan” : Showers of fish and showers of
frogs are fairly common, but it was not until
the other day that I heard of a shower of
prawns. Buderim Mountain was the place
which had this new kind of drizzle.
“The Prodigal”: The rival merits of bul-
locks and draught horses are the subject of
much controversy. It is agreed that bul-
locks can be purchased for half the price of
a draught-horse team. But the pace of bul-
locks, in hot climates especially, is six miles
a day less than that of draught horses. And,
besides, on the return journey, with empty
waggons, the bullocks go no faster than when
loaded, whilst draught horses, with empty
waggons, can beat their loaded-waggon pace
by several miles. In warm climates, too,
bullocks cannot be driven in the heat of the
day, lest they should drop dead from over-
heating ; whilst the draught horse can keep
going at a moderate pace, with occasional
rests, even in the hottest day of Australian
summer. Whether a bullock can pull a
heavier load than a draught horse has not
been definitely settled. A test was made at
1 ine Hill, Central Queensland, years ago,
when two’ bullocks were pitted against two
draught horses to pull waggons loaded with
10 tons a-piece. The horses managed to pull
their waggon farther in one pull than the
bullocks ; but it was hardly a fair contest,
as the horses rerused to work under the
stockwhip, unlike the bullocks, and it was a
kangaroo dog that set them going, and was
thus indirectly responsible for their victory.
No other recorded tests of the bullock versus
the draught horse, tnat I know, have been
made under proper conditions, though no
doubt old teamsters have had enlightening
experiences of their own.
Queensland’s back-country “fighting par-
son” is now training for the amateur boxing
championships, to be held in Brisbane be-
fore the end of the year. Parson Sams is
under 10st., but he is a wiry type of muscu-
lar Christian, and his staying power is mar-
vellous. _ He is a hard doer, who walks 20
or 30 miles a day ; a hard hitter with his
tongue as well as his fist; and yet (or prob-
ably therefore) the sacerdotal favorite of all
creeds in way-out Bananaland.
His Neighbor’s Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ST. PETER oped his gate one wintry
morn
To let two mortals in;
And one was almost spotless as the snow
(The paths he’d come were trimly kept,
I trow),
And one was soiled with sin.
Yea, soiled with sin, and dark with many a
stain,
He raised a suppliant cry;
And Peter smiled and took him by the hand,
And beckoned him to where a seraph band
Sent floods harmonious by.
And noting in the other’s face surprise
That such a soul should catch the keeper’s
call,
Said Peter: “Friend, thy record’s pure and
clear,
But, if Ms roads were tMne, I deeply fear
Thou hadst not come at all!”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, Mat 6, 1911.
My Dear Titargomindah,-—-
It is unofficially announced that Lord
Dudley will pass through Brisbane about
the 13th of this month, to stalk the festive
buffalo in the Far North. No tidings have,
however, yet been received at the vice-regal
residence.
Miss Byrnes and Miss McPhillamy, of
your town of Bathurst, are the guests of the
Vision at Government House, and when
they depart Society in this Banana city
will be able to say with all truthfulness,
“I tea-ed thee every hour.”
While we’re on the subject of Government
House, let me tell you of the committee of
the forthcoming children’s pageant, and how
the Vision handed it something in the ice-
tongs the other day. They had approached
Miss Macgregor with the proposal that she
should be the queen-child of the pageant—a
grown woman, mind you, my dear —and
were working the gushful smile double shifts
when her Ex. closed the application with
two or three brief facts: (1) That Miss was
not a child; (2) that she was over 21; (3)
that she was too tall. Then the deputation
hopped down a rung and bore off the pretty
little second daughter of Premier Denham
to be measured for the crown.
Mayoress Diddams is doing her very best
to make the Naval and Military Coronation
Hospital Ball a huge success. A strong com-
mittee has been formed, and Mayoress Gil-
lespie, of South Brisbane, led a further con-
tingent of females to the last committee
meeting, to swell the combined forces of
North and South. The fact that Miss Mac-
gregor presides over a girls’ committee has
given a wonderful interest to a section of the
organisation where formerly not twopen’orth
of it existed. The naval and military heroes
will make gay the ballroom, and the valuable
“handy-man” will be much in request as a
decorator. That same “handy-man” will
not, however, be pleased to hear that one
kind lady suggested, at a recent committee
meeting, that he should also act as char-
woman, and cleanse the extremely grubby
floors of Finney’s old buildings, on which the
couples will gyrate.
The Scotch picnic, in the Lucinda on Sat-
urday, was a fearsome mixture of Bur-r-r-ns,
brogue and whisky. The usual toasts, punc-
tuated with speeches, were lowered in the
cabin, and Premier Denham, the one lone
Englishman, made his best apology for his
nationality. The Premier’s speech was en-
livened by an ancient countryman of Johnny
Walker, who, catching sight of a bottle
through the skylight, hastened downstairs to
shakh hands with it. As he reached but,
a committee-man, horrified at the breach of
etiquette, sought to restrain him. “But,
hoots, mon,” said the stranger, “Ah’m as
dry as a besket.” With that he poured him-
self a nobbier and drank to Caledonia.
The wedding epidemic has in no wise
abated. On Tuesday last Mr. Westralian
Haly annexed a good Queenslander in Miss
Doris de Winton. Mr. Haly’is a son of
one of the original large squatting families
of this State. His father was Mr. C. R.
Haly, of Taabinga, and a cousin is Mrs.
Oampbell-Praed.
Architect Lange Powell has returned home
with his attractive Victorian bride, and the
glad hand of congratulation is working over-
time.
A cable, received from London this week,
announces that dentist Andrew Menzies and
his pretty little wife have been touring the
South of England per motor. While hubby
flits to Edinburgh, Mrs. Menzies will be the
guest of General and Mrs. Burrows, whose
son (Captain Burrows) recently married
her bridesmaid, Miss Aileen O’Dwyer.
Mrs. T B. Steele, of Chelmer, gathered
in a number of men and maidens on Friday
for the first private dance of the season,
and if its success, taken in conjunction with
that of a dance held in the Cafd Majestic,
and engineered largely by Miss Bracker and
other “butterflies” of Clayfield, is any guide,
Society will whirl considerably this winter.
An extra spice of interest was added to a
wedding celebrated a while ago at the Syna-
gogue, by the wrestling abilities displayed by
several well-known women who were onlook-
ers at the function. The manner in which they
clawed and pushed to gain a sight of the
bride, showed them to be no unworthy
sisters of the renowned suffragettes who
“stiffen out” the English policeman and
pursue the perspiring statesman with loaded
brollies and a rock in a sock.
Our newest Gent. One is F. M. O’Sullivan,
son of Attorney-General Tom O’Sullivan, and
grandson of “Paddy” O’Sullivan, a member
of Bananaland’s first Parliament. He is at
present Associate to Justice Lukin.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.

THE DUMB MUTE’S CRIME.
Have mercy, Dan, have mercy,
Appealed the drunkard’s wretched Wife,
When he in his raving frenzy
Raised a chair to end her life ;
’Twas speedily wrested from his grasp
; By his son a silent mute,
Whom grappled like a tiger
With the grim and drunken Brute.
Aroused to desperation to
Take his mother’s part,
He fiercely plunged a carving-knife
Through the evil ruffian’s heart.
The victim toppled over dead
And the dumb lad in Despair
Realised his ghastly Deed
And piteously tore his hair.
Well, he saved Is. anyhow, even if he did have to
stand his trial for murder. . . .E.C. (S.A.) : Ghost

Ireland than in England, inasmuch as, by recent
legislation, every Irish tenant may reasonably look
forward to becoming a peasant proprietor. (2) Irish
landlords have been hard hit legislatively, since the
days when Arthur Balfour said that “Irish land-
lordism was the worst in Christendom.” To-day
the English landlord’s position is the more satisfac-
tory from his point of view. (3) In both England
and Ireland, between 1892 and 1902, the crop area
has decreased 50 per cent, as regards wheat and 17
per cent, as regards oats ; green crops in general and
forage crops have slightly increased, and pasturage
has increased 14 per cent George Dobson: Write

“ Lantana ” : The up-country mayor is
the gladdest thing that blooms. Know one who
rules in a decent-sized town in N. S. Wales that
could be truthfully addressed as follows :

tor of Starr Bowkett Building Society, etc.
Don’t know when he finds time to plumb or
gasfit !
“10-2-4” : I carved this, with a groan of
pain, from a Sydney evening paper last week -
The modern bachelor is a domesticated person. He is
tidy, methodical, orderly, with a passion for preciseness
that a pedagogue might envy. “Sew my own buttons
on?” said a young man about town the other day.
“Rather; and darn my own socks, too. It’s awfully
rippin’ fun. I simply love sewin’. It’s so soothin'—eh,
what?”
Do “ young men about town” employ this imbe-
cile jargon? Personally, I have not encountered
the outrage in real life. But it is becoming dis-
tressingly common in the sheets that our lady-
helps best love. The peril which menaces the
community is that Cyril, the aspiring young
suburban toothsmith, deluded into the view that
this is the form of speech in use among the
ultra-smart, may unwarily employ it in the
hearing of his large, bluff, downright progenitor,
Bill Smith, who rose from the status of a working
navvy to affluence and a J.P.-ship. A probable
result in that case will be the distribution of a
dead and mutilated dentist about the scenery.

A CIGAR is the BEST
SMOKE OF ALL!
But why can’t you get a
Good Cigar in the Country?
ALL over Australasia the only places you can
buy a really high-class Cigar is in the big
cities. In the country districts a good Cigar
cannot be had; because it’s only at the pubs that
you can buy Cigars at all in the out-back districts.
The publican knows that selling liquor pays him
better.- He reckons that his customers only call
for a Cigar when they-ve got a few drinks in and
want to dodge getting “fu’ the noo.” Any old
Cigar does for that.
So he buys a few hundred at a time from
some “back-yarder"—real “Flor de Cabbage"
with big gold pictures all over the box and
mighty pretty bands round them—made from the
waste leaf and the cheapest growths, but called
by some high-falutin’ name. (The making of these
Cigars is another story—l’ll tell you about it
another time.)
fdsg
Tb e s torekeeper or tobac-
conist in country towns does
not sell good Cigars either.
If he does, he has to add to
the selling price of the Cigar,
the maker’s profit, the profit
of the warehouseman he
buys from, the profit of the
traveller who calls and sells
them to him, and his own
profit.
So that a good Cigar
that sells in the city at 6d.
he’d have to charge 9d, for,
and not grow fat on it then,
because the number he would
sell would be small; and
good Cigars have to be kept
in a uniform temperature,
free from exposure to the
air, or else they’ll go off.
Those are just some of
the reasons why you can’t
get a good Cigar in the
country parts of Australia,
and also why so very few
Cigars are at present smoked
in the country.
Buy a 100-box of my
‘‘DIADEMAS,” smoke 5 or 1
6 of them, and, if-they’re
not as good as any 6d.
Cigar you’ve ever smoked,
you needn’t pay a penny.
That’s the way I'm in-
troducing my Cigars into
Australia. I know Cigar-
making from A to Z. I know
these DIADEMAS Cigars of
mine are just the coolest,
smoothest and most luxu-
rious smoke any man could wish for. Cigar-
smokers of a lifetime say so; and the more you
know about a good Cigar the better you’ll like
them.
My DIADEMAS are made from the very
finest, mild, rich, mellow, Java leaf, the filling or
inside heart of the Cigar being all straight long
Brazilian. The Cigars are hand made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before it
goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed to
burn free and draw easily. The Cigar is 4 inches
long, and the picture at the side is a copy of it,
exact in size and shape.
Now, this DIADEMAS Cigar is as good as
any 6d, Cigar, sold in the cities by high-class
tobacconists, as I sell it direct to the customer —
saving the cost of wholesalers’, salesmen's, and
retailers’ profit. I only charge you 22/6 the box
of 100 DIADEMAS, postage paid right to your
address.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade. Please add exchange to country and
interstate cheques.
My Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN

Legal Notice.
r*!o LANGDON MORCOM or other per-
JL sons claiming liis share in the Estate
of ELLEN SIBELLA BILLING, deceased.
Pursuant to an Order of the Chancerj
Division of the High Court of Justice dated
the 27th February, 1911, made in the Mat-
ter of the Estate of Ellen Sybella Billing de-
ceased in an Action wherein Harry Fitch
was Plaintiff and Caroline Gilbert (Widow)
Defendant (1911. B. 399). Whereby it
was Ordered that the following enquiry
should be made that is to say:—
“1. An Enquiry whether Langdon Mor-
com a nephew of the Testatrix is living or
dead and if dead when he died and if he pre-
deceased the Testatrix whether he left any
and what issue who survived her and if he
survived the Testatrix who by devise descent
or otherwise are or is entitled to the moiety
of the two one-fifth shares of the Testatrix’s
real estate which descended to him and who
is his legal personal representative” the said
LANGDON MORCOM or the person or
persons claiming by devise descent or other-
wise to be entitled to the moiety of the said
shares of the Testatrix’s real estate or to be
the legal personal representative of the said
LANGDON MORCOM or otherwise to be
interested under the said enquiry are by
their Solicitors on or before the Ist day of
July 1911 to come in and prove their claims
at the Chambers of Mr. Justice Warrington
and Mr. Justice Parker, Royal Courts of Jus-
lice, London, England (Room No. 252) or
in default thereof they will be peremptorily
excluded from the benefit of the said Order.
Tuesday, the 4th day of July 1911 at 12
o’clock at noon at the said Chambers is ap-
pointed for hearing and adjudicating upon
the said claims.
Dated this 20th day of March 1911.
R. T. WATKIN WILLIAMS,
Master of the Supreme Court.
NOTE. —The said Ellen Sibella Billing
died on the 28th September 1910. The said
Langdon Morcom left England in a Mer-
chant Ship in or about the year 1862 and
was seen in England again in the course of
the year 1867. He is believed to have left
his ship in Australia in 1868 or. and
was last heard of in the latter year.
BELL, BIIODRICK & GRAY,
Ormond House, 63 Queen Victoria
Street, London, England,
Agents for
BEWES & DICKINSON,
East Stonehouse, Devon, England.
Solicitors for the Plaintiff (the Executor).
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that ap-
plication is intended to be made to.the
Parliament of New South Wales during the
next ensuing Session thereof for leave to
bring in a Bill to continue the provisions of
the “Warwick Farm Railway Act,” where
by EDWIN ERNEST ALLAN OATLEY,
his heirs, executors, administrators and as-
signs were enabled and authorised to con-
struct and control a Railway from and con-
necting the line of Railway from Sydney to
Liverpool with the Warwick Farm Race-
course, commencing at a point between the
Liverpool and Cabramatta Railway Stations,
distant in a southerly direction about one
thousand three hundred yards from the Cab-
ramatta Station, and thence along the land
as now fenced and used for the purposes of
the said Railway from the Great Southern
Railway line to the Warwick Farm Race-
course, situate at Warwick Farm, and to
use, work and manage the said Railway,
and to carry passengers and goods thereon,
and to charge fares and make charges in re-
spect thereof, and to do all such acts and
things as might be necessary for construct-
ing, controlling, and working the said line
of Railway, and for purposes consequent
thereon and incidental thereto.
By the terms of the said “Warwick Farm
Railway Act” the provisions therein con-
tained authorising the construction and con-
trol of the said Railway in the manner there-
in provided were thereby limited to a term of
twenty-one years from the passing of the
said Act.
The aforesaid period of twenty-one years
has now expired, and an Act of the Legisla-
ture is necessary to authorise and enable the
said Edwin Ernest Allan Oatley, his heirs,
executors, administrators and assigns to
continue the construction and control of the
Railway aforesaid.
Dated at Sydney this 21st day of April,
A.D., 1911.
P. J. HOURIGAN,
Solicitor for the said Bill,
and the said
EDWIN ERNEST ALLAN OATLEY,
Temple-court,
146 King Street, Sydney.

The quickest way to ascertain this
WITHOUT EXPENSE, is to write
to me describing your invention, enclosing a small sketcli
where necessary. Nicety of drawing is not essential;
all that is necessary at first is to give me YOUR IDEA.

able, and, if so, give the necessary instructions for
further procedure.
Persons desirous of obtaining really reliable advice or
information regarding Patent matters are cordially in-
vited to consult me personally or by letter. In all cases
a careful consideration of their plans, an honest opinion,
and a prompt reply may be expected.

Frauds.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN the Prehistoric Man tried to
benefit his clan —
When he advocated iron in the place
of futile flints,
Varied persons sadly rose—
Speaking through their varied nose
They deplored such schemes with horror, giv-
ing signs and fearful hints
Of the dreadful things to be—
Thus they maundered, stodgily,
Till the Prehistoric Person rose and cursed
in bitter gall;
Till he seized his oaken club,
And departed for the scrub,
Where he forged an axe of iron—their re-
i turned and slew them all!
As he smote them one by one,
All the stolid tribe looked on —
“Bill is right!” they said in chorus, “Throw
\ away the ancient gauds.”
So the Prehistoric Man
Bossed henceforth his staggered clan —
And his people rose to honor with the Burst-
ing of the Frauds!
When Columbus wished to steer towards the
Western Hemisphere,
Rose a crowd of holy persons, who protested
bitterly: ‘
“It is certain, Christopher,”
Said the Church, “that you must err,
For there are no foreign countries to be
found across that Sea;
Yea, by candle, book and bell,
We have proved it —proved it well,
’Twere a sin against high Heaven to persist,
O foolish man.”
But Columbus sailed away,
In his ancient ocean-dray,
And he wrecked the fool predictions of that
holy caravan.
It* was most extremely rude
Of Columbus, who pursued—
Who persisted whilst they feasted with their
trulls and pious bawds.
But the Men;- it seems to me,
j Who do most for Destiny,
Are the Titans like Columbus —men who
burst the ancient Frauds,
i ,
For the Frauds must go at last—they are
passing, passing fast,
’Tis the World; against the Windbags, ’gainst
the futile, vaunted shams;
As the years go driving by,
• Dead delusions, piled on high,
Mark the progress of the species, and the
Door, of Credence slams!
i Slammetli loudly, slammeth hard —
And the door is triple-barred,
Shutting out the fool-delusions men were
slaves to long ago;
Pass the old exploded, myths
Forged by holy fetich-smiths —
Manufacturers of fetters and of sodden
creeds of woe !
Pass the liars, and their lies —
For the world with open eyes
Scans the work that lies before it—scans the
rubbish, and applauds;
Thus our patent Duty .here
Is to sweep the wreckage clear —
Let us labor late and early at the Shifting
i’ of the Frauds.
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
A Chinese Whale.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HIS hilt stood on the southern shore of
Twofold Bay, overlooking the whal-
ing station at Kiah. Dense mul-.
berry bushes and jungle-like creepers con;
cealed the eerie wind-racked shanties that
leaned over the hillside.- Grease and blub-
ber-smoke had evidently preserved the tim-
bers of the whalers’ homesteads against the
fierce Antarctic winds that sweep over the
southern limits of Australia.
Peter’s hut stooped ’ against a bullock-
proof fence which marked the eastern boun-
dary of a deserted cattle station. The skele-
ton of a whale-boat peeped from a roofless
house. A broken-hafted bomb-spear and
the scrap-ends of a disused oil-tank added to
the general litter, and suggested a once
thriving industry.
Peter was sitting near the hut door, his
bare feet and sea-chafed hands half covered
in the folds of a trawling net. Below, in
the surf-trenched hollow, an ancient yawl
pressed her weary shoulders against the hip
of a red sandbar. Mvriads of gulls and
inollie-hawks* clung to. she wave-worn reefs
bejond the trying-out sheds.
Peter’s voice was a shrill soprano at times,
but beneath it ran a dote- that could lift
itself when required above the music of the
humming, white squalls, that spin up** from
the southern Ice Limit.
“Fifty years ago”—his old sea-blown-eyes
puckered humorously—“we stowed our
rum under the oil tanks, and ran it to the
Victorian goldfields' whin the b’ys were
ready. ’Twas smart goin’ in those days, an’
the throqpers always enj’yed a plate av
prawns whin they looked in at the' whaling
shed.”
Peter’s skin was blue where the salt-water
sores had newly healed. There was a light
in his eyes nimble as pointed steel.
“Many ah enterprise has vanished at the
click of a trooper’s bit and chain,” I ven-
tured sorrowfully.
Peter rolled some tobacco in his palm
thoughtfully, while his ferocious black-
bowled pipe dived and scooped into if until
the weed hung beard-like over its charred
rim.
“I was thrapped wanst almost red-handed,
here in. this hut, betune five tubs av spirit
ah’ a Chinaman named Wong Hoy.
•“t’Twa's the night the Dunbar split up on
- the,jocks, an’ ’twas. black as the thrimmins
hyv a funeral party. God save us, ’twas
' horth Atlantic weather, Wit’ wind from the
pack ice. But a good night, me son, for
overlandi'nV rum. Five tubs., in the corner
beyant: Barney Cash and Ennis waitin’ for
the cart to lift it away.
“Cash an’ Ennis were good lads, foot,
han.d an’ eye. ‘D’ye hear the wind?’ says
Cash, huggin’ the fire. ‘ ’Tis like the feet
av mountains sthridin’ over the sea.’
“The rain tore through the shingles; the
tide was at the door soppin’ the flure where
we'vSpt. ; Ennis lay on the table listenin’,
..hie small eyes half shut, a revolver under the
cuff av his coat. The ears av a rat had
Ennis, an’ the eye av a thracker whin cir-
cumstances provoked his sinsibilities.
“‘Hush*!’ says he, kickin’ Cash Wit his
toe; ‘the ghosts are walkin’.’ .
“The door opened, an’ the wind licked us
like the wet tongue ,av a wolf. ‘God spare
us!’ says Cash. ‘Fwat is it?’ *'■'<, >:
“The face iv a Chinaman came through
the door: the rain was dhrippin’ from his
bald head; the eyes av him looked at the
three iv us.
“ ‘Come in,’ says Ennis, widout movin’.
‘Fwat brings ye on the bache to-night, John,
says he, 1
“The Chinaman looked scared av some-
thin’ : ’twas like as. though a thunder-clap
had shaken his bones. The wind roared
in upon us, an’ the lightnin’ jumped blue
acrossvhis owld eyes.
“He' was thravellin’ along the coast towns
Wit’ fancy goods an’ joolery. ‘You let me
Slay here lillie while?’ says he. ‘Welly bad
night outside.’
“ ‘Be seated, John. We wouldn’t turn a
dog adrift in such weather,’ says Cash.
“‘Nor a tlirooper either!’ An’ Ennis put
away his. gun wit’ a laugh.
• “We shook up the fire an’ brought out the
cards. -We played an’ waited for the cart
to take away the illicit spirit in the corner.
“The Chinaihfin lost an’ won at euchre.
The money stood in a small heap in the mid-
dle av the table. At midnight we heard a
thrimingus noise outside; ’twas like hell’s
horses dhraggin’ the. drowned ships from the
bed iv the sea. We took the hurricane-lamp
an’ ran out, an’ the. waves flew upon us in a
blindin’ smother av foam.
“ ‘Mother av Ilivven ! Look !’ says Cash,
swingin’ high the lamp. ‘Look and be-
hold !’ says he.
“We stood by watchin’ an’ listenin’ to. the
blowin’ av It, as the walls av sUrf battliered
an’ slammed it ashore. Mud an’ flyin’ shells
cut the., air in front of us. ’Twas like a
giant tlirashin’ the beach wit’ a bullock’s
hide. It rowled an’ fizzed an’ slashed the
sea in halves. An’ through the roar av
wind an’ water we cud hear the breathin’ av
It. : -n ■ .
“The, killers had dhriven it ashore—a half-
murdered 'bull whale swimmin’ in its own
blood an’ grease.
“ ‘Are ye game to hit it under the fluke
wit’ a harpoon?’ says Barney Cash to us.
“ ‘I am not,’ says Ennis, dhrawin’ back.
‘Time enough to sthrike whin the sea goes
down an’ the sun is up.’
“Tlie big wounded bull whale lay there
while the surf thrashed it into a blind'
spoutin’ heap. Goin’ back to the hut we
heard the rattle av the cart over the hill
yondfer. Jim Cash, whip in hand, came in,
his face black as thunder whin he saw the
Chinaman sittin’ nver the fire.
“ ‘Ye yellow scut,’ says he. ‘I saw ye
connivin’ wit’ a tlirooper at Bega awhile
ago. Ye damned spy, get out av this!’
“The five tubs av spirit stood in the cor-
ner ay the hut. An’ Wong Hoy made a
bad defence whin we pressed home the charge
of spyin’. The divil got hold av him as
we threw him from the hut. He struck Jim
Cush wit’ his shut hand, an’ the two brothers
knocked him to the ground. ’Tis strange
how a small clip under the ear will make a
Chinaman lie still. . ....
“ ‘He’ll pull round prisintly,’ says Ennis,
pourin’ whisky into Wong Hoy’s open
mouth. Barney an’ Jim Cash sat by the
fire howldin’ their chins an’ suckin’ their
lips.
“ ‘He will wake up,’ says Jim, lookin’ over
his shoulder at the big stiff hody* on the
flure.
“ ‘At the Judgment trumpet,’ whispered
Barney. ‘I sthruck too hard,’ he says,
stoopin’ over the fire. ‘Too hard an’ too
quick.’
“Where will ye bury him?’ asks Ennis
widout looking up. ‘lf he was in the pay
iv the police they will follow him here.’
“‘The. black thrackers will run straight
to his grave whether ye bury him'on a hill
or in the sea!’ cried Jim Cash. ‘A man
may cover his sins, but ’tis hard to hide the
dead:’ -
“A dead Chinaman is poor company on a
wet, howlin’ night. An’ the fear av the
black police set our minds goin’. The five
tubs of illicit spirit an’ the dead Chinaman
was enough to hang us all. Barney Cash
was first to see an end av the throuble. He
stood up wit’ the firelight in his eyes, his
dhry lips crackin’ wit’ fear and divilry.
“‘D’ye not see how ’tis to be worked?
Come wit’ me, b’ys, while I conduct yez
to the grandest funeral iv the century.’ He
took Wong Hoy in his arrums an’ walked
into the blindin’ wind an’ rain.
“ ‘Come to the funeral,’ says he, looking
back at us. ‘An’ for ever howld your
tongues.’
“’Twas no business av mine to follow those
men. Ennis took a spade an’ ran after
Cash. There was no need to ask what was
done. Anyone could guess.
“They found the big bull whale gaspin’
in torment on the beach. Ennis levered
the jaws open wit’ the spade, while Barney
Cash rammed the dead Chinaman into the
gullet.
““Twill be the Big Sea mystery av the
thing is found out,’ says Cash, slappin’ the
whale on the head wit’ the spade. ‘The tide
will cover our thracks, an’ the police will
kape a mile from the whale whin the sun
comes out.’, ,
“They came back to the hut an’ dhrank to
the health av the whale an’ the Chinaman,
while the cart waited by the door an’ the
five tubs av spirit stood like five publicans
in the corner beyant.
“‘lf I’m thrapped carryin’ the stuff over
the hills,’ says Jim Cash, ‘they’ll hold me
in gaol till Wong Hoy is found.’
“ ‘Then,’ says Ennis, ‘let us hide the five
tubs in the belly iv the whale until mortifi-
cation sets in an’ dhrives the constabulary
out iv.. the district.’
“Barney Cash seconded the motion, an’
we dhranlk to the health av all good strong
whales that kape away poor wake policemen.
“We took the tubs to the beach one by
one, and cut a tunnel under the big fluke
wit’ an axe an’ blubber spade, an’ rowled
tbd illicit spirit fair into the belly av the
brute. Barney was a bricklayer by thrade;
he tuck-pointed the corners iv the whale,
an’ but for the dead Chinaman stretched
in the dark gullet, he ,wud have v planted
threes an’ a verandah to Cover up the
openin’.
“We slept through the next tide an’ the
next, until the hammerin’ av the throopers

“ ‘Good mornin’, Cash,’ says Sergeant
Cunningham, easily. ‘Have ye seen an owld
Chinaman about here lately?’
“ "Twould puzzle me to see anythin’
smaller than a camel this mornin’, sergeant,’
says Barney, rubbing his eyes. ‘Have ye
seen the whale, sergeant?’
“ ‘I have not,’ says the throoper, holdiu’
up his head. ‘I have learnt sobriety in
me time, and I have learnt,’ says he, ‘how
to begin the day wit’out seein’ whales.’
“An’ away he rode wit’out spakin’ an-
other word.
“Ennis said no word, but stared open-
mouthed across the beach. The high tide
had swept it clean as a well-broomed flure.
The whale was gone, dhrawn out by the
big undertow.
“Two hundred pounds’ worth av oil, an’
five tubs av spirit,’ says Ennis bitterly.
‘ ’Twas worse than dhrownin’ a goldmine.’
“ "Twas a chape funeral, anyway,’ says
Barney Cash.
“No more was said, although ’twas
mighty harrud to think that so much good
liquor should go asthray.
“A month afterwards I met O’Donnel, the
whaler, in the pub at McCrossan’s. From
his top boots to his greasy guernsey he was
covered wit’ flies —big brown whale flies
that followed him from the beach.
“‘Did ye hear av the catch last week?’
says he, ‘near Cape Dromedary?’
“ ‘I did not,’ says I.
“ "Twas the best thing iv the season,’
says he, drawin’ a blubber fly out av his
beer. ‘A dead bull wliale dhriftin’ north
wit’ a crowd iv sharks bitin’ their way
clane troo the middle. We gave thim har-
poons for lunch, an’ cleared thim off. We
took away eighteen barrels av pure oil an’
fiye tubs iv whisky !’ y
“ ‘A quare kind iv whale, O’Donnel,’ says
I. ‘How dy’e account for the whisky?’
“ "Twas a Chinese whale,’ says he, flickin’
another fly from the beer. ‘An’ ye’ll say no
word to the boys whin I tell ye that it was
thravellin’ wit’ a long pigtail sthreamin’
from the blow-hole!’ ”
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
The Wrecker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
REAT place, the Murray, for a man
I T wot’s ’ad ’is fill o’ the towns —like-
wise fer a man the towns ’as got
no use fer. Plenty o’ fish, rabbits, game—
wot more could a feller want?
“I lived on the river fer years, 'tween
Swan Hill and Nyah—Tyntynder they useter
call it. Tent on a bit of a knob right on
the Victorian bank, which was a island
when the floods was up. And they was
floods there, too —places about there you
couldn’t see land, an’ the lay of the stream
was mud-dark to all but the old river ’ands.
Work? Oh, now an’ agen —on a station
out at Stony Crick on the Noo South side —
just to satisfy me conshunse with a excuse fer
livin’, an’ to get a bit o’ tea and flour, which
don’t grow wild on the Murray, though a lot
o’ things does, or grows tame in the home-
stead gardens.
“I was great pals with the crew of the
Sydney, tradin’ ’tween Swannill an’ Mil-
dura. ’E useter take notes, as I’d tie to a
bit o’ wood, an’ chuck to ’im as she passed,
an’ sometimes ’e’d chuck me a answer, or
maybe a parcel or a paper. Me camp was
on a steep bank, as I tole yer, an’ jist where
the channel come acrost the river, so the
boat c’d come right in close. Letters? Me
people was at Mildura, an’ I’d done time —
never mind wot for- —an’ I didn’t - want to
bother ’em ’cept by writin’, which didn’t
seem to ’urt their feejin’s as much as a
sight o’ me.
“Any’ow, one trip a noo skipper gets
command o’ that boat, an’ when she
comes puffin’ up I sees me pal in
the steerin’ box showin’ the boss the
road. They calls it the road, anyway.
It seems the old capting, who useter steer in
close fer my special benefit, ’ad kicked the
bucket or somethin’, an’ this bloke ’ad got
’is job. Well, me pal, the deck ’and, brings
the tub in close, as per usual, an’ chucks
me me mail. I chucks a note on a stick
back to ’im, ’e ’avin’ to let the wheel go fer
. a minute to ’tend to me. Then that skipper
surges a million. ’E rouses a treat on me
pal fer comin close (‘dang’rous navigation,’
’e calls it), snorts on me standin’ on the
bank an’ replyin’ similar, collars me note
an’ tears it up, an’ chucks the bits in the
water.
“Well, ’e was still talkin’ to me an’ ’im-
self when ’c spots my dawg yappin’ along
the bank. ‘l’ll settle yer, yer dog’s dog!’ ’e
roars, an’ pumps a charge o’ duck shot inter
the pore brute. Course I talked to that
skipper fer keeps then, an’ found I ’adn’t
forgotten me early trainin’ in cursin’. But
’e on’y put ’is tub up stream and puffed away
larfin’. I buried me on’y mate where the
floods wouldn’t wash ’im out, an’’ sat down
with me ’ead in me ’ands, to think out
gettin’ even with that capting.
“An’ it didn’t take me long to work out
’ow. It was full flood then, an’ even the
knowin’ old skipper (the decent one, I
mean) had tole me w’at a ’elp the light o’
me camp fire useter be to ’im at night,
pickin’ up the track through the big lake
the swamps made about there. I let the
ole Sydney pass on her trip down, an’ see
me camp in the usual place, in case me
friend the dog shooter ’ad sense enough to
smell a plant. But the day before she was
due back fer Swannill I shifted me camp to
another island a few ’undred yards back
inter the swamp, lit me fire at dark, an’
chewed over the yarns I’d read about crook
beacon lights an’ uncharted rocks.
“There was a billybung ’tween me and me
old camp, an’ now it was near as deep as
the river, but it was full o’ snags—any num-
ber of ’em —beauties, stumps big as a hut,
an’ all heights, an’ dead gums, all shapes,
an’ layin’ all roads. I was ’fraid the crew
might be at the wheel, an’ might keep the
track if the clouds went, but I knew ’e’d ’ave
to work at Mildura an’ Euston, an’ again
at Swannill, an’ ’ud want some sleep on the
trip. So I chanced that.
“Anyway, the ole push-an’-pull comes
gruntin’ along some time after midnight, an’
I c’d see —in course I was watchin’ fer ’er—
be the steerin’ box light that the noo skip,
was at the wheel. r Now,’ I prays, ‘keep
yer course by my fire, as the ole capting
useter.’ An’, sure enough, ’e turns in with
my blaze on ’is close right—must a been
tole to steer by it, as I ’oped. Anyone but
a noo chump would a tumbled they was off
the track by the quicker way on the boat out
o’ the main channel; but ’e didn’t. E come
snortin’ along the billybung three-quarter
speed a’ead.
“She noses along shinin’, till —wallop !
she gets into the snag nest, an’ leaves arf o’
one paddle on a lovely stump. The healthi-
est end of a log got the balhnce of the same
paddle and settled it, but those boats. is
geared to work the paddles separate for
sharp turns, an’ the other one didn’t find a
snag. But they likewise wear their steerin’
fixin’s on the side, an’ a pokin’ limb kept
’arf o’ that a bit further on.
“Wot with seein’ that dog potter takin’
’is boat into that fam’ly o’ snags, thinkin’
wot the bosses at Swannill ’ud say to ’im
fer smashing up one o’ their boats on ’is second
trip, an’ larfin’ at the general circus, I was
fair doubled up. Then a low-down tree
spoilt the shape of ’er wheel ’ouse, frightened
ole Harry out o’ the capting, an’ bent ’er
funnel doo south, an’ I was clean ’elpless. I
larfed meself ’oarse watchin’ the tub’s
capers.
“She was goin’ strong. The crew was
used ter bumps, an’ was on’y just wakin’ up
ter the fact that they were gettin’ more than
a fair whack; an’ the genius in charge didn’t
’ave the savvy to shut off steam —or p’r’aps
he was jammed in wot was left o’ the wheel
’ouse. But when she bumped an’ scraped
clear o’ the timber an’ banks, an’ got into
straight water, I got serious quick. I saw
that ’er curvin’ course, with one • paddle
an’ the steerin’ chains euchred, meant a
bump for wot stopped ’er. All of a jump I
see’d my bank was goin’ to stop ’er, an’ I
didn’t care fer to .share those few yards o’
dry land with a rampin’ Murray steamer.
Sure enough, she come in a sweepin’ curve
bang at my island, an’ from the tree I’d
shinned up, without waitin’ to see if the
curve was graceful, I watched ’er charge
that bit o’ ground full belt, grindin’ my punt
out o’ sight, an’ pilin’ up with ’er battered
nose on wot useter be my tent.
“Me pal was jerked wide awake be this
time, an’ I ’ails ’im. ’E cuts the Sydney’s
small boat at the back loose, an’ sneaks
over to me tree. ‘Get!’ ’e says, fierce like.
‘Boss’s gone mad, an’ frightened me with the
gun into blamin’ you. He’s round t’other
side the boat now, with both barrels loaded,
lookin ’fer you. Get out with the boat —I’ll
tell the old chump I got wet tryin’ to get
her back.’
“Did I get? Well, I’m here now.”
HILARION.
Ringy Ringy Rosy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
<< 7DINGY ringy rosy," the morning skies
-* »- are blue,
And yonder by the slip-rails your sweetheart
waits for you.
The lizards on the fences are basking in the
sun,
The Wintertime is over, the Summertime’s
begun. :
“Ringy ringy rosy," round and round they go,
The glad and happy children of thirty years
ago.
Those cool and frosty mornings, oh, I remem-
ber well
The wattles by the wayside, the warnings of
the bell,
The manna ’neath the gumtrees that show-
ered overnight
From Nature’s secret sources*, to make a
child’s delight;
The yams for which we rooted, the quinces
hard and tart
We swapped for tops and marbles, a fallen
log our mart!
u ßingy ringy rosy” —across the years I
hear
The voices from the schoolyard uprising
strong and clear;
And all the proud achievement, the failure
and the strife,
That make the sordid total of this our
mortal life ;
The pomp and human splendor, the Future
and the Past, j '
When weighed within the balance seem! little
things at last.
To be a World’s Immortal, to be a world’s
unknown,
Win monument of marble, or simple slab
of. stone — ;
How little matters either, when every foe
and friend
Must come to lie together in quiet 4t the
end!
W hen pays each generation, of high or
humble birth,
Its tribute unto Caesar, its dust untio the
Earth!
“ Ringy ringy rosy ” —beyond the lanflfs and
seas
We carry our delusions, we bear our mem-
ories.
The world is full of pleasure, the world is
full of pain—
Alas, the golden morning that never comes
again !
“Ringy ringy rosy,” round and round they
go,
The blithe and merry children of thirty
years ago.
“Ringy ringy rosy,” a requiem it seems,
In saddened notes recalling my boyhood
hopes and dreams.
The silver song of Childhood! Who would
not give to be
A child again, the harvest of all thb land
and sea?
“Ringy ringy rosy!” I hear the sweet refrain
Re-echoed in the morning across the years
again.
“Ringy ringy rosy,” the fields are wet with
dew,
And yonder by the slip-rails your sweet-
heart waits for you;
The briar by the wayside is turning to the
sun,
The Wintertime is over, the Summertime’s
begun.
“Ringy ringy rosy,” round and round we’ll
sing,
Our simple songs of Childhood, the Children
of the Spring.
VICTOEIA.
E. J. BRADY.
